401 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Averna v. Palm Springs


L. A. No. 24796


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 2d 38; 331 P.2d 4; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 206



October 24, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County. Hilton H. McCabe, Judge.

Proceeding in quo warranto to test validity of annexation of uninhabited territory to a city.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant entered after sustaining demurrer to amended complaint without leave to amend, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner appealed an order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which granted judgment in favor of defendant city in the property owner's quo warranto action challenging the validity of the city's annexation of certain uninhabited territory as being in violation of the Annexation of Uninhabited Territory Act of 1939, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 35300-35326.


OVERVIEW: The property owner appealed on grounds including that the city had allegedly failed to give written notice of the annexation proceedings to one owner within the territory, that the notice and hearing did not satisfy statutory requirements, that the annexation was unreasonable, and took property without due process of law. The court affirmed the trial court's order granting judgment in favor of the city. The court held that the city complied with the notice and hearing requirements for the annexation proceeding, and that the protests received by the city council did not deprive the city of jurisdiction to annex the territory. The court held that the annexation was not unreasonable, and the permissible shape, character, and extent of the territory annexed was a political question. The court held that the annexation was not a fraudulent abuse of the authority conferred by Cal. Gov't Code §§ 35300-35326, and did not operate to take property without due process of law. The court stated that the prospect that the city would extend its ordinary municipal services such as police and fire protection to the annexed territory would justify the imposition of ad valorem taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed trial court's order granting judgment in favor of the city in the quo warranto action challenging the validity of the city's annexation of the uninhabited territory.

 

402 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Department of Mental Hygiene v. McGilvery


L. A. No. 24830


Supreme Court of California


50 Cal. 2d 742; 329 P.2d 689; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 191



September 12, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Burnett Wolfson, Judge.

Action by the Department of Mental Hygiene to enforce payment of its claim for maintenance of a mentally ill person in a state hospital.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant executor challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of respondent Department of Mental Hygiene (department) in an action brought by the department to enforce payment of its claim for maintenance of a mentally ill person in a state hospital.


OVERVIEW: A decedent's daughter was adjudged to be mentally ill, and the department provided mental health care to her. After the decedent's death, the department brought an action against the decedent's estate to recover the costs of the care provided to the daughter. The trial court entered a judgment in favor of the department, and the executor of the estate sought review. The court affirmed and held that the decedent was liable for his daughter's care under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6650. Also, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6652, the department had the authority to bring an action to collect the costs from the estate. Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6650 et seq. did not violate the decedent's equal protection right because it did not place him in a discriminatory classification. The executor failed to overcome the presumption that the department acted in accordance with the Constitution and laws of California. Finally, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6650 et seq. was not an unconstitutional taking of private property without compensation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the department in its action against the executor to enforce payment of the department's claim for maintenance of a mentally ill person in a state hospital.

 

403 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Superior Court


L. A. No. 24942


Supreme Court of California


50 Cal. 2d 693; 329 P.2d 5; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 187



August 19, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Superior Court of Los Angeles County to assign a malpractice case to some judge other than the one assigned to hear the case.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, plaintiffs in a pending malpractice action, sought a writ of mandamus to compel the respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to disqualify the judgment assigned to their malpractice lawsuit and assign their case to another judge pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6.


OVERVIEW: The superior court asserted that § 170.6 made an unconstitutional delegation of legislative and judicial powers to litigants and was an unwarranted interference with the powers of the court because it failed to require the litigant to set forth the reasons that constituted the basis for the assertion that the judge was prejudiced and because there was no provision for a judicial determination of whether the judge was prejudiced. Deferring to the legislature, the court determined that the legislature could have reasonably concluded that a party should have an opportunity to obtain the disqualification upon a sworn statement. The possible abuse inherent in the procedure of § 170.6 was a matter to be balanced against the attainment of the statute's objective. The court rejected the argument that the filing of an affidavit without a hearing was unconstitutional as depriving the court of judicial power because the legislature required a showing in the form of a statement under oath.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a writ of mandate to issue directing the superior court to assign the case for trial to a different judge.

 

404 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Abbott v. Los Angeles


L. A. Nos. 24354, 24355, 24356, 24357, 24358


Supreme Court of California


50 Cal. 2d 438; 326 P.2d 484; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 167



June 6, 1958 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 2, 1958.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. A. A. Scott, Judge.

Actions to enforce pension payments on a fluctuating basis rather than in fixed amounts determined at time the pensions were granted.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgments adverse to plaintiffs, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff pension claimants (claimants) brought an action against defendants, a city and its pension board (collectively city), seeking to enforce pension payments upon a fluctuating basis rather than in fixed amounts. The claimants sought both declaratory and money judgments. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) ruled in favor of the city. The claimants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Claimants were employees and widows of employees of the city. While the employees were employed, but prior to retirement, the city amended its charter to grant pensions on a fixed, rather than a fluctuating, basis. The claimants alleged that amendments were invalid as applied to them. The court noted that the claimants' rights to the fluctuating pensions had vested during their employment and could only be modified prior to retirement for the purpose of maintaining the integrity of the pension system. The court found that no new advantage had been granted to the claimants that would be substantially commensurate with the disadvantages of the amendments. Further, there was no showing that the amendments bore any material relation to the integrity or preservation of the pension program applicable to the claimants. The city's allegation of rising costs alone did not excuse the city from meeting its contractual obligations. However, pursuant to the city charter, the amount of damages was limited to those payments that had accrued within six months prior to the time the claimants presented claims therefor.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower court was reversed.

 

405 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wein


Crim. No. 6130


Supreme Court of California


50 Cal. 2d 383; 326 P.2d 457; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 164



May 27, 1958 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 25, 1958. Carter, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b)) from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. LeRoy Dawson, Judge.

Prosecution for robbery, rape, sex perversion, kidnaping and kidnaping for purpose of robbery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed; motion to augment record, denied. Judgments of conviction of kidnaping for purpose of robbery imposing death penalty, and judgments of conviction of other offenses imposing imprisonment for terms prescribed by law, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) found the defendant guilty of robbery, rape, sex perversion, kidnapping, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, and sentenced him to death. A motion for a new trial was denied. The judgments of death were appealed automatically pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 1239(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant objected to jurors' examination on their opinions on capital punishment and to the discussion of their responsibility for imposing the death penalty if warranted by the facts of the case. On appeal, the court found that a prosecutor in a death penalty case has the right to ascertain the views of the potential jurors. He also asserted that it was prejudicial error for the prosecutor to have stated both in his voir dire examination of the jury and his closing argument that a sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole would not necessarily mean that he would remain in prison for the remainder of his life. On appeal, the court found that the remarks could only have been taken by the jurors as factors to be considered in assessing the penalty. The defendant also argued that Cal. Pen. Code § 209, which permitted the imposition of the death penalty for the crime of kidnapping to commit robbery if the victim suffers bodily harm, applied only where the movement was over a substantial distance and not merely between the rooms in a dwelling. However, the court held that it was the fact, not the distance, of forcible removal that constituted kidnapping.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to augment the record and affirmed the judgments of death and the order denying a new trial.

 

406 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Johnston v. Claremont


L. A. No. 24247


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 2d 826; 323 P.2d 71; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 273



March 26, 1958 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Plaintiffs and Appellants for a Rehearing was Denied April 23, 1958. Shenk, J., Carter, J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Donald R. Wright, Judge pro tem. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action for declaratory relief with respect to a zoning ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed; application for leave to produce additional evidence and for Supreme Court to make additional findings, denied. Judgment holding that ordinance rezoning plaintiffs' property as commercial property was valid, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both plaintiff property owner and defendant city appealed from a judgment in a declaratory relief action in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that an ordinance of the city rezoning the owner's property as commercial property was valid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property owner's land was zoned for agricultural and residential use. He requested that defendant city change the zoning from residential to a commercial zone, which was done by adopting a city ordinance. A majority of the voters had voted against the ordinance at a subsequent referendum. The issue before the court was whether the zoning ordinance was subject to referendum. The amendment of legislation was necessarily legislative in character. Where the city amended a zoning ordinance so as to exclude from a particular zone property previously included in such zone, its act in amending was a legislative act and not an administrative act. Cal. Gov. Code § 65804 made no distinction between adopting zoning ordinances and making amendments thereto. As it was acting in a legislative capacity, the ordinance was subject to referendum. Since no hearing before the legislative body was held, the ordinance was not validly enacted. If plaintiffs' position were adopted, it would give to a validating act a prospective operation, and would prevent it from having a uniform application, and thus the act would be unconstitutional, in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and denied the application for leave to produce additional evidence and for the supreme court to make additional findings.

 

407 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Mendoza v. Small Claims Court


L.A. No. 24776


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 2d 668; 321 P.2d 9; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 255



Jan. 28, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Edward T. Bishop, Frank G. Swain and Julius V. Patrosso, Judges. Reversed.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel a small claims court to try a proceeding in unlawful detainer. Judgment granting writ, reversed.



DISPOSITION: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied February 26, 1958.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant small claims court sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in a proceeding in mandamus to compel a small claims court to try a proceeding in unlawful detainer. Respondent had brought a proceeding in unlawful detainer for default of rent in small claims court.


OVERVIEW: The small claims court had dismissed the action upon its own motion on the ground that the 1955 amendment to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 117, upon which its jurisdiction was predicated, was unconstitutional. From a judgment of the superior court that a writ of mandate issue compelling the small claims court to entertain the proceeding, the small claims court appeals. The court held thata stay of proceedings on a judgment in unlawful detainer by the small claims court was discretionary with that court, and there was no automatic stay of proceedings whether or not an undertaking was filed. However, the court held that actual possession was a most valued right and was an essential part of property, and no one, consistent with constitutional safeguards, could be deprived of the possession or title to property, or any other substantial right, without reasonable notice and opportunity to be heard. The court held that since there was no automatic stay and the defendant could be dispossessed prior to the trial de novo, the 1955 amendment to § 117 was therefore void insofar as it purported to give jurisdiction to small claims courts in unlawful detainer proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower reviewing court, which entered judgment in a proceeding in mandamus to compel a small claims court to try a proceeding in unlawful detainer. The court held that the statute which purported to give jurisdiction to small claims courts in unlawful detainer actions was unconstitutional.

 

408 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Garmon v. San Diego Bldg. Trades Council


L.A. No. 23005


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 2d 595; 320 P.2d 473; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 249; 41 L.R.R.M. 2496



Jan. 16, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. John A. Hewicker, Judge. Affirmed in part and reversed in part.

Action against unions to enjoin picketing and to recover damages. Judgment for plaintiffs reversed insofar as it awarded injunctive relief, and affirmed insofar as it awarded damages.



DISPOSITION: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied February 13, 1958. Gibson, C.J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of San Diego County, California, found that defendant unions' intent was to compel plaintiffs, a lumber business, to require their employees to join one of their unions, and enjoined the unions from picketing and awarded damages to the business. The judgment was affirmed. On certiorari the Supreme Court of the United States ordered that the judgment of California Supreme Court be vacated and the cause be remanded.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court found that the business could not be granted federal judicial relief and, because its business in interstate commerce was less than $ 500,000 per year, the NLRB refused to exercise jurisdiction. The business was caught in a "no man's land" in which no equitable relief could be granted by a state court. The court concluded from the decisions of the United States Supreme Court that the business was without equitable relief under federal law. The court found that the policy declared by the state legislature concerning coercive conduct between employer and employee as to whether the employee should join a particular union is a valid state policy. The court also found that the trial court correctly concluded that by their demand the unions sought to require the business to interfere with the bargaining rights of their employees and force upon them terms and conditions of their employment and labor representation not of their own choosing and that in fact they had rejected. The conduct, the court concluded, constituted an unlawful labor practice contrary to and in violation of state law. The award of damages was supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court in part and reversed in part. The judgment for the business was reversed insofar as it awarded injunctive relief, and affirmed insofar as it awarded damages.

 

409 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Seven Up Bottling Co. v. Grocery Drivers Union


L.A. No. 23627


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 2d 625; 320 P.2d 492; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 250; 41 L.R.R.M. 2512



Jan. 16, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. A. Curtis Smith, Judge. Affirmed in part and reversed in part.

Action to enjoin a union and others from picketing and other interference with plaintiff's business and for damages. Judgment for plaintiff reversed insofar as it awarded injunctive relief and affirmed insofar as it awarded damages.



DISPOSITION: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied February 13, 1958. Gibson, C.J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant union sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) ruling in favor of respondent employer in the employer's action to enjoin the union and others from picketing and interfering with its business and for damages.


OVERVIEW: The employer entered into a collective bargaining agreement concerning the wages, hours, and working conditions of its nonsupervisory employees with an employees' association. Soon thereafter, the union engaged in activities designed to compel the employer to recognize it as the exclusive bargaining representative of the employees. The union engaged in peaceful picketing of retail stores which sold the employer's beverage products. Truck drivers of suppliers refused to cross the picket lines to make deliveries, thus compelling some retail stores to cease handling the employer's products. The court held that the trial court's award of damages under the circumstances was proper. However, the trial court had no jurisdiction to award injunctive relief where the union claimed that the employer was engaged in interstate commerce. The trial court erred in finding that state courts had concurrent jurisdiction with the National Labor Relations Board in entertaining such a controversy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment for the employer insofar as it awarded injunctive relief and affirmed insofar as it awarded damages.

 

410 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Wirin v. Parker


L. A. No. 24053


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 890; 313 P.2d 844; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 237



August 6, 1957 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 4, 1957. Shenk, J., and Spence, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Philbrick McCoy, Judge.

Action to enjoin chief of police from expending public funds to conduct police surveillance by means of concealed microphones.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendant reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which was entered in favor of defendant public servant, who was the chief of police, in the citizen's action to enjoin the public servant from expending public funds to conduct police surveillance by means of concealed microphones.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a, the citizen filed an action to enjoin the illegal expenditure of public funds for dictograph surveillance. The complaint alleged that the installation, maintenance, and use of dictographs was in a house, apartment, room, office, store, bar, jail cell, or other place of occupancy, without the consent, knowledge, or permission of persons who were present during such installation, maintenance, or use. The trial court entered a judgment in favor of the public servant, which the court reversed. The court held that an injunction against the alleged activity was proper because the authorization and direction for the use of dictograph surveillance by the public servant was in violation of U.S. Const. amends. IV and XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 19. Moreover, the public servant's answer indicated that he intended to continue to engage in the illegal conduct. The court held that an injunction against the expenditure of public funds to defray the cost of the entry upon private premises without the possessor's consent for the purpose of secreting a microphone could be easily understood and obeyed and would not have inhibited lawful police activity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that was entered in favor of the public servant in the citizen's action for an injunction.

 

411 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Carmen


Crim. No. 5667


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 851; 313 P.2d 817; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 236



August 2, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner was convicted for first-degree murder and assault with intent to commit murder. The prisoner filed a habeas corpus petition to secure release from custody upon alleged jurisdictional grounds on the basis of allegations that the prisoner and the victims were "Indians" and that the murder and the assault had been committed in "Indian country."


OVERVIEW: At the prisoner's trials, the prosecution alleged and proved that offenses were committed in the county in which the trials were held. At the time of oral argument on the appeal from the murder conviction, the prisoner suggested for the first time that the murder had been committed on a small tract of land the constituted "Indian country," and that the prisoner was an "Indian," with the result that exclusive jurisdiction was vested in the federal courts. The prisoner's habeas corpus petition claimed that the state courts lacked jurisdiction over the offenses for which he was convicted. The court held that the prisoner's allegations were insufficient to entitle him to any relief in the habeas corpus proceeding because, in the absence of exceptional circumstances which were not present, the prisoner was not entitled to contest, in a collateral attack upon the final judgments of conviction, the trial court's determination and exercise of jurisdiction, upon the basis of new and additional facts which did not appear in the trial court record.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of habeas corpus. The writ was discharged and the prisoner was remanded to custody.

 

412 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Geiger v. Board of Supervisors


Sac. No. 6796


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 832; 313 P.2d 545; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 233



July 19, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Board of Supervisors of Butte County to suspend and reconsider an ordinance providing for a sales and use tax, and either to repeal the ordinance or submit it to the referendum vote of the electors of the county.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizen sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent county board of supervisors (board) to suspend and reconsider an ordinance providing for a sales and use tax and either to repeal the ordinance or submit it to the referendum vote of the electors of the county.


OVERVIEW: A county ordinance levied a sales tax upon retailers at the rate of one per cent of their gross receipts from all tangible personal property sold in the county, and imposed a use tax at the same rate. Prior to the effective date of the ordinance, the board was presented with a properly certified petition demanding that the ordinance be repealed or submitted to the voters of the county. After receiving an opinion of the attorney general, which concluded that the ordinance was not subject to referendum, the board refused the demands. The court denied the writ, finding that the constitutional provisions that reserved the referendum powers to the people expressly excepted tax levies or appropriations for the usual current expenses of the state. The same exception applied to the referendum powers reserved to the county electors, and, therefore, Cal. Const. art IV, § 1 did not secure to county electors the right of referendum over tax levies for the usual current expenses of county government. It was not disputed that the revenue from the tax was to be used for the usual current expenses of the county, and it followed that the ordinance was not subject to referendum.


OUTCOME: The court denied the citizen's petition for a writ of mandate to compel the board to suspend and reconsider an ordinance providing for a sales and use tax and either to repeal the ordinance or submit it to the referendum vote of the electors of the county.

 

413 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Arenson v. National Auto. & Casualty Ins. Co.


L. A. No. 24193


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 528; 310 P.2d 961; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 202



May 10, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Philip H. Richards, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief and to recover from an insurance company the amount of a municipal court judgment, costs incurred in an action against the insured which the company wrongfully refused to defend, and the insured's obligation to pay an attorney's fee for the defense of that action.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment awarding plaintiff an amount less than that to which he was entitled, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured filed an action for declaratory relief and to recover from defendant insurer the amount of a municipal court judgment, costs incurred in an action against the insured which the insurer wrongfully refused to defend, and the insured's obligation to pay an attorney's fee for the defense of that action. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) on remand awarded $ 175 for attorney fees. The insured appealed.


OVERVIEW: The insured argued that the award was inadequate and unsupported by the evidence. On appeal the court agreed, reversed the trial court, and remanded for a new trial. The insured argued that the attorney fees were not reasonable because the insured had hired an attorney that the insured could not afford and that the insured had failed in his constitutional challenge to the statute that was the basis of the judgment against him. The court found that the insurer by respecting its contract with the insured could have prevented the extensive litigation in which the insured engaged in defending the action initiated by the school district. The insured did not voluntarily employ an attorney but was compelled to do so when he and his wife were sued on a claim covered by the insurer's policy. The insurer had refused to either defend or to settle. The insured was warranted, so far as related to the liability of the insurer, in proceeding as he did in defense of the municipal court action, and the attorney's fees were properly incurred for all of the services which the attorney performed. The court further found that the fee of $ 175 was grossly inadequate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and remanded for a new trial.

 

414 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Union Oil Co.


Sac. No. 6610


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 476; 310 P.2d 409; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 197



April 26, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Grover W. Bedeau, Judge.

Action to recover interest on overpayments of franchise tax refunded to defendant.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant taxpayer sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) entered in favor of plaintiff state in plaintiff's action to recover an overpayment of a tax refund.


OVERVIEW: Defendant taxpayer received a refund on certain taxes it had paid after plaintiff state changed a tax regulation. The refund included interest on the overpayments. Plaintiff filed an action to recover the interest paid, alleging that the payment of interest was the result of a mistake of law. The trial court entered judgment in favor of plaintiff. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that absent a statute, plaintiff was not liable to pay interest on any indebtedness. The court held that defendant was not entitled to retain the interest paid because an amendment to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 26080, which was adopted after defendant paid the taxes and before the refunds were issued, did not unconstitutionally impair a vested contractual rights. The court held that the relationship between plaintiff and defendant was not contractual. Defendant had no vested right to receive interest on the refunds and that the legislature could cut off the right of taxpayer to receive interest. The court also held that plaintiff's action was governed by the three-year statute of limitations for relief on the ground of mistake, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 338.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court entered in favor of plaintiff state because defendant taxpayer had no vested contractual right to receive interest on refunds of overpaid taxes, and plaintiff's cause of action was governed by the three-year statute of limitations for relief on the grounds of mistake.

 

415 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


First Unitarian Church v. County of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 23847


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 419; 311 P.2d 508; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 195



April 24, 1957 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 22, 1957. Gibson, C. J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Bayard Rhone, Judge.

Action to recover taxes paid under protest and for declaratory relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants on order sustaining general demurrer to the complaint without leave to amend, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff church sought to recover taxes paid under protest to defendant county; the church also sought declaratory relief. The church had refused to include in its property tax exemption application the nonsubversive declarations required by Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 32 (1953). The Superior Court of Los Angeles (California) sustained a general demurrer to the complaint without leave to amend. The church appealed.


OVERVIEW: The church contended that the county tax assessor unconstitutionally denied the church a property tax exemption and that Cal. Const. art. XX § 19 (1952) was an unlawful limitation of its First and Fourteenth Amendment rights. Making an oath was contrary to the church's religious beliefs. The court held that the church was not excused from making the oath any more than any other taxpayer because the oath obviously was not a test of the church's religious opinion. If the church objected to making an oath, it had to be for reasons relating to the content of the particular oath and not merely because it was an oath. The limitation on speech was a conditional one, imposed only if a tax exemption was sought, therefore, by no standard could the infringement on freedom of speech be deemed a substantial one. The law demanded the ascertainment of nonexistence of subversion for purposes of taxation, and Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 32 (1953) required the church's cooperation in establishing it. Because the church failed and refused to allege that it had complied with the law, the complaint failed to state a cause of action.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the church's complaint failed to state a cause of action. The demurrer was properly sustained without leave to amend.

 

416 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Prince v. San Francisco


S. F. No. 19450


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 472; 311 P.2d 544; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 196



April 24, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. William T. Sweigert, Judge.

Action to recover taxes paid under protest and for declaratory relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) ruling in favor of defendant City and County of San Francisco in the taxpayer's action to recover taxes paid under protest and for declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was a veteran of World War II and obtained tax exemptions from the government pursuant to the provisions of Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 1 1/4. However, when Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 32 was added to implement the provisions of Cal. Const. art. XX, § 19, the taxpayer refused to sign the non-subversive affidavit. The government denied the taxpayer's application for the exemption, and the taxpayer paid under protest. The taxpayer argued that Cal. Const. art. XX, § 19 and Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 32 were unconstitutional. The court held that the pursuit of benefits for veterans by the means employed in Cal. Const. art. XX, § 19 and implementing legislation did not in any way violate the right of free speech. Further, the classifications imposed were reasonable and proper, they did not violate any constitutional provision, and the oath requirement was valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment for the government.

 

417 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


State, Subsequent Injuries Fund v. Industrial Acci. Com.


L. A. No. 24468


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 365; 310 P.2d 7; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 190



April 19, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING to review an award of the Industrial Accident Commission apportioning liability of successive employers for occupational disease contracted by employe.



DISPOSITION: Award annulled with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State of California Subsequent Injuries Fund (state fund) sought review of an award of respondent Industrial Accident Commission apportioning liability of successive employers for an occupational disease contracted by respondent employee.


OVERVIEW: The state fund challenged a ruling by the commission in favor of an employee who suffered permanent partial disability from silicosis resulting from successive employments in underground metal mining and the challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Lab. Code § 5500.5. The court found the statutory section constitutional, but found that the commission erroneously issued an award of workmen's compensation against the state fund directly in favor of the employee, rather than an apportionment or reimbursement award in favor of one or more of his employers against whom award in his favor could be made. The court found that in accordance with Cal. Const. art. XX, § 21, provision for the state fund was encompassed within the legislature's plenary power, unlimited by any provision of the constitution, to create, and enforce a complete system of workmen's compensation. The statute was thus, constitutional. However, the award should have been made in favor of the employer or employers who paid the original award, payable out of the state fund in an amount equal to the unreimbursed portions of the original payment or payments to which such employer or employers were found entitled.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the award of the commission and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

 

418 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Los Angeles v. Belridge Oil Co.


L. A. No. 24129


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 2d 320; 309 P.2d 417; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 184



April 12, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Joseph W. Vickers, Judge.

Action to recover license taxes.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of respondent taxpayer in an action to recover license taxes.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that the taxpayer was subject to the license tax for such portion of its gross receipts as were derived from or attributable to its business or selling activities in the city and that not more than 20 percent of the taxpayer's total gross receipts for the years at issue were derived from or attributable to defendant's business in the city. The court affirmed. The court found that to allow a city to levy a license tax based upon gross receipts attributable to selling activities outside the city was an unreasonable discrimination and a denial of equal protection of the law. The court determined that a just and reasonable construction required that the measure of the tax be limited to those gross receipts attributable to selling activities within the city.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

419 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ivanhoe Irrigation Dist. v. All Parties & Persons


L. A. No. 23043


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 2d 597; 306 P.2d 824; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 293



January 24, 1957 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by Ivanhoe Irrigation Dist. v. McCracken, 357 U.S. 275, 78 S. Ct. 1174, 2 L. Ed. 2d 1313, 1958 U.S. LEXIS 1774 (1958)
Subsequent appeal at Ivanhoe Irrigation Dist. v. All Parties & Persons, etc., 53 Cal. 2d 692, 3 Cal. Rptr. 317, 350 P.2d 69, 1960 Cal. LEXIS 246 (1960)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County. Benjamin C. Jones, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Proceeding by an irrigation district to obtain confirmation of a contract entered into by it with the United States, by which the United States undertook to deliver water from the Central Valley Project to the district and to expend funds for the construction of a distribution system within the district.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for objecting defendants, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants state and irrigation district challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) in favor of respondent landowner, enjoining the irrigation district from failing or refusing to supply the landowner with water for irrigation purposes.


OVERVIEW: A state and an irrigation district imposed a limitation on how much land a landowner could own in order for water to be distributed to him. The trial court entered a judgment in favor of the landowner and enjoined the irrigation district from refusing to provide water to the landowner for irrigation purposes. The state and the irrigation district sought review, and the court affirmed. The court held that title to the water in the state was with the state, but was held in trust for the benefit of the water users in the state. The irrigation district was not permitted to discriminate against the landowner solely because he owned more land than was allowed by a contract between the states and the United States. The actions of the irrigation district in not distributing water to the landowner constituted a trespass to the title of the water under Cal. Water Code § 1052.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the landowner.

Santa Barbara County Water Agency v. All Persons & Parties


L. A. No. 22761


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 2d 699; 306 P.2d 875; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 296



January 24, 1957 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 19, 1957. Gibson, C. J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County. Charles F. Blackstock, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Proceeding by a county water agency to secure a judicial determination of the legality of the establishment of such agency and its member units and the validity of a master contract between the agency and the United States and member unit contracts entered into between the agency and certain public bodies.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed insofar as it declared validity of existence of agency and member units, and reversed insofar as judgment declared validity of contracts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, persons having or claiming to have an interest in the formation of a county water agency, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which entered judgment confirming the validity of a master contract between the agency and the United States Bureau of Reclamation, and five member unit contracts between the water agency and each of five public bodies.


OVERVIEW: The interested persons contended that the Santa Barbara County Water Agency Act violated Cal. Const. art. IV, § 31, which prohibited legislative authorization for the donation of public funds. On appeal, the court held that the financial assistance of the agency was a proper means of effecting the public purpose for the general good of the whole of the area served by the agency. However, the court held that the excess land provisions of the contracts involved here would have deprived the landowners of the member units of their property rights without due process of law and would have constituted a denial of the equal protection of the laws. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment insofar as it declared the validity of the Santa Barbara Water Agency Act and the existence of the county water agency and its member units. However, insofar as the judgment confirmed and declared valid the master contract and the five member unit contracts it was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, and reversed in part, the trial court's judgment in favor of the county water agency. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment insofar as it declared the validity of the existence of the county water agency and its member units, and reversed insofar as the judgment declared the validity of certain contracts containing excess land provisions.

 

421 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. McCaughan


Crim. No. 6050


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 2d 409; 317 P.2d 974; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 275



Nov. 19, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County and from orders denying a new trial and granting probation. Frank C. Damrell, Judge. Reversed.

Prosecution for involuntary manslaughter. Orders denying new trial and admitting defendant to probation, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California) convicting her of involuntary manslaughter under Cal. Penal Code § 192(2) and the trial court's orders denying a new trial and granting probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of involuntary manslaughter after an inmate under her care died as a result of being force-fed. The trial court denied defendant's motion for a new trial and admitted her to probation. Defendant appealed. The court reversed. The court held that the trial court improperly instructed the jury with respect to Cal. Penal Code § 361 because the provisions of § 361 were so vague and uncertain that defendant's conviction was a denial of due process of law. The court concluded that the words "harsh" and "unkind" in the statute did not provide an ascertainable standard of conduct or a workable standard of guilt and that insofar as it purported, without further definition, to make "harsh" and "unkind" treatment criminal offenses, § 361 was void for vagueness. The court stated that in view of the conflicting evidence as to the nature of defendant's acts and the general verdict, the error was prejudicial. However, the court also stated that the unconstitutional provisions of § 361 relating to "harsh" and "unkind" treatment were severable from the constitutional provisions relating to "cruel" treatment and "neglect of duty" and did not vitiate the whole statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the trial court, which denied defendant a new trial and admitted defendant to probation after she was convicted of involuntary manslaughter.

 

422 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


State, Subsequent Injuries Fund v. Industrial Acci. Com.


L.A. No. 24469


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 2d 354; 317 P.2d 8; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 268



Nov. 12, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING by Subsequent Injuries Fund for annulment of an award made against it in a proceeding by insurance carriers to secure apportionment of liability to an employe who became permanently totally disabled as a result of successive employments. Award affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State of California Subsequent Injuries Fund sought annulment of a reimbursement award it made to respondent California Industrial Accident Commission in a proceeding by various insurance carriers to secure an apportionment of liability to an employee who became permanently disabled from silicosis suffered as the result of successive employments over 40 years.


OVERVIEW: The Fund claimed that the language of Cal. Lab. Code § 5500.5 did not authorize reimbursement to the carriers. If the court found that § 5500.5 did authorize reimbursement to the carriers, the Fund maintained that the statute was unconstitutional. Upon review, the court observed that the language of § 5500.5 did not expressly permit payments to a carrier. However, various other workmen's compensation statutes made no distinction between an employer or a carrier with respect to reimbursement rights. A legislative declaration stated that in a situation such as the employee's, it was inequitable for total liability to fall on one or more of his employers that were solvent or that had solvent carriers. The court concluded that under § 5500.5, the legislature contemplated reimbursement of the carrier as well as the employer. The court held that reimbursement of carriers where the date of injury occurred subsequent to the effective date of § 5500.5 was not unreasonably discriminatory and was not a gift of public funds. Rather, it was ultimately reimbursement of the employers because the coal mining industry as a whole supported the carriers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reimbursement award the Fund made to the carriers.

People v. Valenti


Crim. No. 6062


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 2d 199; 316 P.2d 633; 1957 Cal. LEXIS 258



Oct. 29, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County dismissing an information during trial. William J. Keough, Judge pro tem. * Appeal dismissed.


UNKNOWN a1 Appointed pursuant to stipulation, see Const. art. VI, § 5.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which dismissed an information against defendant during trial. Defendant claimed that the appeal should have been dismissed because it was not one of the judgments or orders comprehended by Cal. Penal Code § 1238.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed the information based on the illegality of the arrest based on its mistaken view that the prosecution should not continue because of the illegality of the arrest. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision and dismissed the appeal holding that only by improperly strained and unnatural construction of the order and of § 1238 could the court hold that such order was appealable. The court held further that a reversal would have been futile because defendant had been in jeopardy within the meaning of the provision of Cal. Const. art. I, § 13, which forbids double jeopardy and, therefore defendant could not be tried again.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal order in favor of the defendant and dismissed the appeal.

 

424 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Mass


S. F. No. 19433


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 2d 494; 304 P.2d 1015; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 299



December 21, 1956 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Milton D. Sapiro, Judge.

Action to secure court permission to dismiss a school teacher.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for plaintiffs reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher appealed the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which ruled in favor of respondent school board in the dispute over his firing for refusal to answer questions on Fifth Amendment grounds.


OVERVIEW: In 1950 the teacher signed a required teacher's loyalty oath, in which he stated that he had been a member of the Communist Party, but that he was no longer connected with it. When the teacher was called before the House of Representatives Committee on Un-American Activities, he refused to answer a number of questions put to him by the committee on the ground of privilege against self-incrimination under the U.S. Const. amend. V. The school board terminated him under the provisions of Cal. Educ. Code § 12604, which provided that refusal to answer questions put to him was insubordination. The trial court upheld his dismissal. On appeal, the court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the case for further proceedings. The court held that the school board and the trial court incorrectly interpreted the statute as requiring dismissal, without any discretion, once the refusal to answer questions was proved. The court noted that such an interpretation would render the statute unconstitutional, and held that the act contemplated a full and fair hearing on the reasons for the refusal to answer questions, before a decision could be made to terminate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the school district, and directed the trial court to conduct a hearing to consider the teacher's reasons for refusing to answer questions, and any other matter germane to the charges against the teacher.

People v. Jefferson


Crim. No. 5931


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 2d 438; 303 P.2d 1024; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 293



December 4, 1956 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 3, 1957.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239) from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County and from an order denying a new trial. Grover W. Bedeau, Judge.

Prosecution for assault by life prisoner on another prisoner with a deadly weapon.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction imposing the death penalty, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied his motion for a new trial, convicted him, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4500, of assault with a deadly weapon by a prisoner undergoing a life sentence in a state prison, and imposed the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: A witness saw defendant stab the victim with a knife in a building at the state prison. Two knives with fresh bloodstains were found in the building. Defendant appealed his death sentence and conviction for assault with a deadly weapon by a prisoner undergoing a life sentence in a state prison. The court affirmed. The court held that the trial court did not err in denying a motion to set aside the indictment on the ground that the foreman did not comply with Cal. Penal Code § 907 because the evidence that defendant inflicted knife wounds on the victim was a reasonable ground for the grand jury's conclusion that he violated Cal. Penal Code § 4500. A certified copy of the judgment and commitment was substantial evidence that defendant was undergoing a life sentence at the time of the assault because the Adult Authority had not acted to fix the second degree murder sentence at a lesser term than life. The court found no variance in the indictment and proof and no prejudicial error in the refusal to give an instruction on lesser or included offenses, to admit sections of the penal code, or to grant a new trial based on evidence of which defense counsel was aware at trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's assault conviction and death sentence and the denial of his motion for a new trial.

Estate of Skinker


L. A. No. 24036


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 2d 290; 303 P.2d 745; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 277; 62 A.L.R.2d 1137



November 20, 1956 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County fixing an inheritance tax. L. N. Turrentine, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action against respondent state controller, appellants, an executrix and her attorney, challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which overruled their objections to the report of the inheritance tax appraiser and fixed an inheritance tax due by reason of the death of the executrix's decedent. At issue was the proper amount of the deduction for attorney's fees and the executrix's commission.


OVERVIEW: A state inheritance tax was imposed on transfers from decedents. Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 13988 provided an inheritance tax deduction for the ordinary commissions allowed executors under Cal. Prob. Code § 901. After the decedent died, § 901 was amended to provide a greater deduction. Use of the revised commission rate would have resulted in lower inheritance taxes, and appellants argued that the new law applied. The trial court rejected appellants' argument, and the court affirmed. The court held that because the entire statutory scheme of the inheritance tax laws and probate proceedings made valuations as of the date of the decedent's death, the law applicable to executor's commissions effective on the date of the decedent's death also applied. The construction of the law urged by appellants would have resulted in an unconstitutional gift of state funds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision in favor of the state controller in the action to set inheritance taxes for the estate.

In re Florance


Crim. No. 5935


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 2d 25; 300 P.2d 825; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 247



August 28, 1956 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner ward filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus to release the ward from custody in which she was placed when it was determined that her home was unfit and she was made a ward of the juvenile court.


OVERVIEW: The ward was declared a ward of the juvenile court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 700(d) because her home was found to be unfit after her mother was convicted of certain criminal offenses. The ward filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus to be released from custody, and the court issued a show cause order. The court discharged the show cause order and denied the ward's habeas corpus petition, holding that the order making the ward a ward of the juvenile court was appealable. The ward was therefore not entitled to a writ of habeas corpus because she had an adequate remedy on appeal. Finally, the ward's due process rights were satisfied because the juvenile court fulfilled its requirement of giving notice to the ward's parents of the proposal to have her declared a ward of the juvenile court.


OUTCOME: The court denied the ward's petition for a writ of habeas corpus to release her from custody.

Nathan H. Schur, Inc. v. Santa Monica


L. A. No. 23679


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 2d 11; 300 P.2d 831; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 245



August 17, 1956 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Frank G. Swain, Judge.

Consolidated actions for declaratory and injunctive relief.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for plaintiff in second Schur action, and judgment adverse to plaintiffs in Troeger action, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prospective licensee sought review from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied two consolidated actions for declaratory and injunctive relief. Appellant was seeking declarative and injunctive relief regarding the police chief's refusal to issue to appellant a license to operate games of skill.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and five other plaintiffs sought a declaration of their rights, that licenses be issued to them, and the city and its officers be enjoined from interfering with their businesses. The appellant's action and another action by a corporation similarly aggrieved were consolidated. At the trial of the consolidated actions the trial court heard evidence on the question of whether the games for which licenses were sought by were of skill or gambling and found that licenses were sought were of chance and violated the California Penal Code. On review, the court reversed and held that it was not concerned with the original complaint or with whether relief could have been granted at that time because the appellant followed the prescribed administrative proceedings available to them and all was done in accordance with city ordinances which required the denial of a license if the business, sought to be licensed violated the state laws against gambling. The court held that because local hearing agency, the city council, found the games did not violate the law and appellant was entitled to licenses that the police chief refused to issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed both judgments, which denied appellant's request for declarative and injunctive relief, and granted appellant a new trial on both actions.

Lynn v. Duckel


S. F. No. 19220


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 2d 845; 299 P.2d 236; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 236



July 24, 1956 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 21, 1956. Shenk, J., and Carter, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Preston Devine, Judge.

Action for a mandatory injunction and for damages.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowner sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which decided in favor of defendant director of public works on the homeowner's action to obtain a mandatory injunction and damages.


OVERVIEW: The alley was a 10-foot public alley that connected two streets. It was too steep and narrow for vehicular use, and it had always been used solely as a pedestrianway. The homeowner bought a house and lot, which contained a large depression and abutted the alley. The homeowner, without the required permit from the city's director, brought a bulldozer into the alley and used it to level off his lot. He then commenced running trucks in the alley, making it impassable to pedestrians. The director found the condition created by the homeowner to be dangerous to the public. He halted the running of the trucks and installed a post at the entrance of the alley. The homeowner then applied for a permit to build a paved roadway in the alley, which was denied. The court affirmed the judgment, by holding that the homeowner's action was barred because he did not exhaust his administrative remedy of appealing his permit denial prior to instituting the suit. The court further held that he was properly denied relief because he did not come into the trial court with "clean hands," as he ran trucks in the alley without a written permit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which decided in favor of the director on the homeowner's action to obtain a mandatory injunction and damages, because he did not exhaust his administrative remedy prior to instituting the suit. He was also properly denied relief because he did not have "clean hands," as he ran trucks in the alley without a written permit.

 

430 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Lundberg v. County of Alameda


S. F. No. 19026


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 2d 644; 298 P.2d 1; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 219



June 6, 1956 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 5, 1956. Shenk, J., Carter J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. A. J. Woolsey and Cecil Mosbacher, Judges. James R. Agee, Judge, dissented.

Action to challenge the legality of a tax exemption.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment declaring exemption invalid, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer, a citizen resident of defendant county, brought a suit to challenge the legality of a tax exemption under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 214. The Superior Court of Alameda County (California) declared the exemption invalid. The county and an intervening religious organization appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer challenged that portion of § 214 which granted a tax exemption to property used exclusively for school purposes of less than collegiate grade that were owned and operated by religious, hospital, or charitable funds, foundations, or corporations for nonprofit purposes. On appeal, the court made several findings. First, nonprofit schools owned by nonprofit organizations and operated for the benefit of the public came within the term "charitable" under Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 1c. Second, when more than one reasonable meaning of a provision of the California Constitution exists, it is the duty of the courts to accept that meaning chosen by the legislature. This fundamental principle is particularly applicable when, as here, the statute is enacted pursuant to an enabling provision of the California Constitution and there is not only a reasonable legislative construction of that provision but also approval of that construction by the people on referendum. Finally, the exemption did not contravene U.S. Const. amend. I. An exemption of property used for educational purposes may validly be applied to school property owned and operated by religious organizations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that declared the exemption invalid.

Lambreton v. Industrial Acci. Com.


L. A. No. 23976


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 2d 498; 297 P.2d 9; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 206

Employee appealed and argued that his first application for benefits sufficiently presented the misconduct issue to constitute the commencement of the proceedings for the collection of additional compensation. The court affirmed the commission's order of dismissal because the statute of limitations ran on employee's ability to seek increased compensation from employer.
