432 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nunn


Crim. No. 5748


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 2d 460; 296 P.2d 813; 1956 Cal. LEXIS 201



May 8, 1956 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 6, 1956. Carter, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, and from an order denying a new trial. Mildred L. Lillie, Judge.

Prosecution for illegally prescribing narcotics.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant osteopath sought review of the decision of the trial court, which convicted the osteopath on four counts of prescribing narcotics for a person not under his treatment for a pathology, in violation of Cal. Health and Safety Code § 11163, and one count of prescribing a narcotic for a person who represented himself to be an addict, in violation of § 11164.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the conviction was supported by the evidence and that there was no prejudice in admitting an opinion that most narcotic addicts are caused by criminal association. The court further held that the terms "in the regular practice of his profession" and "good faith" are both clearly understood from Cal. Health and Safety Code § 11163 and 11330 and have a commonly accepted meaning. The court additionally held that the statute could constitutionally prevent the osteopath from prescribing medication because the state had the power to regulate the practice of a profession that affected the public health and safety. The court finally held that the osteopath was not entrapped and that the jury was given clear advice on each and every material issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the osteopath's convictions.

 

433 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Calhoun v. Superior Court of San Diego County


L. A. No. 23779


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 2d 18; 291 P.2d 474; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 200



December 30, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied January 25, 1956. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Application Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of San Diego County and John A. Hewicker, Judge thereof, from requiring petitioner to stand trial on an indictment.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was indicted by the grand jury for conspiring to receive political contributions from persons licensed by the Board of Equalization (board) to sell alcoholic beverages under Cal. Elec. Code § 5002.5 and for obstruction of justice under Cal. Penal Code § 182(5). Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent Superior Court of San Diego County (California) from requiring petitioner to stand trial on the indictment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was a director of a nonprofit business association for businesses that sold alcoholic beverages and general counsel for an association of wholesale liquor distributors, which gave him the authority to contribute to political funds. The grand jury found that petitioner used his positions in these associations to fund a campaign to reelect a member of the board by improperly using, in part, monies provided by retail liquor licensees to fight a different political cause. Petitioner argued that the evidence before the grand jury furnished no reasonable basis for the charges against him and that the trial court expressed bias and prejudice against him in ruling upon his motion to quash the indictment. The court held that Cal. Elec. Code § 5002.5 and Cal. Penal Code § 182(5) were constitutional. The court further held that the evidence showed an elaborate conspiracy to utilize contributions from both retail and wholesale liquor licensees to finance a political campaign and that petitioner was connected with the campaign as more than a donor. The court also held that if petitioner believed that the trial court was not impartial his remedy was under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ.

 

434 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


General Electric Co. v. Superior Court of Alameda County


S. F. No. 19317


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 2d 897; 291 P.2d 945; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 379



December 29, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in prohibition to prevent the Superior Court of Los Angeles County from enforcing its order requiring petitioner to produce its books and records for inspection.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation brought an action to enjoin the retail sale of its products by the real party in interest at prices below those alleged to have been established in compliance with the statutorily established terms. Respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) ordered the corporation to produce its books and records for inspection. The corporation sought a writ of prohibition to prevent enforcement of the order.


OVERVIEW: The real party in interest alleged in its responsive pleading an affirmative defense that the corporation had arbitrarily and capriciously fixed the fair trade prices of its commodities far in excess of its costs and had an unlawfully unreasonable margin of profit on the public sale of its articles. The real party in interest further alleged that by virtue of the aforesaid conduct, the corporation had engaged in inequitable conduct that precluded it from seeking equitable relief. The corporation claimed that its profits were not material to any issue presented in the underlying action. The court held that the superior court should not have ordered the corporation to produce its books and records for inspection. The court found that the corporation's cost records were not shown to be material to any issue presented in the basic action and a determination of the corporation's profits alone would not have constituted substantial proof of an absence of fair and open competition.


OUTCOME: The court granted a peremptory writ of prohibition against the superior court to prevent the production of the corporation's profit records.

 

435 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


California Oregon Power Co. v. Superior Court of Siskiyou County


Sac. No. 6601


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 2d 858; 291 P.2d 455; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 375



December 29, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied January 25, 1956.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Siskiyou County from proceeding to try an action.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant power company sought a peremptory writ to prevent respondent Superior Court of Siskiyou County (California) from trying a pending action in which the power company was defendant and the state was plaintiff. The power company contended that jurisdiction was lacking over the action involving its impact on the levels of a river.


OVERVIEW: In the underlying action, the state contended that the fish it regularly stocked in a river died and that 14 people drowned because the power company caused a river's flow to fluctuate through the operation of its hydroelectric plants. The state asserted that the effect constituted a nuisance. After the state intervened in a power company proceeding before the Federal Power Commission, it sought an injunction as to the nuisance. The power company contended that the federal government occupied the field with regard to dams on navigable streams and that the superior court lacked jurisdiction to hear the state's action. The court found that the Federal Power Act (Act), 16 U.S.C.S. § 791 et seq., gave the federal government superior rights, such as the regulation of interstate and foreign commerce, and the administration of reservations and public lands. However, it also found that the Act left the states with their traditional subject matter jurisdiction. Therefore, the court held that the superior court had jurisdiction to hear the case because the state merely sought relief for a condition it contended was a nuisance.


OUTCOME: The court denied the power company's request for a peremptory writ.

 

436 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Scoville Mfg. Co. v. Skaggs Pay Less Drug Stores


S. F. Nos. 18830, 19068


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 2d 881; 291 P.2d 936; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 377



December 29, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 25, 1956. Schauer, J., and McComb, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County and from an order granting a temporary injunction. O. D. Hamlin, Jr., and Thomas J. Ledwich, Judges.

Action for an injunction.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed; appeal from order dismissed. Judgment granting injunction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporation sought review from two orders of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted temporary and permanent injunctions in favor of plaintiff corporation, in an action filed by plaintiff under the Fair Trade Act of 1931, as amended in 1933 and 1941, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code, §§ 16900- 16905, against defendant for failure to comply with a contractual arrangement with plaintiff concerning product pricing.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff entered into contractual arrangements with its buyers and wholesalers, including defendant, in which the prices of certain of plaintiff's products were required to be sold as specified. These contracts purported to be in compliance with Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 16902. Plaintiff filed suit against defendant, pursuant to the Fair Trade Act, Bus. & Prof. Code, §§ 16900- 16905, seeking to enjoin defendant's retail sale of its products below those specified by it as the producer. Temporary and permanent injunctions were granted in favor of plaintiff. On appeal of the two orders, the court affirmed judgment and held that both the Fair Trade Act and McGuire Act, which allowed for voluntary contract trade restraints under certain circumstances, were constitutional, because based on prior federal and state challenges, it was determined that the Fair Trade Act was a proper exercise of police power, it was not arbitrary or unreasonable legislation, and it did not deny due process of law or equal protection of the laws. The act was only a legislative declaration that contracts between private parties establishing certain retail prices was not against public policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment granting a temporary and permanent injunction in favor of plaintiff in an action filed against defendant for failure to comply with a contractual arrangement concerning retail sale of products at a certain market price.

 

437 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Martin


Crim. No. 5767


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 2d 755; 290 P.2d 855; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 364



December 9, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County granting motion to set aside an information. David Coleman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with two counts of horse-race bookmaking and two counts of keeping and occupying premises for the purposes of such bookmaking. The State challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendant's motion to set aside information on the ground that the evidence against him had been obtained in violation of his constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted defendant's motion on the ground that all the evidence had been obtained by illegal searches and seizures. Defendant was arrested after being found on two premises used for bookmaking. Defendant contended that when he was arrested, the police officers had unlawfully entered the premises in violation of his constitutional rights. Defendant also claimed that he was a stranger who had been hired to watch the premises. The State argued that because defendant disclaimed any interest in the premises, he lacked standing to challenge use of the evidence. The court declined to uphold the order of the trial court. First, the court rejected the State's argument and established a vicarious exclusionary rule, by which defendant had standing to challenge the evidence based on claimed violations of constitutional rights of other persons. The rule was similarly based on the purpose of disallowing the State from using evidence obtained in violation of constitutional guarantees. However, the court concluded that the police officers' conduct did not constitute an illegal entry into the premises. They had reasonable cause to believe that offenses were being committed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order setting aside the information because the evidence obtained as a result of defendant's arrest was not the product of illegal conduct on the part of the police officers.

 

438 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Tarantino


Crim. No. 5705


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 2d 590; 290 P.2d 505; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 348



November 28, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 28, 1955. Gibson, C. J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco and from an order denying a new trial. Eustace Cullinan, Jr., Judge.

Prosecution for conspiracy to commit extortion and for extortion.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and in part reversed and remanded for new trial. Judgment of conviction affirmed as to one count charging extortion, and reversed and remanded for new trial as to other counts charging extortion and conspiracy to commit extortion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted defendant of three counts of extortion and of conspiracy to commit extortion and which denied his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for conspiracy to commit extortion and for three counts of extortion. The state presented excerpts of tapes that related to threats, promises, and demands for money that corroborated the testimony of the state's witnesses. Defendant was convicted. On appeal, the court affirmed one count of conspiracy and reversed the other convictions. The court stated that the installation of the recording device into defendant's room, which was unconstitutional, was not made lawful by the authorization of the head of the police department or the district attorney. The court stated that it affirmed the one count of conspiracy because the evidence that related to that count was entirely independent of the recordings and convincingly established guilt. The court stated that it could not be said that the rest of the verdicts rested on evidence independent of the recordings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for one count of extortion. The court reversed defendant's convictions for the other two counts of extortion and the conviction for conspiracy to commit extortion. The court granted defendant a new trial.

 

439 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bertch v. Social Welfare Dep't


S. F. No. 19232


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 2d 524; 289 P.2d 485; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 342



November 15, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 14, 1955. Shenk, J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Albert C. Wollenberg, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to review an administrative order of the state Social Welfare Board.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment denying writ reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant church members challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied the church members a writ of mandamus in their proceeding for review of an order of respondent state Social Welfare Board (board). The board denied their appeals from county board of supervisors' decisions denying them benefits under the Old Age Security Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 2160 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The church members lived together in a home owned by their church, shared possessions, and received a low monthly stipend from the church for necessities. The board found that they were not "needy" persons under the Act because they had voluntarily accepted lower standards of living and any aid they received would be devoted to the church, not to their individual needs. The court reversed the judgment. The board properly made its decision according to the procedures of Cal. Gov't. Code § 104, and the church members were not entitled to a trial de novo. However, the findings on which the board based its conclusion that they were not needy were immaterial. The church members met the standards of need as defined in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 2160 because they were not receiving adequate support from a responsible relative, had not entered into life care contracts, and were not otherwise ineligible. The board's own manual indicated that the Act did not require income received to be expended for particular purposes or in particular amounts. The aid was not an unconstitutional gift of public funds because the care and relief of aged persons was a special matter of state concern.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment denying a writ of mandamus to the church members in their proceeding for review of the board's denial of their appeals from decisions of county boards of supervisors denying benefits to the church members under the Act. The board's petition for a rehearing was denied.

 

440 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ruiz v. Industrial Acci. Com.


L. A. No. 23751


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 2d 409; 289 P.2d 229; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 330



October 28, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING to review an order of the Industrial Accident Commission denying a claim for death benefits.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widow challenged the decision of respondent Industrial Accident Commission (California), which denied her claim for death benefits.


OVERVIEW: The widow's husband was found to have contracted silicosis in the course of his employment and disability payments and medical expenses were awarded. The denial of death benefits was based on the ground that the claim was not filed within 240 weeks from the date of injury, even though that period had elapsed before the time of death. The court affirmed. The plain language of the statute sustained the commission's order. The court found that the last paragraph of Cal. Lab. Code § 5406 unmistakably declared a time restriction that was based on two conditions conjunctively applicable. It was clear that the claim for death benefits, having been filed more than 240 weeks from the date of the decedent's injury did not meet the statutory requirement and was, therefore, barred. The court determined that the time limitation was more in the nature of a qualifying condition in the exercise of any right to death benefits. The court concluded that the widow's claim that the application of the statute was an unconstitutional deprivation of property without due process had no bearing because her right to recover death benefits was wholly statutory.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

Financial Indem. Co. v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County

Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 2d 395; 289 P.2d 233; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 329

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied November 23, 1955. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Application Should be Granted.

Roman Catholic Welfare Corp. v. Piedmont

Supreme Court of California

PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the issuance of a building permit.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner church filed a proceeding in mandamus against respondent city to compel the issuance of a building permit.


OVERVIEW: A city denied a church's request for a building permit was denied on the sole ground that the zoning ordinance of the city prohibited the construction of any school within a designated zone. The church filed a proceeding in mandamus to compel the issuance of a building permit. The court held (1) that the city's ordinance was unconstitutional and void because of its arbitrary and unreasonable discrimination against private schools; and, (2) that the city could not, by ordinance, constitutionally prevent the construction of a building to be used for private school purposes in an area where public schools were located.

443 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Steinmetz v. California State Board of Education


Sac. No. 6530


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 2d 816; 285 P.2d 617; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 279



July 5, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel petitioner's reinstatement as associate professor at a state college.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher brought an action in mandamus to compels his reinstatement as a professor at a state college, after he was dismissed because of his refusal at a hearing before the State Board of Education to answer two questions as to whether he was or had been a member of the Communist Party.


OVERVIEW: The teacher was fired after he refused to answer questions about whether or not he was or had ever knowingly been a member of the Communist Party. The court denied the writ of mandamus, holding that Cal Gov't. Code § 1028.1 specifically provided that it was the duty of a public employee, when ordered to do so, to appear before the governing body and answer questions as to present knowing membership in the Communist Party or as to past knowing membership. The court held that the statute's use of "knowing membership" referred to the person's knowledge of his membership rather than his knowledge of the characterization of the organization. The court found that the teacher's discharge was not because of membership in a proscribed organization but because of his refusal to answer the question. The court distinguished statutes that compelled disclosure of information concerning membership in a proscribed organization from statutes that provided for discharge or disqualification because of membership. The court held that where a statute merely provided for the disclosure of information, a requirement that an employee have knowledge of the nature of the organization was not necessary.


OUTCOME: The court denied the teacher's petition for a writ of mandamus reinstating him to his teaching position.

 

444 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pond


Crim. No. 5612


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 2d 665; 284 P.2d 793; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 267



June 10, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County denying a new trial. Charles W. Fricke, Judge.

Prosecution for grand theft.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Order denying defendant new trial, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied defendant's motion for a new trial after a jury had convicted him of grand theft, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 487(1), based on an alleged violation of Cal. Lab. Code § 405, not specifically charged in the indictment, which regulated misuse by an employer of a cash bond obtained from an employee.


OVERVIEW: The indictment charged defendant with grand theft, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 487(1), without reference to Cal. Lab. Code § 405. At trial, the trial court instructed the jury concerning the Labor Code section that made it a crime to obtain a bond from an employee and then use the funds for any purpose other than liquidation of accounts between the employer and employee or for return to the employee. The jury convicted defendant on counts where the evidence indicated that defendant had taken bonds from several employees and had commingled the property with his own. The court reversed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion for a new trial because it found that a charge under the Penal Code could not have been construed to include a charge that he violated a section of a different statute found within the Labor Code. It was impossible in the state of the record to determine whether convictions were based upon a permissible view of the evidence or upon a confusion which led the jury improperly to believe that the alleged offenses were violations of the Labor Code.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, which had denied defendant's motion for a new trial.

People v. Cahan


Crim. No. 5670


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 2d 434; 282 P.2d 905; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 243; 50 A.L.R.2d 513



April 27, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 25, 1955. Shenk, J., and Spence, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from orders of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County granting probation and denying a new trial. Harold W. Schweitzer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) granted defendant probation and denied him a new trial. Defendant appealed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of conspiring to engage in horse-race bookmaking and related offenses in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 337a. He was granted probation for a period of five years on the condition that he spend the first 90 days of his probationary period in the county jail and pay a $ 2,000 fine. He appealed from the order granting him probation and the order denying his motion for a new trial. Defendant contended that police obtained evidence to use at trial by illegal search and seizure. On appeal, the court reversed the orders of the trial court. The court held that evidence obtained in violation of the constitutional guarantees was inadmissible. The adoption of the exclusionary rule opened the door to the development of workable rules governing searches and seizures and the issuance of warrants that would protect both the rights guaranteed by the constitutional provisions and the interest of society in the suppression of crime.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court that granted defendant probation and denied him a new trial. The court held that the evidence obtained in violation of the constitutional guarantees and used at trial was inadmissible.

People v. Berry


Crim. No. 5656


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 2d 426; 282 P.2d 861; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 242



April 26, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County and from orders denying motions for new trial. Ben V. Curler, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Prosecution for assault by life prisoner on fellow prisoner in violation of Pen. Code, § 4500.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction imposing death penalty, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which convicted him of assault by life prisoner on fellow prisoner in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4500 and denied his motions for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant took an iron bar and struck a fellow prisoner two blows over the head, cracking his skull. As the result of the extensive skull fracture and brain damage, the victim died that night. The jury found defendant guilty of assault by life prisoner on fellow prisoner, and it also found him sane at the time of the crime's commission. Defendant appealed his conviction and two orders denying his motions for a new trial. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that § 4500 was constitutional in its definition of the element of "malice aforethought." There was sufficient evidence to establish that defendant was undergoing a life sentence. Though defendant admitted that he committed the assault, evidence of the victim's death was properly admitted. On the issue of sanity, the findings of the two court-appointed alienists were made in accord with defendant's rights as prescribed by Cal. Penal Code § 1027. The trial court did not err in instructing the jury on the presumption of sanity. The court rejected defendant's argument that the legal test of sanity based upon knowledge of "right and wrong" should have been changed by overruling the prior decisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for assault by life prisoner on fellow prisoner and defendant's death sentence. The court also affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motions for a new trial.

Mallon v. Long Beach


L. A. No. 23176


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 2d 199; 282 P.2d 481; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 221



April 5, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Respondent City of Long Beach for a Rehearing was Denied May 4, 1955. Shenk, J., Schauer, J., and Spence, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Paul Nourse, Judge.

Action to enjoin a city and its officers from appropriating and expending income derived from sale of oil and gas produced from tide and submerged lands granted in trust to city by state.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendants after demurrers to complaint were sustained without leave to amend, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of appellee city in its use of income derived from the sale of oil and gas produced from tide and submerged lands for general municipal purposes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant citizens sought to enjoin appellee city from appropriating and expending for general municipal purposes the income derived from the sale of oil and gas produced from the tide and submerged lands granted in trust to the city by the state. The trial court found in favor of the city, and the appellate court affirmed. On review, the court found that the city's interest in the tidelands was acquired not as a "municipal affair," but subject to a public trust to develop its harbor and navigation facilities for the benefit of the entire state and was therefore subject to the control of the legislature. The court concluded that the expenditures by the city for storm drains, a city incinerator, a public library, public hospitals, public parks, a fire alarm system, and many other items were purely "municipal affairs" within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XI, § 6. Moreover, they were normal expenditures for a municipal corporation to make. Therefore, the lower courts' decisions were reversed.


OUTCOME: The lower courts' decisions were reversed. Appellee city was unable to use public monies for general municipal purposes because the monies were to be used to benefit the entire state and would therefore violate the prohibition against using public monies as a gift.

 

448 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Temescal Water Co. v. Department of Public Works


L. A. No. 23083


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 2d 90; 280 P.2d 1; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 209



February 15, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County. Carl B. Hilliard, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel Division of Water Resources of Department of Public Works to withdraw and cancel a permit to appropriate water.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment denying writ reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant water company challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which sustained respondents', department of public works and water conservation district, demurrers to the water company's petition for writ of mandate without leave to amend. The water company sought to compel the department of public works to withdraw and cancel a permit issued to the water conservation district to appropriate water.


OVERVIEW: The water company argued that there was no unappropriated water available to supply the water conservation district pursuant to its application and that it was entitled to a writ of mandate under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1085. The court found that the proper remedy for reviewing the issuance of a permit, despite protest upon the ground of the unavailability of unappropriated water, was by a writ of mandate pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1094.5. The court found that the water company's petition for mandate did not state a cause of action under that section, because it did not allege that the water company had in any manner participated in the proceeding upon the application. The court found that the water company could establish its participation as an interested party in a judicial proceeding to review the department of public works' determination by making a protest to the application before the department of public works and demonstrating the interest in that proceeding. The court found that the trial court abused its discretion by sustaining without leave to amend the demurrers to the original petition, because it was capable of amendment to state a cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court with directions to permit the water company to amend its petition.

 

449 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Haggerty v. Associated Farmers of California, Inc.


S. F. No. 18825


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 2d 60; 279 P.2d 734; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 206



February 4, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 3, 1955. Gibson, C. J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County and from an order refusing to vacate the judgment. Edward L. Kellas, Judge.

Action for damages and to enjoin enforcement of a county ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for plaintiff reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Fresno County (California) that found in favor of respondent representative and permanently enjoined the county from enforcing or causing an ordinance to be enforced and concluded that the ordinance unconstitutionally abridged the right of free speech.


OVERVIEW: The county's ordinance made unlawful the emission or transmission of any loud or raucous noise upon or from any public highway or thoroughfare or from any aircraft. Shortly after its enactment, several persons were arrested upon charges of violating the ordinance. Those that were arrested were members of a farm labor union and by means of automobile horns and loudspeakers were attempting to attract the attention of farm workers to induce them to quit work and engage in a strike to enforce union demands for wages. The representative filed an action on behalf of those arrested to enjoin the enforcement of the ordinance. In reversing, the court found that the decisive question was whether the prohibition of the ordinance was a reasonable one. The court held that it was unnecessary to decide whether an absolute prohibition within the limits of a county or municipality could be upheld, because the ordinance under attack was not of that kind. Accordingly, the court held that the ordinance, upon its face, was not unconstitutional and did not violate free speech rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the lower court and found that the ordinance was not unconstitutional on its face.

 

450 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


San Francisco v. Ross


S. F. No. 18982


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 2d 52; 279 P.2d 529; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 205



February 3, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel city controller to certify to availability of funds for acquisition by eminent domain of off-street parking garage site.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, city and county, filed a motion in mandamus to compel respondent city controller to certify the availability of funds for acquisition by eminent domain of the proposed off-street parking garage site. The city controller asserted that the proposed facility was not for public use and thus, the city and county could not use its power of eminent domain to acquire the property.


OVERVIEW: The city approved a parking bond issue under the Parking Law of 1949, Cal. Sts. & High. Code §§ 32500-33552, but the city controller refused to certify the availability of funds in the city's off-street parking bond fund for the acquisition of the proposed site under its general power of eminent domain pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1238.1. The city controller held that the proposed garage was private in nature and not one for public use for which eminent domain could be used. The city and county filed a motion in mandamus to compel the city controller to certify the funds. The court denied the city's writ of mandamus holding that the proposed use of the land was not strictly for public use because there would be no stringent government controls over how the property would operate to insure the public interest, and that a portion of the ground floor of the building was to be leased and occupied by retail stores. The court held that the city's police power could not be used to regulate the activities of particular individuals and exclude from such regulations others of the same general class.


OUTCOME: The court denied the city and county's writ of mandamus seeking to compel the city comptroller to certify the availability of funds to acquire the land of the proposed site for an off-street parking facility in the downtown area of the city.

 

451 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Burchell v. Strube


S. F. No. 18780


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 2d 828; 279 P.2d 1; 1955 Cal. LEXIS 388



January 25, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 24, 1955.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. William T. Sweigert, Judge.

Interpleader action.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment against one defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant assignee of the heir-hunter's interest in the estate challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) granted in favor of respondents, administrator of estate and heirs of decedent. The assignee contested this decision precluding his claims based on res judicata of the prior probate court adjudication pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code § 1020.1.


OVERVIEW: The administrator filed an interpleader complaint in the superior court against the assignee, who claimed a 40 percent interest in the heirs' share in the estate, and also against the heirs, who contended that the assignee had no interest and that the prior probate court determination was res judicata. The assignee contested the adverse superior court judgment rendered based on res judicata due to the assignor's failure to appeal the probate court determination, which was held as binding on the parties on every issue. The court affirmed that judgment because the probate court fully and finally adjudicated all the parties' rights. The court determined that the assignment agreement was based on and included the terms of § 1020.1, which was intended to protect the heirs against the assignee's claims. The court concluded that this agreement's assignment of the heirs' interest in the estate before the distribution was subject to legislation like § 1020.1 that permitted judicial review of the value of the consideration given by the heirs. The court determined that § 1020.1 was not so indefinite in its terms as to render its application here an unconstitutional violation of due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court challenged by the assignee of the heir-hunter's interest in the estate, which was entered for the administrator and the heirs against the assignee. The superior court correctly decided that res judicata precluded the assignee's claims based on the binding, earlier probate court disposition that occurred under the statute.

 

452 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Livingston Rock & Gravel Co. v. County of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 22991


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 2d 121; 272 P.2d 4; 1954 Cal. LEXIS 233



June 25, 1954 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 21, 1954. Shenk, J., Carter, J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. J. T. B. Warne, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action to enjoin county from enforcing zoning ordinance provisions which would prohibit plaintiffs from conducting cement mixing plant in a rezoned district.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for plaintiffs reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, cement company and lessor, sought to enjoin defendant county from enforcing zoning ordinance provisions that would restrict them from operating a cement mixing plant in a rezoned district. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) determined that the ordinance could not be applied to require removal of an existing business. The county appealed.


OVERVIEW: The cement company leased space in a zoned area from the lessor to construct and operate a cement mixing plant. After the plant had been certificated, permitted, and went into operation, the area was rezoned under an ordinance pursuant to which the planning commission could conduct proceedings to require that nonconforming uses be closed. The cement company and lessor sought to enjoin the application of the provisions that would allow the existing business to be discontinued. The trial court determined that the ordinance could not be applied to require the removal of the existing business. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the application of the procedures through the planning commission was subject to review by certiorari or mandamus so that the cement company and lessor had an adequate remedy at law. The court concluded that because there was an adequate remedy at law to review the decision the cement company and lessor were not entitled to injunctive or declaratory relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the trial court, which enjoined the county from enforcing provisions of a zoning ordinance that would restrict the cement company and lessor from operating a cement plant in a rezoned area.

 

453 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Los Angeles v. Belridge Oil Co.


L. A. No. 22586


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 2d 823; 271 P.2d 5; 1954 Cal. LEXIS 213



May 26, 1954 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 23, 1954. Schauer, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Roy L. Herndon, Judge.

Action to recover license taxes.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendant reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the grant of summary judgment to respondent company from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in the city's action to recover business license taxes from the company.


OVERVIEW: The company produced and sold crude oil and natural gas. The company's main office was situated in the city's limits but the company's manufacturing and production plants were not. The company claimed it was not subject to the city's business license tax, because Los Angeles, Cal., City Tax Ordinance § 21.166 did not cover the production and sale of goods. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the catch-all provision of § 21.166 was broad enough to include the company's activities. The court found that the term "selling" as used in § 21.166 was not limited to just businesses that were predominately in sales, but included businesses like the company, that were engaged in fabricating, servicing, and supplying goods for a price. The court ruled that the fact that the company's products did not enter the city limits was immaterial because the actual sales of their products took place within the city's limits. The court held that the city's tax claim for a certain year was barred by the time limitations under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 343.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the company in the city's action for the recovery of business license taxes.

 

454 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Melancon v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L. A. No. 22883


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 2d 698; 268 P.2d 1050; 1954 Cal. LEXIS 198



April 16, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Superior Court of Los Angeles County to enforce petitioner's claimed right to take depositions in connection with a derivative stockholders' suit, and proceeding in prohibition to restrain such court from proceeding with a hearing to require petitioner to furnish security for expenses which defendants may incur in derivative suit.



DISPOSITION: Writs denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner shareholder filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to enforce petitioner's claim that he was entitled to take depositions of individuals and corporate officers in connection with a derivative stockholders' suit. Petitioner also sought a writ of prohibition to prevent a hearing on defendants' motions to require petitioner to furnish security.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner shareholder filed a shareholders' derivative suit against defendant corporation and officers. Defendants then filed a motion, pursuant to Cal. Corp. Code § 834, to require petitioner to furnish security for expenses. At the same time, petitioner gave notices and served subpoenas on some defendants and corporate officers. Respondent trial court stayed further proceedings on the depositions until after a hearing on defendants' motions. Petitioner then sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent to allow the depositions to proceed, and also sought a writ of prohibition preventing respondent from holding a hearing on the motions for security. While the petitions were being considered, the trial court granted the motions. The court denied the writs, holding that a writ of prohibition ordinarily issued only to prevent future judicial acts rather than to undo acts already performed. The right of a corporation to require a shareholder to give reasonable expenses was absolute. Petitioner was not entitled to take depositions because it constituted a step in the prosecution of the action, and he was required to first give security.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner shareholder's petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent trial court to enforce petitioner's claim that he had the right to take depositions of individuals and officers in connection with his derivative stockholders' suit. The court also discharged petitioner's writ of prohibition seeking the prevention of the trial court from proceeding with a hearing on motions to require petitioner to furnish security.

 

455 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Armenta v. Churchill


L. A. No. 22902


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 2d 448; 267 P.2d 303; 1954 Cal. LEXIS 183



March 5, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. Benjamin J. Scheinman, Judge.

Action for damages for wrongful death.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed; appeal from order dismissed. Judgment for defendants reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the widow and surviving children of the decedent, brought a cause of action against defendant alleged tortfeasor to recover damages for the decedent's wrongful death. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered a judgment in favor of the alleged tortfeasor. The survivors sought review of the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The decedent was killed while working on a road-paving job when a dump truck backed over him. The truck was being driven by the alleged tortfeasor and was owned by the alleged tortfeasor's wife. The survivors contended that the trial court committed prejudicial error in instructing the jury and in excluding certain evidence. On appeal, the supreme court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The supreme court found that the trial court erred in refusing to give certain instructions to the jury proffered by the survivors. Cal. Admin. Code tit. 7 § 1753(b) provided that a dump truck was required to be equipped with a horn that was sounded whenever the truck was being operated in reverse. The safety order should have been read to the jury because it established a minimum standard of care in the safe operation of the alleged tortfeasor's truck. The trial court also erred when it failed to instruct the jury as to Cal. Veh. Code § 671(b), which provided that a person was not permitted to back up a vehicle until such movement could be performed with reasonable safety.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

 

456 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Pickens v. Johnson


Sac. No. 6378


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 2d 399; 267 P.2d 801; 1954 Cal. LEXIS 179



March 1, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. James O. Moncur, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Consolidated actions for declaratory relief, and for damages for forcible entry and unlawful detainer.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments for respondents affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lessors filed an action in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), seeking declaratory relief involving their rights under a contract of conditional sale of the business and equipment on the premises leased to respondent lessees. The lessees also filed an action for damages arising from the forcible entry and unlawful detainer of the premises. Judgment was entered in favor of the lessees, and the lessors appealed.


OVERVIEW: In the course of operating a business, the lessees incurred obligations to third persons for which an action was commenced against them in court, and an attachment was issued against "goods, wares and merchandise" of the lessees located on the leased property. The lessors claimed that the lessees had violated the lease because they incurred the obligations on which the attachment was based in the lessors' name and credit, that the agreement was no longer in effect, and that the lessors were entitled to regain possession of the premises and business. In the second action, the lessees claimed damages from the lessors for ousting and excluding them from possession of the premises and business, asserting no breach of the lease or agreement. The court found that there had been no breach of the lease or agreement, that the merchandise was not bought in the lessors' name or on their credit, that the levy of the attachment did not breach the lease, that the lessors ousted and excluded the lessees from possession without right, that the lessees had not abandoned the premises or their rights under the lease, and that the lessors were liable in damages to the lessees for their conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court in favor of the lessees.

 

457 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ashley


Crim. No. 5421


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 2d 246; 267 P.2d 271; 1954 Cal. LEXIS 171



February 19, 1954 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 17, 1954. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. William B. Neeley, Judge.

Prosecution for grand theft.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found him guilty of grand theft under Cal. Penal Code §484 and denied his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of grand theft under Cal. Penal Code §484. Defendant had induced his victims to loan him money based on promises that they would be made part of his company and that the money would go to the construction of a theater. In fact, the money loaned was used to alleviate the financial problems affecting defendant's business. On appeal, defendant argued that the evidence did not support the conviction of theft and that the trial court erred in denying his motion for a new trial. The court held that a promise made without intention to perform was a misrepresentation of a state of mind and thus a misrepresentation of existing fact and was a false pretense within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code §484. As such, the evidence justified the finding that defendant had acquired the money with a felonious intent. The trial judge did not fail in safeguarding defendant's rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction because there was sufficient evidence to justify the finding that defendant had acquired the money through false pretenses within the meaning of the penal code.

OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's entry of judgment.

Beyerbach v. Juno Oil Co.


L. A. No. 22341


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 2d 11; 265 P.2d 1; 1954 Cal. LEXIS 154



January 5, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County. Frank Lamberson, Judge.

Stockholder's derivative action to compel individual defendants to transfer an oil lease to defendant corporation, and for an accounting.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff stockholder brought a stockholder's derivative action to compel defendants, two stockholders to transfer an oil lease to defendant oil company and to account to the oil company for the rents and royalties. The Superior Court of Tulare County (California) dismissed the action because the stockholder failed to furnish security for reasonable expenses incurred by defendants' in connection with the action. The stockholder appealed.


OVERVIEW: The stockholder and the two stockholders had orally agreed that the oil company would be organized to operate oil leases acquired by the stockholder and two stockholders and transferred to the oil company in consideration of stock. A certain oil lease was not transferred as agreed to. The court found that Cal. Corp. Code § 834 was not unconstitutional because so long as the basis of a classification was reasonable, the legislature could provide in various types of action that a successful plaintiff could recover attorneys' fees or only a defendant could appeal. The court found that there was no reasonable probability that the action would benefit the corporation. The court found that the amount of security was not unreasonable and beyond the range of judicial discretion. Under § 834 the trial court, within its sound discretion had the authority to fix the nature and amount of the security. The court held that when the stockholder refused to deposit the security for the corporation, the action could not continue against that indispensable party, or in its absence against any other party, and the trial court had no alternative but to dismiss as to all defendants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of dismissal.

McCarthy v. Manhattan Beach


L. A. No. 22507


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 2d 879; 264 P.2d 932; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 338



December 30, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 27, 1954. Shenk, J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Arnold Praeger, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developers sought declaratory judgment that certain zoning ordinances restricting use of their ocean-front property was void. The trial court decided in favor of defendant city, and the developers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The developers contended that the trial judge's on-site observations were improper and that the zoning restriction went beyond mere regulation and constituted an unwarranted interference with the land use. The court held that (1) the ordinance in question involved a city-wide zoning plan, (2) the trial judge's view of the property was admissible evidence, (3) the zoning restriction for beach recreational purposes was a legitimate exercise of the city's police power in the interests of public health, safety, morals and general welfare, and (4) the fact that the developers would suffer some financial detriment did not require invalidation of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment upholding the validity of the city's zoning ordinance that restricted development of the beach.

 

461 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Dixon


Crim. No. 5171


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 2d 756; 264 P.2d 513; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 327



December 15, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to secure release from custody, contending that real evidence used to convict him for a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 480 was obtained by unlawful search and seizure and that his confession, which was received in evidence, was obtained by coercion.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted and sentenced for possession of counterfeiting apparatus, and no appeal was taken from the judgment. He then sought a writ of habeas corpus, which the court denied. It was apparent from the record as a whole that the matters of which petitioner complained were before the trial court and that there was evidence which would have supported findings that there was no violation of his constitutional rights in connection with his confession or the search and seizure. He was not entitled in this proceeding to a consideration of claims which were based upon his version of the conflicting evidence and which could have been, but were not, raised on appeal. Likewise, petitioner's contentions that irrelevant evidence was erroneously admitted at his trial and that the prosecution was guilty of misconduct clearly could have been dealt with upon a timely appeal from the judgment of conviction, and such matters were not reviewable in this proceeding. Further, Cal. Penal Code § 480 was constitutional because the Congress did not intend to exclude state legislation from the field of punishing counterfeiting.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause and denied the writ requested by petitioner.

People v. Building Maintenance Contractors' Asso.


S. F. No. 18345


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 2d 719; 264 P.2d 31; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 324



December 11, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco granting a new trial. Sylvain J. Lazarus, Judge.

Action to enjoin alleged violations of Cartwright Act ( Bus. & Prof. Code, §§ 16700- 16758).



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Order granting defendants a new trial following a judgment for plaintiff, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco grant of new trial after jury had returned a verdict against defendants to enjoin them from alleged violations of the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 16700- 16758.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were a group of maintenance contractors in San Francisco who provided local buildings with services such as window cleaning, janitorial work, elevator operators, and night guards. Defendants agreed to fix prices at which maintenance work was provided and to prevent competition among themselves by forcing customers to pay higher prices if customers sought to change maintenance contractors. Plaintiff, the State of California, contended that such practices violated the Cartwright Act (Act), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 16700- 16758. A jury agreed; however, court granted defendants a new trial and plaintiff appealed. Court reversed, holding defendants clearly had achieved price fixing, which was illegal at both common law and according to the Act. Court further held that the provision of the Act, § 16723, on which defendants relied stating agreements securing "reasonable profits" were not illegal was too vague to be given effect and that defendants' additional arguments had no merit.


OUTCOME: Grant of a new trial to defendants reversed because defendants clearly had achieved price fixing, which was prohibited by both common law and under the Cartwright Act, and the provision on which defendants relied, stating agreements to secure "reasonable prices" not illegal, was too vague to be given effect.

Safeway Stores v. Retail Clerks Int'l Ass'n


S. F. No. 18481


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 2d 567; 261 P.2d 721; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 305; 33 L.R.R.M. 2049; 24 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P67,889



October 16, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 12, 1953. Gibson, C. J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Ralph E. Hoyt, Judge.

Action to enjoin strike activities of certain unions.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment granting preliminary injunction, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant unions sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which after a hearing on the order to show cause granted a preliminary injunction in favor of respondent store.


OVERVIEW: The store brought an action to enjoin the strike activities of the unions, who went on strike to protest the refusal of the store to include store managers in the contract or to recognize the clerks' unions as the representatives of the store managers. The court held that the store managers were agents of management, and owed undivided loyalty to their principal. The court held that as members of the unions they would under union rules be in duty bound to advance the cause of the community of interest of store managers and clerks in any dispute or disagreement with their principal. The court held that it was eminently proper that management supervisors, be kept free from the divided loyalty that would be engendered by compulsory membership in the local unions. The court held that the trial court was correct in deciding that the coercion sought to be exercised by the unions under the circumstances of the case was not reasonably related to any legitimate interest of organized labor; that the activities of the defendants were not in the furtherance of any proper labor objective, and that as a matter of sound public policy were enjoinable within the equity jurisdiction of the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which granted a preliminary injunction in favor of the store.

 

464 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Wotton v. Bush


L. A. No. 22287


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 2d 460; 261 P.2d 256; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 292; 3 Oil & Gas Rep. 12



September 18, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Jesse J. Frampton, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff oil producer sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment for defendant State Oil and Gas Supervisor. The oil producer sought a declaratory judgment that its real property was located within a designated oil field, thus could be drilled and placed into production without complying with the spacing provisions of Cal. Pub. Res. Code. § 3, div. 3.


OVERVIEW: The oil producer filed an action to declare that its property lay within field that was producing oil or gas on a designated date so that the property would be excepted from the Oil Well Spacing Act (Act), by virtue of an exception found at Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 3605. When the trial court held that the property was not within the designated field, the oil producer contended the statutory exception was unconstitutionally vague and that it improperly delegated legislative power to the California Division of Oil and Gas (division). On appeal, the court affirmed because the statute was constitutional. The court found that the statute was not void for vagueness, even though the legislature had not statutorily defined the term "field" used in the exception, because the term had an understandable meaning in the industry. The court found that there was no improper delegation of power because the legislature had evidenced its approval of the division's interpretation and its intent that the division's interpretation would continue to serve as a standard for administrative review by the division when it reenacted § 3605 with no changes during a reenactment of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's finding that the oil producer's property was not within the designated field and that the oil producer must comply with statutory spacing regulations.

Southern Pacific Co. v. Public Utilities Com.


S. F. No. 18667


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 2d 354; 260 P.2d 70; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 281



August 14, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied September 10, 1953. Edmonds, J., and Schauer, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review an order of the Public Utilities Commission requiring petitioner to discontinue midday passenger trains from San Francisco to Sacramento by use of steam locomotives and substitute in lieu thereof modern self-propelled railway passenger cars, and to make a study and submit to commission plans for automotive passenger service between San Francisco and its East Bay stations and for modernization of its Oakland Pier passenger terminal facilities.

OVERVIEW: The train company sought approval of its proposal to discontinue the operation of its midday passenger trains, which ran between Oakland Pier and Sacramento. 466 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Daugherty


Crim. No. 5366


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 876; 256 P.2d 911; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 242



May 5, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing and Modification was Denied May 28, 1953.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239) from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sonoma County and from an order denying a new trial. Donald Geary, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction of first degree murder, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction of first degree murder and sentence of death handed down by the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California) after finding that defendant was sane and denying his request for a new trial after the verdict.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was tried for the murder of his wife, with whom he had years of constant turmoil. He had repeatedly accused her of infidelity and even hit her on occasion. He began to drink heavily and continue to fight with her until the day he killed her. She had been stripped, kicked, stabbed, and dragged by the time she was found by a neighbor. She died soon thereafter and defendant was found in bloody clothes. At trial, he did not contest that he killed her, but claimed that he was so deranged by alcohol and illness that he was incapable of forming an intent or of premeditation necessary for the first-degree murder charge. After a guilty verdict, defendant was sentenced to death by lethal injection. The conviction and sentence were affirmed on appeal when the court found that such method of execution was not cruel and unusual. The court also held that the jury was properly instructed as to the issue of sanity, therefore their finding that defendant was sane would not be overturned. Further, the court held that defendant was able to distinguish right from wrong and that the intent with which defendant killed his wife established the requisite torture for the conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction murder and sentence of death.

 

467 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Malone v. Superior Court of San Francisco


S. F. No. 18752


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 546; 254 P.2d 517; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 216



March 17, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in certiorari to review orders directing perpetuation of testimony and issuance of subpoenae duces tecum.



DISPOSITION: Portion of order covering issue of legality of appointment of additional members of Democratic County Central Committee in San Francisco, annulled, together with all other issues raised, except those pertaining to illegal expenditure and concealment of funds, as to which the order was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Applicant political party member filed an application for an order directing perpetuation of testimony and issuance of subpoenas duces tecum. The trial court (California) granted the political party member's application and denied respondent witness' motion to vacate the order; the witnesses appealed.


OVERVIEW: The political party member alleged that he expected to be a party plaintiff in an action involving the illegal appointment of persons to the political party, the illegal conduct of the political party's business, the illegal handling of expenditures and funds of the political party, and the holding of illegal meetings of the political party. The political party member filed an application to perpetuate testimony and obtain issuance of subpoenas under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 2083-2089, and his application was granted. The court affirmed in part and annulled in part the order granting the application. The court held that there was sufficient proof that the political party member would be entitled to relief with respect to his claims regarding the mismanagement of political party funds and expenditures. However, there was no proof the political party member would be entitled to relief on his other anticipated claims. The court therefore annulled the portion of the order requiring the perpetuation of testimony and issuance of subpoenas as to issues other than the illegal expenditure and concealment of funds, and it affirmed the portion of the order relating to such claims.


OUTCOME: The portion of the order granting the political party member's application for perpetuation of testimony and issuance of subpoenas regarding the illegal expenditure and concealment of political party funds was affirmed; the remainder of the order relating to all other claims was annulled.

 

468 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Kelleher


Crim. Nos. 5264, 5265


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 424; 254 P.2d 572; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 204; 31 L.R.R.M. 2542; 23 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P67,452



March 10, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied April 2, 1953. Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees filed writs of habeas corpus seeking release from custody resulting from their arrest for picketing in violation of a temporary restraining order issued by the superior court (California).


OVERVIEW: The employees were members of a labor union. Their labor union and another labor union were involved in a dispute regarding which union was entitled to represent the employer's employees. The employees were picketing in front of the employer's place of business. The employer sought and obtained an injunction enjoining the employees from picketing. The employees continued to picket, so they were arrested. The employees filed writs of habeas corpus to secure their release. The court denied the writs. The court found that the Jurisdictional Strike Law applied because the dispute was between two labor organizations involved in a dispute as to representation. The Strike Law afforded the employer the right to injunctive relief. The court found that the Strike Law did not violate the free speech, press, and assembly guaranties of the Constitution. Therefore, the employees were in contempt of a properly issued injunction and were not entitled to be released from jail.


OUTCOME: The court denied the employee's writs of habeas corpus.

 

469 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Seven Up Bottling Co. v. Grocery Drivers Union


L. A. No. 21347


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 368; 254 P.2d 544; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 200; 33 A.L.R.2d 327; 31 L.R.R.M. 2530; 23 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P67,450



March 10, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 2, 1953.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Archie D. Mitchell, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff beverage bottler brought an action against defendant labor unions in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), seeking injunctive relief and damages arising out of the unions' alleged activities in violation of the Jurisdictional Strike Act, Cal. Lab. Code §§1115-1120. The trial court entered a preliminary injunction in favor of the bottler, but then entered judgment for the unions. The bottler appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected the unions' contention that the Jurisdictional Strike Act interfered with its rights to free speech. The unions' activities thwarted the main purpose of the act to protect an employer against interference with his business and the public from disturbances resulting from a dispute between unions as to which should have the right to bargain collectively with the employer. The act specifically provided that a concerted refusal to work or concerted interference with an employer's business was against public policy when it arises out of a dispute between two labor organizations as to which should have the exclusive right to bargain collectively. Therefore, the unions' picketing to achieve unlawful ends, refusal to work, and interference with the employer's business was banned.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court in favor of the unions.

 

470 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Sommer v. Metal Trades Council


L. A. No. 21757


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 392; 254 P.2d 559; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 202; 32 L.R.R.M. 2004; 23 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P67,455



March 10, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 2, 1953. Gibson, C. J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County granting a preliminary injunction. W. Turney Fox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant union appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that plaintiff manufacturer was entitled to a preliminary injunction to stop union picketing and a secondary boycott sponsored by the union. The trial court relied upon the Jurisdictional Strike Act of California (Act), Cal. Lab. Code § 1115 et seq., to establish its jurisdiction in the matter.


OVERVIEW: There was a controversy between a local union that was affiliated with the manufacturer and the union. A disputed election occurred, and the union continued its peaceful picketing and a secondary boycott. The manufacturer filed an action in the trial court seeking damages and injunctive relief. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction. The court affirmed and held that the Act prohibited any concerted effort to interfere with an employer's business and that California's jurisdiction was not preempted by § 8(b)(4) of the National Labor Relations Act, 29 U.S.C.S. § 158, because there was no overlap between California law and federal law. Because federal law did not protect the union's activity, California had the power to regulate the limits of a permissible contest available to the industrial combatants. The local union had preliminarily established that it was qualified and that the employees of the manufacturer had joined the local union voluntarily. Therefore, the manufacturer was entitled to preliminary injunctive relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which granted the manufacturer's request for a preliminary injunction against the union.

French Art Cleaners v. State Board of Dry Cleaners


S. F. No. 18570


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 459; 254 P.2d 42; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 207



March 10, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 2, 1953. Gibson, C. J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Edward Molkenbuhr, Judge.

Actions for injunctive relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments for plaintiffs affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State Board of Dry Cleaners and others, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which entered judgment in favor of plaintiff corporations, enjoined the Board from enforcing the price fixing portions of the Dry Cleaners' Act of 1945 embodied in Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 9560 through 9567 and declared those sections unconstitutional.
