The corporations initiated the action to enjoin the enforcement of the of the price fixing provisions on the ground that the price fixing provisions were invalid. The Board sought injunctive relief in its cross-complaints to prevent the corporations from violating the order and the minimum price schedules fixed by the Board. The Board also sought to restrain the corporations from alleged violations of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code ch. 4, § 7(2) based on allegations that the corporations' advertising methods was misleading the public. The trial court granted the corporations requested relief and denied the Board's cross-complaints. On appeal, the court upheld the trial court's judgment. State Board of Dry Cleaners v. Thrift-D-Lux Cleaners, Inc.


L. A. No. 21666


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 436; 254 P.2d 29; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 206



March 10, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 2, 1953. Gibson, C. J., Carter, J., and Traynor, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Clarence M. Hanson, Judge.

Action to enjoin violations of Dry Cleaners' Act of 1945.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment dismissing action on sustaining demurrer to amended complaint without leave to amend, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff board of dry cleaners (board) challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed the board's action for injunctive relief against defendant dry cleaning business for alleged violations of the Dry Cleaners' Act of 1945 (Act), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code ch. 18, art. 5, §§ 9560-9567. The trial court sustained the dry cleaning business' general demurrer to the amended complaint.


OVERVIEW: The Act provided for the creation of the board that could establish minimum price schedules for dry cleaning services. The dry cleaning business charged a price below the board's established minimum price. The board filed a complaint charging the dry cleaning business with violations of the price schedule. The dry cleaning business sought a demurrer based on the ground that the minimum price provisions of the Act were in violation of the due process clauses of the state and federal constitutions. The trial court sustained the demurrer and dismissed the action. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the price fixing provision of the Act was invalid because it was not, by any recognized or recognizable standard, an enactment that provided for the public health, safety, morals, or general welfare. Because the Act conferred legislative authority upon a board composed of members who were directly interested in the operation of the regulatory rule and its penal provisions without a guide for the exercise of the delegated authority, the court found that the board and the price schedule violated the due process clauses of the state and federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the board's action for injunctive relief, and the trial court's sustaining of the dry cleaning business' demurrer in the board's action for injunctive relief against the dry cleaning business for violations of the Act.

Simpson v. Los Angeles


L. A. No. 21945


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 271; 253 P.2d 464; 1953 Cal. LEXIS 191



February 20, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 19, 1953. Carter, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Clarence M. Hanson, Judge.

Action to restrain enforcement of a city ordinance relating to surrender of unclaimed impounded animals for purpose of medical research.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, residents, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that respondents, municipality and medical research corporation, could enforce a city ordinance relating to the surrender of unclaimed impounded animals for medical research.


OVERVIEW: The residents filed suit against the municipality and medical research corporation seeking to prevent them from effectuating a city ordinance that provided for the Department of Animal Regulation's surrender of unclaimed animals that had been impounded for five days to institutions for humane use for medical research. The trial court found against residents, who appealed. The court held that a city, under its police power, could enact local measures that did not conflict with general statutes. The court found that the ordinance did not conflict with state laws. The court held that the ordinance specifically provided that the research institutions receiving dogs were certified as organizations which would use them humanely for the good of mankind and the increase of knowledge relating to the cause, prevention, control, and cure of disease. The court held that the ordinance afforded adequate notice to an animal owner, whose identity was known, to claim the animal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the municipality and medical research corporation could enforce the city ordinance relating to the surrender of unclaimed impounded animals for medical research.

Stout v. Democratic County Cent. Committee


S. F. No. 18702


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 2d 91; 251 P.2d 321; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 168



December 23, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Democratic County Central Committee of San Francisco to revoke appointments of additional committeemen.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner elected officials sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondents, Democratic County Central Committee of San Francisco and its members, to revoke appointments of additional committeemen.


OVERVIEW: After the primary election, 25 persons were appointed by a majority of the committee to serve as additional committee members pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 2833. The elected members from the primary charged that § 2833 violated various provisions of the Constitution forbidding local and special laws, Cal. Const. art. I, § 11; art. IV, § 25(9). They further asserted that the real parties in interest, 25 elected persons, were unlawfully holding the position of committeemen and requested that the committee be ordered by a writ of mandate to revoke their appointment. The committee claimed that that quo warranto was the proper remedy and that § 2833 was valid. The court held mandamus was the proper remedy to determine whether the office claimed by a person legally existed. Title to office may be incidentally determined in mandamus. The court concluded that there was no rational basis for the classification of city and county for apportionment of committeemen in § 2833. Thus, the italicized portion of that section was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ.

Serve Yourself Gasoline Stations Asso. v. Brock


L. A. No. 21968


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 2d 813; 249 P.2d 545; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 308



October 31, 1952 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 25, 1952. Edmonds, J., did not Participate Therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Julius V. Patrosso, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, gas station owners and operators, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared, in favor of appellee Director of the Department of Agriculture, that statutes regulating price advertising on or near gasoline stations, were constitutional and were applicable to appellants' price signs.


OVERVIEW: Owners and operators contended that the statutes in question involved an improper exercise of the police power, depriving them of their property without due process of law. The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the regulation of price advertising was a proper exercise of the police power. The court held that in reviewing legislation for the purpose of testing its propriety as an exercise of the police power, the power of the court was limited to determining whether the subject of the legislation was within the state's power, and if so to determine whether the means adopted to accomplish the result were reasonably designed for that purpose, and had a real and substantial relation to the objects sought to be attained. The court concluded that it was apparent upon its face that the primary purpose of the legislation was the prevention of misleading advertising, which purpose had long been deemed a proper subject for the exercise of the police power. The court held that although the right to advertise was a property right, the requirement of full disclosure of price and brand name was a reasonable method of regulating gasoline advertising.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for the Director, finding that statutes regulating price advertising on or near gasoline stations were constitutional and were applicable to gas station owners and operators' price signs.

 

476 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Peterson v. Johnson


L. A. No. 22050


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 2d 745; 249 P.2d 17; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 301



October 21, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. A. A. Scott, Judge.

Action to quiet title.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiffs affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landowners challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which quieted plaintiff landowners' title to 80 acres of land in which defendants claimed an interest.


OVERVIEW: Before the state took title to the 80 acres, the land was assessed to one defendant as an undivided part of 120 acres owned by him. After defendant conveyed the entire parcel to his wife, who failed to pay the taxes, the property was placed on a delinquent tax list. Three years later, 40 acres of the land was conveyed by defendants to a third party, who in turn conveyed the acreage to plaintiffs. Plaintiffs then redeemed the 40 acres under the partial redemption statute, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 4146- 4155. When plaintiff applied for such redemption, the assessor placed a separate valuation on the part sought to be redeemed for each tax delinquent year. The remaining acres were valued at the difference between the original valuation and the valuation placed on the 40 acres. Thereafter, the state took title to the remaining 80 acres under a tax deed. Subsequently, plaintiffs bought the 80 acres from the state and then filed their action to quiet title to it. The court held that the proceedings during and after partial redemption were not void and that the assessor properly valued the properties. The court further held that the partial redemption statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

477 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Hirschman v. County of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 22035


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 2d 698; 249 P.2d 287; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 295



October 17, 1952 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied 39 Cal. 2d 698 at 703.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. W. Turney Fox, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel county civil service commission to set aside its decision upholding discharge of county employees for failure to sign an oath of office.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, permanent civil service employees, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied the employees a writ of mandamus to compel defendant county civil service commission to set aside its decision upholding the discharge of the employees for failure to sign an oath of office. The employees also sought a mandate to compel their reinstatement and the payment of retroactive wages.


OVERVIEW: The employees refused to take an oath to defend the government and to faithfully discharge their duties and refused to sign affidavits, stating that they were not members of certain political organizations. There was pending litigation as to part of the affidavit, dealing with membership in certain organizations. As such, the county asked the employees to sign the rest of the oath and affidavit. When the employees refused, they were discharged for insubordination. The court held that the employees were not required to speculate upon what the courts were to determine in the future and that, under the contested provisions, the employees were required to name only those organizations that they knew advocated the overthrow of the government with force. As such, the court held that the requirement was sufficiently certain to be understood and applied, and that the employees were properly required to furnish information as to memberships in organizations that, to their knowledge, had advocated the overthrow of the government. The court noted that the statements were similar to the oath prescribed by the Levering Act, Cal. Govt. Code §§ 3100-3109, which had been upheld in court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

478 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ericksen v. Southern Pacific Co.


S. F. No. 18478


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 2d 374; 246 P.2d 642; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 268



July 31, 1952 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 28, 1952. Edmonds, J., Schauer, J., and Spence, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Lile T. Jacks, Judge.

Action under Federal Employers' Liability Act for damages for personal injuries for failure to provide plaintiff with a safe place to work.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant railroad company sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which awarded plaintiff lumber inspector $ 18,000 damages. The lumber inspector had commenced an action against the railroad company under a provision of the Federal Employers' Liability Act (FELA), codified at 45 U.S.C.S. § 51, for the railroad company's alleged failure to provide him with a safe place to work.


OVERVIEW: After he injured his ankle at a job site, the lumber inspector filed an action against the railroad company under FELA for the railroad company's alleged failure to provide him with a safe place to work. The trial court entered a judgment on the verdict that awarded the lumber inspector $ 18,000 damages. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that (1) FELA did apply to the lumber inspector, as it was reasonable to conclude that his duties satisfied the requirement under § 51 that they were in furtherance of interstate commerce; (2) the railroad company's argument that its negligence was not established was without merit, as under the evidence the jury was entitled to conclude that the lumber inspector was required to perform his duties of a tie inspector in a place where the railroad company knew that there was danger because of the inconvenient location of the ties; and that (3) the verdict was not excessive in light of the lumber inspector's alleged contributory negligence, as there was no showing that the amount of damages fixed by the jury was the result of passion, prejudice, or corruption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's award of $ 18,000 damages to the lumber inspector in his action against the railroad company under FELA.

 

479 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. McCracken


Crim. No. 5291


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 2d 336; 246 P.2d 913; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 264



July 22, 1952 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 21, 1952. Edmonds, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239) from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County and from an order denying a new trial. Robert Gardner, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction of first degree murder, imposing death penalty, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted defendant of child stealing, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 278, and first degree murder, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 189. Defendant was sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the trial court committed prejudicial error in denying defendant's motion for a change of venue. The court held that under all the circumstances, it did not appear that the trial court abused its discretion in denying defendant's motion for a change of venue. The court held that although the type of crime involved was one that was generally condemned and received widespread publicity, there was no claim of threats or open acts of hostility shown against defendant by the people of the venue. Defendant next contended that if trial on the murder charge had been had before a court sitting without a jury, the trial court would have had to adjourn the proceeding after a finding of guilt until his status as a sexual psychopath had been determined. The court held that this argument was based on the erroneous assumption that sexual psychopathy was a mitigating circumstance. The court affirmed the convictions, holding that while the misconduct of the district attorney was serious, the court could not, in the face of the overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt, come to the conclusion that it was so prejudicial as to have caused a miscarriage of justice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for child stealing and first degree murder. The court affirmed the sentence of death.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Subsequent Injuries Fund of the State of California, sought review of an order from the Industrial Accident Commission (California), awarding compensation for personal injuries to respondent employee.


OVERVIEW: The fund was responsible, pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code §§ 4750-4755, for payment of special additional compensation to employees whose preexisting disabilities were aggravated by subsequent injuries. 482 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Sei Fujii v. State of California


L. A. No. 21149


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 2d 718; 242 P.2d 617; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 221



April 17, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Wilbur C. Curtis, Judge.

Action to determine whether certain realty deeded to plaintiff escheated to the state.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment declaring that property escheated to the state, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant alien resident sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), declaring that certain land purchased by him had escheated to respondent State under the California Alien Land Law, Cal. Gen. Laws, Act 261, §§ 1, 2, and 7 (1945).


OVERVIEW: The alien resident, an alien Japanese who was ineligible to citizenship under the naturalization laws, challenged a judgment that certain real property purchased by him had escheated to the State. He contended that the Alien Land Law had been invalidated by the U.N. Charter and that it violated the Fourteenth Amendment. The court reversed the lower court's judgment. The court held first that the U.N. Charter did not provide relief for the alien resident because it was not a self-executing treaty so as to supersede inconsistent state legislation as provided in U.S. Const. art VI. However, the court determined that the Alien Land Law violated the Due Process and Equal Protection Clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment. The court held that the statutory classification of aliens on the basis of eligibility to citizenship was suspect because it in fact classified on the basis of race or nationality. Applying a "most rigid scrutiny" standard of review, the court found that the legislation was not reasonably related to any legitimate governmental interest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment declaring that the property escheated to the State.

 

483 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Parks v. Superior Court of Alameda County


S. F. No. 18524


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 2d 609; 241 P.2d 521; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 208



March 18, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Alameda County, and Charles Wade Snook, Judge thereof, from taking further proceedings on an information filed against petitioner.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner criminal filed a writ in prohibition to restrain respondents, Superior Court of Alameda County (California) and judge, from taking further proceedings on an information filed against the criminal. The criminal was charged with two counts of theft and with writing a bad check in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 476a. The magistrate dismissed the two theft counts, but the district attorney filed an information charging all three offenses.


OVERVIEW: The criminal filed a motion with the superior court for an order setting aside the information pursuant to § 995 on the grounds that he had not been legally committed, and that he had been committed without reasonable or probable cause. The superior court denied the motion, and the criminal filed a writ of prohibition with the court. The court granted the writ and restrained the superior court from taking any action with regard to the charges of theft only. The court found that one charge of theft was not connected to the other charges, and that there had been no commitment order. The court held that the district attorney did not have the power to file an information as to that theft charge under § 739, and it was contrary to the protection afforded by Cal. Const. art. I, § 8. However, the court found that a commitment order had been ordered with regard to the other two charges, which arose out of the same transaction. The court held that § 8 and Cal. Penal Code § 739, protected the criminal from trial on those charges until probable cause was established. The court ruled that the evidence at the preliminary hearing failed to show probable cause for the second charge of theft.


OUTCOME: The court granted the criminal's writ for prohibition and restrained the superior court from taking any proceedings against the criminal on either of the charges of theft contained in the information, but allowed the superior court to proceed with regard to the bad check charge.

 

484 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Dorsey v. Barba


S. F. No. 18369


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 2d 350; 240 P.2d 604; 1952 Cal. LEXIS 179



February 4, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Donald K. Quayle, Judge.

Action for damages for personal injuries sustained in an automobile accident.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendant Catherine Barba and against defendant Vincent Barba, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, tort victims, appealed the judgments of the superior court of Alameda County (California) which denied plaintiffs', tort feasors, motion for a new trial modified the judgment against defendant, tort feasor husband, and entered a jury verdict in favor of defendant, tort feasor wife, in plaintiffs' action for damages related to personal injuries sustained in an automobile accident caused by defendant, tort feasor husband.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, tort victims, challenged the authority of the trial court to deny its motion for a new trial in the subsequent modification of the judgment against defendant, tort feasor husband, when it increased the amount of damages that defendant, tort feasor husband, was required to pay plaintiffs. Plaintiffs additionally challenged the trial court's entry of the jury verdict in favor of defendant, tort feasor wife, in plaintiffs' action for damages sustained in a car accident. The court reversed the judgment for defendant, tort feasor wife, and against defendant, tort feasor husband. Defendant, tort feasor wife, was not able to avoid liability for damages resulting from the accident, as she was the sole registered owner of the automobile and she was unable to show that she had not consented to use of the vehicle by defendant, tort feasor husband. The trial court's assessment of damages was an invasion of plaintiffs' right to a jury determination on the issue of damages, as the assessment was speculative and uncertain. As the assessment of damages was speculative and uncertain the court would only have been allowed to modify the judgment with the assent of both parties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for defendant, tort feasor wife, and against defendant, tort feasor husband, as defendant, tort feasor wife, was liable as a matter of law for any injuries resulting from the negligent operation of her car by defendant, tort feasor husband, since she was the owner of the vehicle. Additionally, the trial court improperly modified plaintiffs', tort feasors, award without their assent.

 

485 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Kimler


Crim. No. 5046


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 2d 568; 233 P.2d 902; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 311



July 13, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate brought a habeas corpus proceeding to secure his release from custody of respondent, the State of California. He was originally convicted in California then released upon parole to the custody of the State of Illinois. Upon release therefrom, he was arrested in Missouri upon a warrant but was released upon a writ of habeas corpus. He was arrested and imprisoned in California under the prior judgments of conviction.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was released on parole in California to the custody of officers in Illinois to complete a term of imprisonment there on the condition that, upon his release from imprisonment in Illinois, he would report to a parole officer in California. After failing to fulfill his condition, the inmate was picked up in Missouri, where he resisted extradition. He obtained release upon a writ of habeas corpus. He contended that the judgment in the Missouri proceeding was a final determination that the action of the State of California in releasing him to Illinois was a pardon. The court denied the writ application. The Missouri court did not purport to determine the effect of the inmate's release in California. The only fact determined there was that the circumstances were not sufficient to justify extradition. The Missouri court did not have jurisdiction to determine the merits of the release in California. The evidence supported a finding that the inmate went to the Illinois prison voluntarily under a conditional parole and that the jurisdiction of the State of California over him continued. When the inmate accept parole, he was bound by the express terms thereof.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause against the State of California and the inmate's application for a writ of habeas corpus to secure his release from prison was denied.

 

486 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Knudsen Creamery Co. v. Brock


L. A. No. 21422


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 2d 485; 234 P.2d 26; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 301



June 29, 1951 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 26, 1951. Edmonds, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Wilbur C. Curtis, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff milk distributor appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denying plaintiff declaratory relief when plaintiff sought to have Order 26 from defendant State Director of Agriculture affecting the minimum price schedule for milk annulled.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff creamery processed its milk in one location, then shipped it to a milk depot at a second location for storage and distribution. When defendant Director of Agriculture issued Order 26, which allowed distributors who processed their milk within the marketing area where the plaintiff's milk depot was located to deduct transportation costs from the regular minimum price they had to pay as required by the Milk Control Act, Cal. Agric. Code div. 4, ch. 10, plaintiff challenged the validity of the order. Plaintiff claimed that the order was arbitrary, unreasonable, and unconstitutional. The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff's request for declaratory relief, finding that the order issued by defendant did not violate plaintiff's equal protection rights as the order applied to all milk distributors within the state. The court also held that plaintiff's economic losses were due to its choice not to process milk in the market area and the losses of plaintiff were not a barrier to the validity of the order which was a valid exercise of the police power. The order was a reasonable regulation effecting the marketing of milk within the state.


OUTCOME: Judgment denying plaintiff declaratory relief affirmed because the disputed order was within the power and jurisdiction of the defendant and the order was not a violation of plaintiff's right to equal protection.

 

487 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


King v. Mortimer


S. F. Nos. 18331, 18332


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 2d 430; 233 P.2d 4; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 297



June 28, 1951 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 26, 1951. Carter, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Edward P. Murphy, Judge.

Action to set aside sales of certificates of building and loan association to association and for reinstatement of sellers as certificate holders, or for damages.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants on sustaining demurrer to complaint without leave to amend, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant certificate holders filed an amended complaint against respondents, association and building and loan commissioner (commissioner), in the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), alleging illegal acts and fraud in the sale of investment certificates. The trial court granted the association and commissioner's demurrer to the certificate holders amended complaint. The certificate holders appealed.


OVERVIEW: The association and commissioner claimed the facts alleged in the amended complaint were not sufficient to state a cause of action. The certificate holders claimed the purchase of the certificates was void as contracts of waiver and they had the right to recover damages. The court held that there were no contracts of waiver and no triable issue of fact. The purchase of the certificates was made on an open market and no contracts of waiver were alleged. Even if there was a violation of the Building and Loan Association Act, 1931 Cal. Stat. § 6.02, the purchases were not rendered invalid and the transactions were not void. The certificate holders failed to show they were entitled to the equitable remedy of rescission because there was no matured claim or debt due to the assignors at the time they sold their certificates to the association and there were no facts to show the difference between the price at which the certificates were sold and any greater amount which the market would have brought but for the alleged fraudulent conduct. Neither the cause for rescission or for damages could be premised on an assumption that the certificates had an undisputed value in their face amount.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court granting the association and commissioner's demurrer's to the amended complaint was affirmed.

Dribin v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L. A. No. 21731


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 2d 345; 231 P.2d 809; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 290; 24 A.L.R.2d 864



May 29, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel superior court to restore to trial calendar, and proceed with trial of, a divorce suit.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner husband brought an action against respondent, the Superior Court (superior court) of Los Angeles County (California) for a writ of mandamus to compel the superior court to restore to its calendar his divorce proceeding brought under Cal. Civ. Code §§ 92 and 108 on the ground of his wife's incurable insanity.


OVERVIEW: The husband filed a divorce complaint that alleged that his wife was adjudged insane in another state court. The husband served the complaint on the wife's guardian and offered into evidence a deposition that stated that the wife was still insane and was an inmate at an out-of-state hospital for the insane. The trial court held that the divorce complaint failed to comply with Cal. Civ. Code § 108 because it did not allege that the husband had a reasonable ability to support the wife. The court discharged the alternative writ and denied the peremptory writ. The court held that the requirement of proof of ability or property sufficient to support the insane spouse constituted an arbitrary and unreasonable class discrimination that did not bear a substantial relation to the objectives of the statute in protecting the insane. The court found, however, that because the wife was confined to an institution outside of the state, a divorce could not be granted. The court held that the requirement that the confinement be within the state was not arbitrary given the court's control of the insane for their protection and welfare.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ and denied the peremptory writ.

People v. One 1941 Chevrolet Coupe


L. A. No. 21780


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 2d 283; 231 P.2d 832; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 285



May 25, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Philip H. Richards, Judge.

Proceeding for forfeiture of automobile used for illegal transportation of marihuana.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment ordering forfeiture, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant legal owner appealed a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ordered the forfeiture of the owner's automobile to plaintiff State of California in its forfeiture proceeding under Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11612, 11613.


OVERVIEW: The legal owner held title to a car. The legal owner lent money to the car's registered owner and received a chattel mortgage, which was duly filed for record and which was a first lien on the vehicle. Prior to making the loan, the legal owner made a reasonable investigation of the moral responsibility, character, and reputation of the registered owner. After seizing the car, the State filed suit against the legal and registered owners under Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11612, 11613, alleging that the vehicle had been used to unlawfully conceal, convey, carry or transport marihuana, and marijuana was unlawfully in the possession of an occupant. The trial court found that the car had been used contrary to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11610, and that the legal owner did not make a reasonable investigation of the registered owner. The legal owner appealed and the court reversed. The court held that the forfeiture action prescribed by California Health and Safety Code was in rem, that the effect of such a forfeiture was to transfer the title to the vehicle from the legal owner to the State, and that the legal owner had been entitled to trial by jury, which he did not receive.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment ordering forfeiture.

 

490 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Stewart v. McCollister


L. A. No. 21682


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 2d 203; 231 P.2d 48; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 277



May 15, 1951 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 14, 1951. Edmonds, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Robert H. Scott, Judge.

Action for damages for personal injuries arising out of an automobile collision.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for defendant reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured party sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), entered in favor of defendant driver in the suit for damages for personal injuries arising out of an automobile collision.


OVERVIEW: The driver alleged that at the time of the accident he was employed by the city, and he was acting within the course of his employment. He argued that the injured party did not present or file any verified claim within 90 days after the accident occurred pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 1981. The trial court agreed with the special defense and concluded that the injured party was not entitled to a trial on the merits. Judgment was entered for the driver and the injured party appealed. The court reversed with directions to the trial court to try the cause upon the merits. When the driver filed an answer to the complaint he did not allege that he was a public employee and did not raise any defense relating to the failure to file a verified claim. He filed an amended answer disclosing the special defense more than 15 months after the accident. The legislature, in enacting § 1981, qualified the common law right of action against the negligent person. The legislature required the filing of verified claim only in the event that the injured person claimed that he was injured as the result of the negligence of a public employee occurring during the course of his employment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment entered in favor of the driver in the personal injury suit.

 

491 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Dean v. Kuchel


Sac. No. 6186


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 2d 97; 230 P.2d 811; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 264



May 4, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel State Controller to draw warrants for payment of salaries of employees of Wildlife Conservation Board.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Department of Finance sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondent state controller to draw warrants for the payment of salaries to employees of the Wildlife Conservation Board (board).


OVERVIEW: The Wildlife Conservation Act (Act), 1947 Cal. Stat. 1325, created the board to study and determine the areas in the state most suitable for the preservation of wildlife and gave it the power to authorize the acquisition of such areas by the Fish and Game Commission (commission). The state controller claimed that the Act was unconstitutional because pursuant to Cal. Const. art. IV, § 25 1/2, the legislature had no authority to delegate to anyone except the commission any powers relating to the preservation of wildlife. The court issued the writ to compel payment because there was no suggestion that the amendment was intended to prohibit the legislature from exercising other powers not delegated. The policies and principles behind the amendment's adoption indicated that the legislature was not to be restricted to acting through the commission in legislating the wildlife field. The constitution was not a grant of power, but was a limitation upon the legislature's powers. Any doubt of the legislature's power to act was to be resolved in the legislature's favor.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ of mandamus to compel the state controller to draw warrants to pay the salaries of the Wildlife Conservation Board's employees.

Pridonoff v. Balokovich


L. A. No. 21512


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 788; 228 P.2d 6; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 227



March 5, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. William B. McKesson, Judge.

Action for damages for libel.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of dismissal on sustaining demurrer to third amended complaint without leave to amend, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which dismissed his libel action after sustaining without leave to amend defendants' demurrer to plaintiff's third amended complaint.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed a libel action against defendants alleging that they caused an article to be published in a daily newspaper falsely stating that he was engaged in espionage for the United States and that he was recalled by the United States. The trial court dismissed plaintiff's action. On appeal, the court reversed. The article was libelous because it implied that because plaintiff was recalled by the United States and was unfit to serve as a spy for the United States, and therefore had a direct tendency to injure plaintiff in his occupation. Plaintiff was not entitled to recover general or exemplary damages under Cal. Civ. Code § 48a; however, his allegation of special damages was sufficiently specific, as he alleged that as a result of the article he lost his job with a specific employer for a specific time period, and that he was damaged in the amount of $ 5,000.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because the article defendants alleged caused to be punished was libelous and because plaintiff sufficiently alleged his claim for special damages.

Orloff v. Los Angeles Turf Club, Inc.


L. A. No. 20746


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 734; 227 P.2d 449; 1951 Cal. LEXIS 222



February 14, 1951 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 15, 1951. Edmonds, J., and Spence, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Charles S. Burnell, Judge.

Action for injunctive relief.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendants reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor defendant racetrack owners and denied the citizen's request for an injunction to restrain the racetrack owners from refusing to admit him to the racetrack. In an earlier appeal, the court reversed the trial court's dismissal entered on an order sustaining the racetrack owners' demurrer.


OVERVIEW: On several occasions, the racetrack owners refused to admit the citizen to their racetrack. The citizen sought an injunction to restrain the racetrack owners from refusing to admit him. The trial court denied the injunction. On appeal the court reversed, holding that the evidence was insufficient to justify the exclusion of the citizen from the racetrack. The court found that there was no evidence that the citizen while on the course was or ever had engaged in illegal activities or in an attempt to commit a crime. Under any proper construction and application of Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51-54, the mere suspicion, based on past conduct and alleged reputed activities off the course, or on unsubstantiated conversations held on the course with persons considered questionable, did not justify the ejection or exclusion of the citizen. The court found that it was not the legislative intent to vest authority in the racetrack owners to determine primarily and in every instance what persons or classes of persons were sufficiently moral to be admitted to the course and thus be permitted to engage in lawful gambling. The court noted that the objective of §§ 51-54 was the protection of the public.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court in favor of the citizen in his action for injunctive relief against the racetrack owners.

Franchise Tax Board v. Superior Court of Sacramento County


Sac. Nos. 6078, 6079, 6080


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 538; 225 P.2d 905; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 268



December 22, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's and Real Party's in Interest Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 18, 1951. Traynor, J., did not Participate Therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Sacramento County from enforcing its order to the Franchise Tax Board to permit inspection of tax returns filed by certain corporations.



DISPOSITION: Writs granted; petition to produce additional evidence denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In refund actions brought by national banks challenging assessments made by the franchise tax commissioner, respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) ordered the commissioner to make available to the banks the returns and related data filed by nearly 25,000 corporations. By this proceeding in prohibition, petitioner franchise tax board, as successor to the commissioner, sought to prevent the enforcement of those orders.


OVERVIEW: The banks filed separate actions for the refund of taxes paid by each of them under the Bank and Corporation Franchise Tax Act (Act), 1929 Cal. Stat. ch. 13, for the taxable year of 1943. The commissioner had conducted a hearing pursuant to the Act in order to fix the rate at which banks and financial corporations would be assessed for the taxable year. After the rate was set for banks and financial corporations, the banks sought to review the returns of nonfinancial corporations used by the commissioner in determining the banks' rate. The commissioner refused because the Act prohibited the disclosure of tax return information. The court granted the board's petition for writs of prohibition of the trial court's order to produce the returns, finding that the banks were not denied due process. The hearing held pursuant to legislative direction was a quasi-legislative function and not a quasi-judicial function that required a judicial type hearing. The court held that the Act permitted a hearing within the prescribed limits, among which was the confidential status accorded the returns of nonfinancial corporate taxpayers granted by the specific terms of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writs of prohibition requested by the franchise tax board and denied the banks' petition to produce additional evidence.

People v. One 1940 Ford V-8 Coupe


L. A. No. 21572


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 471; 224 P.2d 677; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 262



December 15, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Edward R. Brand, Judge.

Proceeding to forfeit automobile to state for violation of narcotics law.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for legal owner of vehicle, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of respondent bank in the state's action to forfeit defendant automobile pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11610.


OVERVIEW: The state argued that the trial court erred when it allowed the bank to introduce evidence to show that an investigation made at the time of the creation of the lien would have demonstrated that the vehicle owner was a person of moral responsibility and of good repute. The bank did not allege that any investigation was made pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11620. Section 11620 required a reasonable investigation prior to the creation of the lien as one of the elements which had to be proved before the court was permitted to release the bank's interest from the forfeiture. The court reversed the trial court's judgment. The court found that at the time the bank's interest was created, the bank was without knowledge that the vehicle was being used for the unlawful transportation or concealment of narcotics. However, the bank's interest in the vehicle was forfeited, along with that of the owner, because the required investigation was not made. The forfeiture statute afforded the bank, as the lien claimant, the opportunity to protect its interest and when the bank failed to do so, the bank could not complain that its constitutional rights were invaded by the forfeiture.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that was entered in favor of the bank in the state's forfeiture action.

Hollywood Turf Club v. Daugherty


L. A. No. 21166


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 352; 224 P.2d 359; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 247



November 17, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 14, 1950.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Clarence M. Hanson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel Commissioner of Corporations to issue a permit authorizing petitioner corporation to declare a stock dividend.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for petitioner reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner horse-racing corporation appealed from the verdict of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which reversed the decision of the defendant corporation commissioner denying its request to issue stock below the par value set in the regulation adopted pursuant to Cal. Corp. Code § 25511.


OVERVIEW: After the commissioner denied the corporation's request to issue stock below the regulation par value, the corporation requested a copy of the record, but then waited almost three months before obtaining it. Under Cal. Corp. Code § 25317, the decisions of the commissioner are subject to review in court if an appeal is filed within 60 days of the decision as stated in Cal. Corp. Code § 25318. Although under Cal. Gov't Code § 11523, the record for the appeal must be requested within 30 days after the decision and may be extended 5 days if there is a delay in the record. The trial court decided that the corporation commenced the appeal in a timely fashion and decided that Cal. Corp. Code § 25511 was unconstitutional and so reversed the commissioner's decision. On appeal, the court reversed and found that whichever time limit was applied, the corporation had far exceeded the time limits because of its own delay and so was time-barred from the appeal. The court also found that restrictions the regulations applied to horse-racing were legitimate under code sections and that the legislature had singled out horse-racing intentionally since all other gambling was banned.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court which reversed the commissioner's denial of the horse-racing corporation's request to issue stock below the regulated par-value.

Hunter v. Justice's Court of Centinela Township


L. A. No. 21652


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 315; 223 P.2d 465; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 242



November 1, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied November 30, 1950.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Justice's Court of Centinela Township and Bernard Lawler, Judge thereof, from proceeding with prosecution for violation of Pub. Resources Code, §§ 3600, 3608, relating to spacing of oil wells.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner filed an action for a writ of prohibition against respondent, Justice's Court of Centinela County, California (trial court), in a state prosecution against the owner for his violation of Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 3600, 3608, relating to spacing of oil wells.


OVERVIEW: A company obtained a state permit to drill for oil on certain property, which included the owner's small piece of property. Because of the permit, the owner began to drill for oil. The state prosecuted the owner for violations of §§ 3600, 3608 because the owner's wells were not properly spaced. The owner sought a petition for writ of prohibition against the trial court to prevent his prosecution on the grounds that §§ 3600, 3608 were invalid because they prevented him from drilling for oil on his own land. The court denied the petition and ruled for the trial court. The court reasoned that §§ 3600, 3608 were reasonable regulations to secure equitable distribution of the oil and production costs because they secured to the owner a royalty on the oil under at least as good of a situation as if the owner sought to perform his own drilling. The inapplicability of §§ 3600, 3608 to wells built before 1931 was reasonable because those drillers already spent resources on their wells that would have otherwise been lost. Portions of §§ 3600, 3608 that could have been invalid did not invalidate §§ 3600, 3608 because they did not apply to the owner.


OUTCOME: The court denied the owner's petition for a writ of prohibition against the trial court in a state prosecution against the owner for a violation of the law regarding the spacing of oil wells.

 

498 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Stevinson Water Dist. v. Roduner


Sac. Nos. 6020, 6021


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 264; 223 P.2d 209; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 236



October 27, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 20, 1950.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Merced County. Stanley Murray, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action to enjoin diversion of foreign water flowing in a creek.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments for plaintiffs affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant riparian owners appealed from the judgments of the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which granted the motions by plaintiffs, water district and utility company, for permanent injunctions preventing the riparian owners from diverting certain "foreign water" that was delivered into a creek.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs had a contract with an irrigation district that entitled them to take water diverted by the irrigation district into a creek. The riparian owners erected certain obstructions in the creek and diverted water for their irrigation of their own lands. Plaintiffs filed actions to enjoin the riparian owners from diverting water from the creek. The trial court granted permanent injunctions. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that plaintiffs had a contractual right to the water that was diverted into the creek. Nevertheless, the court concluded that to the extent that plaintiffs could not beneficially use the water delivered into the creek, they could not prevent its appropriation by anyone who could beneficially use it, including the riparian owners. The court determined that § 67 of the California Water District Act did not authorize the water district to capture or retain water in a natural stream when it had no use for the water and could not find a willing purchaser. The court ruled that because this error appeared only in the trial court's conclusions of law and was not repeated in the judgments, it did not require a reversal or modification.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments granting plaintiffs' motions and entering permanent injunctions against the riparian owners.

Southern California Jockey Club, Inc. v. California Horse Racing Board


L. A. No. 21238


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 167; 223 P.2d 1; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 226



October 18, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Proceeding in mandamus to compel Horse Racing Board to issue a license.



DISPOSITION: Judgment denying relief affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court (California), which upheld a decision of respondent state horse racing board denying the corporation's application for a horseracing track.


OVERVIEW: The trial court upheld the decision of the state horse racing board denying the corporation's application for a horseracing track. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding that the legislative grant of regulatory power to the board was valid because it was confirmed and ratified by a constitutional amendment. The court further held that the board's denial of the corporation's application was supported by competent evidence despite the admission of other improper evidence regarding the proposed track's affect on other area tracks. The court also held that because the board's decision concerned its discretionary powers to determine an applicant's fitness to engage in a business subject to regulation, the court would not interfere with the decision absent the board's abuse of discretion. Finally, the court held that because there was a factual basis for its decision, the board had not abused its discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment upholding the state horse racing board's denial of the corporation's application for a horseracing track.

Kramer v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L. A. No. 21455


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 159; 222 P.2d 874; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 225



October 17, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied November 13, 1950. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in certiorari to annul an order denying a motion to set aside a judgment decreeing distribution of an estate.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner devisees sought a writ of certiorari to annul the judgment of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied the devisees' motion to set aside a judgment distributing an estate.


OVERVIEW: In his will, the decedent left half his estate to his wife and half to the devisees, who were German nationals. The estate property was liquidated. The devisees did not appear in the probate proceedings. It his petition for distribution, the executor alleged that because no reciprocal agreement existed between the United States and Germany that would have permitted a U.S. citizen to inherit property in Germany, pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code § 259, the devisees were not entitled to a distribution. The trial court entered a distribution order that excluded the devisees. Within six months, the devisees filed a motion pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 473 to set aside the order of distribution due to mistake, inadvertence, surprise or excusable neglect. The devisees argued that they were surprised that the executor took a position contrary to their interests and were not aware of the provisions of Cal. Prob. Code § 259. The trial court denied the motion. The devisees sought a writ of certiorari. The court held that the trial court had discretion to enter the distribution order based on the evidence it had before it. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that denied the devisees' motion to set aside the order of distribution of the decedent's estate.

 

501 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Fox Bakersfield Theatre Corp. v. Bakersfield


L. A. No. 21399


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 136; 222 P.2d 879; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 220



October 6, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 2, 1950.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County. Frederick E. Stone. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action for declaratory relief, injunction and recovery of taxes paid under protest.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff theater owners appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which determined that a business tax levied by defendant city was valid.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city enacted an ordinance by voter initiative that imposed a tax on places of amusement or entertainment. The tax, which excluded pool halls where no admission was charged and charitable, religious, and educational organizations, was imposed in addition to an existing business license tax. Plaintiff theater owners sought a declaratory judgment that the tax constituted double taxation, was discriminatory, and was not validly enacted. The trial court determined that the tax was valid. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the mere fact that the state had imposed one excise under one act did not prevent the state from imposing another excise upon the same privilege for the same period. The court also held that it was within the legislature's power to separately classify places of amusement for the purpose of taxation. The court further held that the tax proposition as stated on the ballot was not misleading because the general purpose of the tax was adequately covered in the title and the detail on the ballot of the method of computing the tax was incidental and could be disregarded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that there was no prohibition upon imposing a business tax under one act and another tax under a separate act, that the tax did not discriminate, and that the tax initiative ballot was not fatally flawed.

 

502 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Clemons v. Los Angeles


L. A. No. 20873


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 2d 95; 222 P.2d 439; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 217



September 28, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. W. Turney Fox, Judge.

Action for declaratory and injunctive relief.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed; appeal from order dismissed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which upheld the validity of Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 12.21-C, limiting the subdivision of property to certain minimum lot requirements, in plaintiff's action for declaratory and injunctive relief from the enforcement of said municipal regulation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property owner brought an action, against defendant City of Los Angeles, for declaratory and injunctive relief from the enforcement of Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 12.21-C, against him, charging that it transcended the legitimate scope of the exercise of the police power and constituted an unwarranted and arbitrary interference with his constitutional rights. Individuals filed a cross-complaint in intervention. The trial court held that the ordinance was constitutional, valid, and enforceable. Plaintiff appealed and the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The ordinance considered in relation to defendant's accomplishment of a plan of community development, showed the challenged regulation to have had been reasonably adopted in furtherance of the general welfare, and plaintiff's individual liberty in the disposition of his property could be curtailed in the public interest. The court held that plaintiff was not allowed to divide his property into individual units for conveyance to separate individuals, but that his transactions in violation of the ordinance were not void, but voidable under the Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 11540.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the municipal ordinance limiting the subdivision of property to certain minimum lot requirements was constitutional, valid, and enforceable. Plaintiff's transactions in violation of the ordinance were not void, but were voidable at the option of the other parties to said transactions as provided by the Subdivision Map Act.

 

503 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Wells


Crim. No. 5114


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 2d 889; 221 P.2d 947; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 390



September 13, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus. The prisoner had been convicted for violent battery on a prison guard under Cal. Penal Code § 4500 and the death penalty was imposed.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner sought habeas corpus on the grounds that execution of the sentence would deprive him of life without due process and violate other provisions of the federal and state constitutions. The court denied the writ of habeas corpus. The court held that the nondiscretionary death penalty applied to a felon who, while serving an indeterminate sentence with no fixed maximum term of years, committed an assault under § 4500. The court found that no portion of the prisoner's life term had been remitted, but before the prisoner committed the assault, the adult authority had considered the matter of fixing his term and had declined to reduce it to a term of years. The authority's action was not arbitrary, repugnant to traditional concepts of fairness, or designed to destroy defendant. The court held that the fact that the prisoner was not allowed to present evidence as to his state of mind did not amount to a refusal of his defense, but only a rejection of some evidence concerning the defense. The court ruled that there was no discrimination so there was no equal protection violation. The court found that the imposition of the death penalty was not cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court denied the prisoner's writ of habeas corpus.

 

504 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Escobedo v. State, Dep't of Motor Vehicles


L. A. No. 20902


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 2d 870; 222 P.2d 1; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 388



September 13, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel reissuance or reinstatement of a motor vehicle operator's license.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensee sought a mandate directing respondents, department of motor vehicles and its director, to reissue, return, or reinstate his operator's license.


OVERVIEW: The department of motor vehicles suspended the operator's license of the licensee because he was involved in an automobile accident and failed to meet the security requirements of Cal. Veh. Code § 420. The licensee was not afforded a hearing prior to the suspension of his license. The licensee argued that he was denied due process and equal protection and that § 420 vested judicial power in a purely ministerial department in violation of Cal. Const. arts. III, § 1, VI, § 1. The court denied the writ, holding that: (1) the suspension of the license without prior hearing did not violate due process because the action was subject to subsequent judicial review and was reasonably justified by a compelling public interest; (2) the financial responsibility requirement did not violate equal protection; and (3) giving the department of motor vehicles the power to exercise limited judgment as to the amount of security required was not an unconstitutional delegation of power because the requirement that the department base the amount on the probable size of any court judgment which may have been recovered provided a reasonable, sufficiently certain standard to be followed.


OUTCOME: The court denied the licensee's petition for a writ of mandamus directing the department of motor vehicles to reinstate his operator's license.

Veriddo v. Renaud


Sac. No. 6098


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 2d 263; 217 P.2d 647; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 333



April 28, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sonoma County. Hilliard Comstock, Judge.

Action for damages for personal injuries sustained in an automobile collision.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant after sustaining demurrer to amended complaint without leave to amend, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff motorists challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), which granted defendant driver's demurrer to the motorists' personal injury complaint.


OVERVIEW: The motorists filed a personal injury complaint after they were involved in an automobile accident with the driver. According to the motorists, the accident was caused by the driver's negligence. The motorist's complaint alleged that at the time of the collision, defendant was an employee of the State and that the automobile that the driver was operating was owned by and being driven with the State's consent. In response to the motorists' complaint, the driver demurred on the ground that the complaint, as amended, did not state a cause of action in that it failed to allege compliance with the claims provision of the Cal. Gov't Code § 1981. After the trial court sustained the driver's demurrer, the motorists appealed. On appeal, the motorists argued that the filing of a claim pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 1981 was not necessary because they sought to hold the driver personally liable. However, the court held that the claims provisions of Cal. Gov't Code § 1981 did not apply to claims against the public agency, but only those claims against public employees and officers. Furthermore, the court found no valid constitutional basis for holding that the Cal. Gov't Code § 1981 was void.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

 

507 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Packer v. Board of Retirement, etc.


L. A. 21038


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 2d 212; 217 P.2d 660; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 328



April 25, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 22, 1950.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Frank G. Swain, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel payment of a widow's pension.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Judgment dismissing petition on sustaining demurrer thereto without leave to amend, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant widow challenged the ruling of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied her proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent retirement board (the board) to pay her a pension. The court sustained the board's demurrer and dismissed the widow's action.


OVERVIEW: The widow contended that her husband's service under the 1937 provision of the County Peace Officer's Retirement Law, 1931 Cal. Stat. 268, § 11, gave her, as a third party beneficiary, a separate vested right to a widow's pension, which a 1941 amendment, 1941 Cal. Stat. 745, to the retirement law destroyed. The widow further argued that even if she did not have a separate, vested right to a pension, the 1942 amendment exceeded any permissible modification of her husband's pension plan. The court affirmed the trial court's ruling and held that the widow was not entitled to a pension. In so holding, the court found that 1) the grant of a pension to a member's widow was an increase in benefits which could be taken away before the right vested by the happening of the event calling for the fulfillment of the grant; 2) the widow did not acquire a vested interest in the pension plan until it became payable to her; 3) any one or more of the pension benefits could be eliminated prior to the time they became payable; and 4) the deletion of the provision under which the widow would have been entitled to a pension was not an unconstitutional modification of the county's retirement plan.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the trial court's dismissal of the widow's action and the denial of her request for a writ of mandamus compelling the board to pay her a pension. The court held that any one of the pension benefits could be eliminated prior to the time they became payable and the deletion of the provision under which the widow would have been entitled to a pension was not an unconstitutional modification of the county's retirement plan.

Werner v. Southern California Associated Newspapers


L. A. No. 21132


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 2d 121; 216 P.2d 825; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 321; 13 A.L.R.2d 252



April 14, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 11, 1950. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order sustaining a demurrer to a complaint. Stanley N. Barnes, Judge.

Action for damages for libel.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Judgment of dismissal affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tort victim challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained appellee newspaper's demurrer to the victim's libel complaint. The newspaper argued that the victim neither complied with the retraction provisions of Cal. Civ. Code § 48a nor established special damages as required by the statute. The victim argued that § 48a was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The victim's libel action was dismissed on the newspaper's demurrer because the victim did not first comply with the retraction provisions of Cal. Civ. Code § 48a or adequately plead special damages as required by the statute. The court denied the victim's argument that § 48a violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 9 or the United States Constitution for the following reasons. First, the State constitution did not automatically create a cause of action; it merely recognized limitations to speech as recognized by the common law or as created by the legislature. Second, it was within the constitutional authority of the legislature, for public policy reasons, to limit or restrict libel actions or to extend privileges to newspapers as a special class. Third, if the legislature determined that a retraction was a better remedy than a judgment, that policy determination, even if unsound, could not be thrown out by the court unless it was clearly unconstitutional. Any regulatory exercise by the legislature, the court held, may limit or enhance the rights of some classes over that of others if done so with a reasonable relationship to the intended social goal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court that sustained the newspaper's demurrer to the tort victim's libel complaint. The court held that the legislature was within its constitutional authority to enact a statute that required the victim to first attempt a retraction by the newspaper and also required a showing of special damages.

People v. West Pub. Co.


S. F. No. 17970


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 2d 80; 216 P.2d 441; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 316



April 4, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. James G. Conlan, Judge.

Action to enforce statutory liability of foreign corporation for use taxes.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant legal publishing company challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that denied its motion to quash service of summons and denied its application for writ of prohibition in an action concerning enforcement of taxes levied against the publishing company.


OVERVIEW: This action was commenced by the state to enforce the statutory liability of a legal publishing company for use taxes levied as the result of its sale of law books and legal periodicals to California customers during the period of July 1, 1935, to December 31, 1939 pursuant to the California Use Tax Act of 1935. The publishing company argued that there was a bar on the collection of the taxes due to a statute of limitations on a portion of the tax claim; the unconstitutionality of the tax levy in relation to its mail order sales as a burden on interstate commerce and in violation of the due process clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court stated the present action was commenced well within the authorized period and the statute of limitations did not operate to bar all due tax collections, only those made prior to October 15, 1937. The court also stated that there was no violation of the commerce clause involved in the requirement that that an out of state seller of goods collect a use tax on goods sold for use within the state. Therefore, the tax was not a burden on instate commerce and the court affirmed the decision of the superior court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court that allowed the state to enforce a use tax against a legal publishing company. The court held that the use tax did not burden interstate commerce and was not a violation of the due process clause contained in the Fourteenth Amendment.

Lorenson v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L. A. No. 21155


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 2d 49; 216 P.2d 859; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 312



April 4, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Los Angeles County from proceeding with the trial of an action.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) from proceeding to trial against him on charges of conspiracy to commit robbery, conspiracy to commit assault with a deadly weapon, and conspiracy to pervert or obstruct justice or the due administration of the laws. He also sought a dismissal of the indictment on the basis that the criminal conspiracy statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that he was entitled to a writ of prohibition because the evidence presented before the grand jury was insufficient to show that he had committed any crime. Defendant also alleged that the criminal conspiracy statute, Cal. Penal Code § 182, was unconstitutionally vague. The court rejected the insufficiency argument, noting that the decision of whether or not to indict an individual was left to the province of the grand jury, and that a court could not substitute its judgment therefor. The court also pointed out that the level of evidence necessary to satisfy an indictment was less than that required to convict an individual. While the latter required a showing of guilt beyond a reasonable doubt as to all elements of the offense, the former was satisfied through probable cause, which the court defined as that state of facts that would lead a man of ordinary caution and prudence to believe and conscientiously entertain a strong suspicion of the accused's guilt. The court, reviewing the language of § 182, found it sufficiently definite to satisfy the requirements of due process.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of prohibition and the alternative writ that sought dismissal of the indictment against defendant.

Martin Ship Service Co. v. Los Angeles


L. A. Nos. 20774, 20775


Supreme Court of California

Actions for declaratory relief.
