PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared an occupation tax under Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 21.190a unconstitutional and permanently enjoined the collection of the tax in respondent independent contractors' action against the city for declaratory relief from imposition of the tax.

The superior court declared the tax unconstitutional and issued a permanent injunction to enjoin the collection of the tax. The city appealed. The court reversed the judgment that declared the tax unconstitutional and enjoined its collection. OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that declared the occupation tax unconstitutional and enjoined its collection in the independent contractors' action against the city for declaratory relief.

 

513 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


El Dorado Oil Works v. McColgan


S. F. No. 17862


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 2d 731; 215 P.2d 4; 1950 Cal. LEXIS 286



February 17, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 16, 1950.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Robert L. McWilliams, Judge.

Action to recover franchise taxes paid under protest.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which found in favor of defendant franchise tax commissioner in the corporation's action to recover franchise taxes paid under protest.


OVERVIEW: The corporation was engaged in processing and selling coconut oil and maintained its offices and manufacturing facilities within the state, but had an office in the Philippines where it purchased its raw materials. The tax commissioner rejected the corporation's computation of franchise taxes for two years and recalculated the taxes. The corporation paid the additional taxes under protest and filed an action to recover them. The trial court ruled in favor of the commissioner. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the commissioner's method of computation of the taxes did not violate the corporation's constitutional rights. The commissioner had discretion granted by statute to use a three factor formula in calculating the corporation's net income earned in its unitary business, which did not take into consideration purchases and expenses of manufacture. The legislature's grant of such discretion did not constitute an unlawful delegation of power and the commissioner's order to the corporation to use a five factor formula did not preclude the commissioner from using a three factor formula, which fairly calculated the corporation's activities within and outside the state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against the corporation and held that the franchise tax commissioner's calculation of the corporation's franchise taxes utilizing a three factor formula was within its statutory discretion, was a fair allocation, and did not violate the corporation's constitutional rights.

 

514 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Millsap v. Hooper


L. A. Nos. 20818, 20819


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 2d 192; 208 P.2d 982; 1949 Cal. LEXIS 153



August 19, 1949 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 15, 1949.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. Geo. A. Dockweiler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeals dismissed on motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a new trial. Respondent moved to dismiss the appeal on the ground that the notice of appeal was filed too late.


OVERVIEW: Judgment for respondent was entered, and notice of entry of judgment was mailed and filed 10 days later. The notice was received by appellant's counsel three days later, and appellants' notice of intention to move for a new trial was filed seven days thereafter. The motion was denied 50 days later, but the order of denial was not entered in the minutes for another eight days. Notice of appeal was filed on the 26th day after entry. Respondent contended the appeal should be dismissed as having been brought too late. The court agreed, holding that normally, the effective date of the denial of a motion for new trial was the date of the minute entry, and the 30-day period within which notice of appeal could be filed under Cal. R. Ct. 3(a) did not begin to run until such entry; however, the motion had been denied by operation of law under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 660 before the date of the minute entry. Therefore, the 30-day period in which to file the appeal was calculated from the date of denial of the motion by operation of law; and the appeal was filed after the expiration of that period.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal, which sought review of the denial of a new trial.

 

515 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Phyle v. Duffy


S. F. No. 17855


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 2d 144; 208 P.2d 668; 1949 Cal. LEXIS 150



August 1, 1949 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 25, 1949. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County. Arthur Coats, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel warden of state prison to institute proceeding for determination of sanity of a prisoner under sentence of death.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, defendant's mother, appealed the decision from the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which found that there was no reason to believe defendant was insane, discharged an alternative writ to appellant's mandamus, and vacated defendant's stay of execution.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was found not to be insane, and writs of habeas corpus and certiorari were denied, the execution of his death sentence was set. Defendant's mother, on his behalf, filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel the warden to institute a Cal. Penal Code § 3701 proceeding to determine defendant's sanity by a jury. An alternative writ was issued and the trial court found that there was no reason to believe defendant was insane, discharged the alternative writ, and vacated the stay of execution. On appeal, defendant claimed that because he had previously been found by a jury to be insane, he was presumed to be insane until a jury found him otherwise and that he was entitled to a right to a trial by jury under Cal. Penal Code § 3704. The court noted that to obtain such relief, his proper remedy was the proceeding for mandate. But upon the application for the writ itself he was not entitled to a trial by jury; only when he had obtained the writ could he have such a trial. The court found defendant had failed to show any "good reason" why the warden should initiate an inquiry. The judgment denying the remedy allowed by § 3701 was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

516 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ayres v. City Council of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 20673


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 2d 31; 207 P.2d 1; 1949 Cal. LEXIS 138; 11 A.L.R.2d 503



June 14, 1949 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. W. Turney Fox, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel a city council to approve a proposed subdivision map.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied the landowner's request for relief in a mandamus proceeding to compel respondent city council to approve a proposed subdivision map pursuant to the Subdivision Map Act (Act), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 11500 et seq., for the landowner's land without certain conditions imposed by the city planning commission.


OVERVIEW: A tentative map for the subdivision of the landowner's land was submitted to the city planning commission pursuant to the Act. The planning commission attached four conditions to which the landowner objected, whereupon he appealed to the city council. The city council upheld the conditions. The trial court upheld the decision of the city council and the court affirmed. The court held that where no specific restriction on the city council's power was contained in the charter, and none forbidding the particular conditions was included either in the Act or the city ordinances, it was proper to conclude that conditions were lawful. The court held that it was the landowner who was seeking to acquire the advantages of lot subdivision and upon him rested the duty of compliance with reasonable conditions for design, dedication, improvement, and restrictive use of the land. The requirement for the dedication of land to the widening of existing streets was not a compulsory taking for public use because it was a condition reasonably related to increased traffic and other needs of the proposed subdivision; thus, it was voluntary in theory and not contrary to constitutional concepts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the city council upholding the conditions imposed upon the landowner in developing his subdivision.

 

517 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Pasadena v. Alhambra


L. A. No. 19610


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 2d 908; 207 P.2d 17; 1949 Cal. LEXIS 253



June 3, 1949 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 27, 1949. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.
Writ of certiorari denied California-Michigan Land & Water Co. v. Pasadena, 339 U.S. 937, 94 L. Ed. 1354, 70 S. Ct. 671, 1950 U.S. LEXIS 2110 (1950)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Frank C. Collier, Judge.

Action for declaration of rights of parties to underground water within a basin area, and to enjoin an annual overdraft to prevent depletion of supply.



DISPOSITION: Modified and affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff modified and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a public utility, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which rendered a judgment substantially enforcing the terms of the stipulations allocating water and restricting total production to the safe annual yield against all parties, including the public utility, in respondent city's action seeking to determine ground water rights.


OVERVIEW: The public utility contended that the trial court improperly limited the amount of water that it could take from the ground in a certain area and improperly placed the burden of curtailing the overdraft proportionality on all parties. The court held that the trial court had authority to limit the taking of ground water for the purpose of protecting the supply and preventing a permanent undue lowering of the water table. The court also held that the rights of all the parties, including both overlying users and appropriators, were mutually prescriptive. Accordingly, the court held that the rights were of equal standing, with none prior or paramount. The court also held that the record indicated an actual adverse use of the disputed water, with an invasion of the rights of both overlying owners and appropriators. Furthermore, the court held that the owners of water rights were injured by additional appropriations made after all surplus waters were taken and that they were clearly entitled to obtain injunctive relief to terminate all takings in excess of the surplus. Finally, the court held that there was sufficient evidence that the rights of prior were invaded and damaged.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, as modified, the judgment of the trial court.

 

518 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Carter v. Seaboard Finance Co.


L. A. No. 20189


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 2d 564; 203 P.2d 758; 1949 Cal. LEXIS 216



March 11, 1949 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Carl A. Stutsman, Judge.

Action for damages for fraud, for violation of statute regulating conditional sales of motor vehicles, and for damages for usury.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part with directions. Judgment for defendant affirmed as to first and third causes of action; reversed with directions as to second cause of action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trucker appealed an adverse judgment that the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered against him in his action alleging fraud, usury, and statutory violations against defendant finance company.


OVERVIEW: The company financed and kept chattel paper on the trucker's acquisition of two semi-tractor trailer rigs, one of which the company had earlier repossessed, for use in establishing the trucker's commercial carrier. The repossessed truck later broke down and proved to have had its serial numbers illegally tampered with. When the company commenced repossession action against the trucker for nonpayment, the trucker filed suit, alleging fraud, violation of conditional sale contract statutes, and usury. The trial court entered judgment against the trucker, and he appealed. The court held that: (1) the trucker's fraud claim properly failed because, rather than relying on the representations of the company's agent, the trucker made a satisfactory independent examination of the diesel; (2) the parties' transaction violated the California law applicable to conditional sale contracts because the company's contracts did not conform with the controlling, mandatory terms of Cal. Civ. Code §§ 2981- 2982; and (3) the company did not commit usury under California law because the Personal Property Brokers Act did not fix a maximum interest rate on such loans exceeding $ 300.


OUTCOME: The court, in parts, affirmed and reversed and remanded.

 

519 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Santa Clara County v. Superior Court of Santa Clara County


S. F. No. 17861


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 2d 552; 203 P.2d 1; 1949 Cal. LEXIS 215



March 4, 1949 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 31, 1949. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Santa Clara County and James L. Atteridge, Judge assigned thereto, from proceeding further in connection with a temporary restraining order.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) from proceeding further in regard to a temporary restraining order and order to show cause. Such orders were issued in an injunction action commenced by a taxpaying voter to prevent the county from certifying to the legislature a proposed freeholders' charter adopted by the voters of that county.


OVERVIEW: A taxpaying voter filed an injunction action against the county regarding an election to approve a charter, and the trial court issued a temporary restraining order. The county applied to the court for a writ of prohibition, which was granted. The court held that the county board of supervisors was acting as an arm of the legislature by pursuing the prescribed procedure for preparing the charter and submitting it to the voters for ratification as precedent steps to its certification to the legislature. The court held that the trial court acted in excess of its jurisdiction in granting the temporary restraining order in prevention of the county's performance of its duty to submit the charter to the legislature in culmination of the charter proceedings as undertaken by them. The court found no justification for such judicial interference with the legislative process. The court found that the final act of certification did not involve further expenditure of public funds or any expenditure of the money of the county "taxpayer." The court held that the injunctive act was contrary to the doctrine of the separation of power inherent in the tripartite form of government.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ of prohibition in favor of the county to restrain the trial court from proceeding further in regard to a temporary restraining order and order to show cause that were preventing the county from certifying to the legislature a proposed freeholders' charter adopted by voters.

 

520 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Lockard v. Los Angeles


L. A. No. 20383


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 2d 453; 202 P.2d 38; 1949 Cal. LEXIS 206; 7 A.L.R.2d 990



February 1, 1949 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 28, 1949. Shenk, J., Carter, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Thurmond Clarke, Judge.

Action for a judgment declaring a zoning ordinance to be invalid as to plaintiffs' property.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for plaintiffs reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After exhausting all necessary administrative steps, plaintiffs, property owners and lessees, brought an action against defendant city to challenge their rezoning classification as commercial business and not light manufacturing. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) declared the zoning ordinance invalid insofar as it restricted the property owners to commercial uses.


OVERVIEW: The property owners in a 12-block strip had been cited for violations of the former zoning ordinance. The matter was held in abeyance pending plans for a comprehensive rezoning of the city. When the property was rezoned for similar commercial uses, the property owners challenged the zoning. The court found that there were undisputed facts that supported the planning commission and the city council in refusing to extend the boundaries of the light industrial zone. Thus, whether the 12-block strip was suitable for commercial uses or only for light industrial purposes were questions addressed to the legislative authorities, and their decision would not be disturbed if the matters were fairly debatable. Similar characteristics in adjacent and surrounding areas did not preclude the zoning authorities from placing adjoining territories in different zones. The court found that as a matter of law under the circumstances of this case that the ordinance was not unreasonably related to the interests of the community, or that there was an unwarranted interference with property rights in zoning the 12-block strip.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.

People v. Bemis


Crim. No. 4922


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 2d 395; 202 P.2d 82; 1949 Cal. LEXIS 201



January 25, 1949 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. Charles W. Fricke, Judge.

Prosecution for burglary.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction of second degree burglary, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) and from an order denying a new trial after he was found guilty by a jury of one count of burglary in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his friend were tried together for the burglary of a coffee shop. The friend confessed to the crime and the statements he made to the police were admitted into evidence against defendant. Defendant was convicted, and he appealed. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and the order denying a new trial. The court found that the trial court erred by failing to instruct the jury that evidence of the oral admissions of codefendant ought to have been viewed with caution. The court ruled that the legislature made it clear that it could not have been assumed that the jury would have had in mind the considerations that might have affected the weight or credibility of the testimony of an accomplice or the evidence of the oral admissions of a party.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of second degree burglary.

 

522 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Walker


Crim. No. 4833


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 2d 250; 201 P.2d 6; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 310



December 29, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 27, 1949.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239) from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. Harold B. Landreth, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction imposing the death penalty, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which convicted him of murder of the first degree, imposed the death penalty, and denied defendant's request for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted that there was insufficient evidence to sustain his conviction of murder and that his due process rights were violated. The court rejected defendant's contention, finding that the murder conviction was proper because the only issue before the court was that of defendant's sanity since he had pled not guilty by reason of insanity to the murder indictment. The court ruled that defendant's constitutional rights were protected because the trial court informed defendant that his plea would result in his admission of the commission of the crime and because defendant was represented by counsel. The court held that defendant was not entitled to a jury trial to fix the degree of the crime after his plea because the trial court was authorized under Cal. Penal Code § 1029 to make that determination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. The court held that defendant was not entitled to a new trial because there was no error that justified a reversal of his conviction or a reduction of the offense to second degree murder.

 

523 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Perez v. Sharp


L. A. No. 20305


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 2d 711; 198 P.2d 17; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 261



October 1, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 28, 1948. Shenk, J., Schauer, J., and Spence, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel county clerk to issue a certificate of registry and a license to marry.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding in mandamus, petitioner applicants sought to compel respondent, the County Clerk of Los Angeles County to issue them a certificate of registry pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 69a and a license to marry pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 69. The clerk refused to issue the certificate and license, maintaining that the applicants were a mixed couple and therefore could not be issued a license to marry one another.


OVERVIEW: The applicants were a Caucasian woman and an African American man. In denying the license, the clerk invoked Cal. Civ. Code § 69, which provided that no license could be issued that authorized the marriage of a white person with a Negro. Section 69 implemented Cal. Civ. Code § 60, which stated that all marriages between those races were illegal and void. The applicants contended that the statutes were unconstitutional. The court found that the right to marry was a fundamental right and that legislation infringing on that right had to be based upon more than prejudice and had to be free from oppressive discrimination to comply with the constitutional requirements of due process and equal protection of the laws. The court held that Cal. Civ. Code §§ 60 and 69 were not only too vague and uncertain to be enforceable regulations of a fundamental right, but that they also violated the Equal Protection of the Laws Clause by impairing the right of individuals to marry on the basis of race alone and by arbitrarily and unreasonably discriminating against certain racial groups.


OUTCOME: The court granted the applicants' petition for mandamus that requested the clerk to issue them a marriage license.

 

524 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Orcalles


Crim. No. 4870


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 2d 562; 197 P.2d 26; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 248



September 7, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 30, 1948. Shenk, J., Edmonds, J., and Spence, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239) from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County and from an order denying a new trial. Raymond T. Coughlin, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction imposing death penalty, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which convicted him of murder in the first degree in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1239 and imposed the death penalty. Defendant sought review of the trial court's order denying a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant dated a married woman. He met the woman's husband and her former boyfriend and a dispute ensued. At a later date, defendant attended a party which the husband and boyfriend attended. Without provocation, defendant stabbed the two people, stabbing the husband repeatedly and cutting off his nose and ears. Due to the prosecutor's misconduct, the court found that it was impossible to determine whether the findings as to the degree of the homicide were based on improperly admitted evidence and improper evidence from questions asked and comments made by the prosecutor and whether the death penalty was arrived at the by the jury in the exercise of a sound discretion based upon the case facts or was inflicted because of passion aroused by the misconduct. The fact that the wife made an extrajudicial statement that earlier she had a feeling that the killing would occur was not competent evidence and should not have been admitted. The state's efforts to introduce evidence about the wife's perception of the stabbing when she was not present was prejudicial and could not be repaired by an order to disregard. The court reversed defendant's murder conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's first degree murder conviction.

 

525 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


County of Mariposa v. Merced Irrigation Dist.


Sac. No. 5914


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 2d 467; 196 P.2d 920; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 237



August 30, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied September 27, 1948.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel an irrigation district and its directors to discontinue proceedings to include real property within its boundaries.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondents, irrigation district and its directors, to discontinue proceedings to include real property within the district's boundaries.


OVERVIEW: The district was organized to establish a common water supply for irrigating the lands within the district. The district owned land in the county, upon which some of its water works were situated, that was not yet within the district's boundaries. The district proceeded to include or annex the land in its boundaries and claimed that it would no longer be subject to the general property tax levied by the county. The county claimed that the law authorizing the inclusion of that land within the district's boundaries was invalid in that it would frustrate the purpose of Cal. Const., art. XIII, § 1 to require irrigation districts to pay taxes on land owned by them to the county in which the land was located but which was not within the district's boundaries. The court held that the district was authorized to annex or include additional land within its boundaries. The district was not really empowered to interfere with the counties' taxing power and process. By virtue of art. XIII, § 1, itself, property of public agencies was exempt from taxation, and thus, whenever the agency acquired property it attained that status. Thus, the county's petition was denied.


OUTCOME: The county's petition for a peremptory writ of mandate was denied. The alternative writ was discharged.

County of Los Angeles v. Southern California Tel. Co.


L. A. No. 19311


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 2d 378; 196 P.2d 773; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 231



August 13, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 8, 1948. Carter, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. William J. Palmer, Judge.

Action for injunctive relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county brought an action seeking to compel defendant telephone company to obtain a county franchise and to pay for the privilege of maintaining its lines and poles on streets and highways outside of incorporated areas. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) determined that the telephone company had a valid franchise from the state and denied the county's request for an injunction. The county sought review.


OVERVIEW: The telephone company argued that it had been granted the privilege to maintain its lines on highways by Cal. Civ. Code § 536. The county argued that § 536 was not applicable, and alternatively, if it was applicable, it violated the California Constitution and the United States Constitution. The court found that § 536 was intended to offer the grant of a franchise to telephone as well as telegraph companies and that by constructing and operating communication facilities, the companies agreed to the terms of the franchise. The court concluded that § 536 was not unconstitutional reasoning that because the offer of a franchise in § 536, when accepted, resulted in a binding agreement supported by a valid consideration, there was no gift within the meaning of the constitutional prohibitions contained in Cal. Const. art. IV, § 22 and 31. Furthermore, the court noted that a statute that classified citizens differently did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the United States Constitution, U.S. Const. amend XIV, when there was a reasonable basis for the classification and that there was ample justification for treating corporations differently from individuals in this instance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

527 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Hollman v. Warren


S. F. No. 17709


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 2d 351; 196 P.2d 562; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 227



August 4, 1948 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel governor to exercise his discretion with reference to appointment of notaries public in City and County of San Francisco.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner notary public candidate brought a proceeding seeking a writ of mandamus to compel respondent Governor of California to exercise his discretion with reference to the appointment of notaries public in the City and County of San Francisco.


OVERVIEW: A notary public candidate filed a writ of mandamus in an attempt to compel the governor to determine whether the public convenience necessitated the appointment of another notary public in San Francisco and, if so, to consider the notary public's fitness and qualifications for that appointment pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 8200. Section 8200 limited the number of notaries appointed to San Francisco. The notary public argued that the provision creating the limitation was a special and local law and that there was no rational basis for setting San Francisco County apart from other counties. The court ordered that a peremptory writ of mandate be issued in favor of the notary public, holding that the provision for the limitation on the number of notaries for San Francisco contained in § 8200 was invalid. The court found that the limitation was arbitrary and without any reasonable basis. The court concluded that there was no reason why the number of notaries for all the counties except San Francisco should be subject to the public needs as determined by the governor, while San Francisco's needs were determined by the legislature as to the maximum but not the minimum.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ of mandamus to the notary public in order to compel the governor to determine whether the public convenience necessitated the appointment of additional notaries public to San Francisco. The government code limiting the appointments of notaries in that county was found to have been arbitrary and unreasonable because it applied to no other areas of the state with no articulable reason.

Palermo v. Stockton Theatres, Inc.


Sac. No. 5758


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 2d 53; 195 P.2d 1; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 201



June 15, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 8, 1948.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County. M. G. Woodward, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for plaintiff reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessor challenged a judgment of the District Court of Appeal, Third Appellate District (California), that reversed a judgment declaring a lease of commercial real property in California to defendant Japanese corporation, as lessee, was violative of the California Alien Land Act, 1921 Cal. Stat. p. lxxxiii, Cal. Gen. Laws, Act 261 (1921), and consequently void and of no effect.


OVERVIEW: In 1944, plaintiff lessor filed an action to have a movie theatre lease to defendant Japanese corporation declared void in violation of the California Land Act (Act), after the Treaty of April 5, 1911, U.S.-Japan, art. I, 37 Stats. 1504, referenced in the Act was abrogated. The trial court held that the lease was void. Defendant appealed and the court of appeals reversed the judgment, holding that the Act incorporated the provisions of the Treaty as it existed on April 5, 1911, and that the abrogation of the Treaty in 1940 had no effect on the operation of the Act, which, therefore, still permitted a lease of real property by a Japanese corporation. The court granted a hearing for review, in which plaintiff argued that to construe the Act's reference to the Treaty as specific rather than general rendered the Act unconstitutional. The court held that it would not decide a constitutional question where other grounds were dispositive and that the court of appeals had correctly disposed of all issues involved. The court adopted the court of appeals judgment with the deletions and additions set forth in the opinion and denied plaintiff's petition for rehearing.


OUTCOME: The court adopted, with additions and deletions, the court of appeals judgment that reversed the judgment declaring a lease void in violation of the California Alien land Act (Act). The court held that the Act was not affected by the abrogation of the Treaty between the U.S. and Japan, that was referenced in the Act, and therefore, the Act still permitted a lease of real property to a Japanese corporation.

 

530 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Trombley


Crim. No. 4799


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 2d 801; 193 P.2d 734; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 362; 14 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P64,532



May 3, 1948 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer sought his discharge on habeas corpus from a judgment of conviction in a justice's court in Contra Costa County (California) for violations of Cal. Lab. Code § 216 for wilful failure to pay his employees' wages.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of violating Cal. Lab. Code § 216 in that, having the ability to pay, he willfully refused to pay wages due to three of his employees. The judgment of conviction was affirmed on appeal and petitioner sought discharge on habeas corpus. Petitioner claimed the statute conflicted with the constitutional prohibition against imprisonment for debt under Cal. Const. art. I, § 15. He also argued that the justice who presided at his trial acted without authority and the conditions on which suspension of the jail sentence depended were beyond the judge's power. The court found petitioner was accused of violating Cal. Lab. Code § 216(a), which meant he failed to pay his employees when he knew he had the means to do so. Such a conviction constituted a "case of fraud" falling within an exception to the constitutional prohibition. The imprisonment penalty did not violate the constitution. Moreover, the acts of the judicial officer were not subject to attack on the grounds of lack of authority in a habeas corpus proceeding where no objection to the authority of the acting justice was raised in the trial. The conviction was proper.


OUTCOME: The court denied habeas corpus relief.

Modern Barber Col. v. Cal. Emp. Stab. Com.


S. F. No. 17507


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 2d 720; 192 P.2d 916; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 356



April 29, 1948 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. George W. Schonfeld, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent commission to vacate its findings that certain persons are employees of petitioner within Unemployment Insurance Act and to cancel charges against petitioner.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for respondent on sustaining general demurrer to petition without leave to amend, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant barber college sought a review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied the writ of mandamus to compel respondent, California Employment Stabilization Commission, to vacate its findings that persons, including the owner, part-time bookkeeper, and student barbers, were employees of the barber college for purposes of the Unemployment Insurance Act, 1935 Cal. Stat. 1226.


OVERVIEW: Appellant barber college was operated by a sole owner with a part-time bookkeeper and student barbers who received commissions from their haircuts. Respondent, California Employment Stabilization Commission (commission), made a finding that these persons were employees for purposes of the Unemployment Insurance Act (Act), 1935 Cal. Stat. 1226. The barber college brought a proceeding in mandamus in the trial court to compel the commission to vacate its findings. The trial court dismissed the proceeding, and the barber college appealed. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. The court determined that § 45.11(d) of the Act prohibited any injunction, writ, or other proceeding to challenge the findings until the assessed contributions had been collected. The court also determined that the Act was constitutional because the college had no right to mandamus review of this action and the legislature had the power to create rights and remedies without infringing on the judicial branch.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the writ of mandamus by the trial court. The court determined that the statute required appellant barber college to pay the unemployment contributions to respondent, California Employment Stabilization Commission, in order to have the right to challenge the findings and that this procedure did not violate the state constitution.

 

532 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Eisley v. Mohan


S. F. No. 17534


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 2d 637; 192 P.2d 5; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 345



April 3, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied April 29, 1948. Carter, J., and Schauer, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel county assessor to assess to veteran his possessory interest in real property purchased from Veterans Welfare Board.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, veteran and Department of Veteran Affairs (department), sought to obtain a writ of mandate to compel respondent county tax assessor to assess the veteran's possessory interest in property that was purchased from the department.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a change in policy, the county tax assessor taxed the veteran's property based on its cash value. The veteran demanded that the assessment of the property remain on an unsecured tax roll, or 85 percent of its full cash value, and that he was entitled to a veteran's exemption of $ 1000. The department asserted that the new policy was interfering with the department's sale of property to veterans. The veteran and the department claimed that mandamus was the appropriate remedy and argued that the public property was exempt from taxation. The county tax assessor alleged that the veteran was the equitable owner of the property and that it was assessable to him at its full value. The court held that: (1) Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 15 did not apply to ad valorem taxes assessed for local purposes and did not preclude the issuance of a writ of mandamus; (2) the retention of legal title to the property by the department did not exempt the property from taxation; (3) equitable title and right of possession passed to the veteran under the executory contract of sale with the department; and (4) the property was properly assessable to the veteran in possession.


OUTCOME: The veteran's and the department's writ was discharged and a peremptory writ of mandate was denied.

 

533 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Knutzen


Sac. No. 5688


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 2d 573; 191 P.2d 747; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 339



March 31, 1948 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County directing distribution of an estate, an order settling the final account of an administrator, and an order denying a new trial and refusing to vacate the judgment. C. W. Miller, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed; appeal from second order dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant United States, as successor in interest of the alien heirs, appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which settled the final account of plaintiff administrator of the deceased's estate by directing the distribution of the deceased's estate to the administrator as the sole heir and denied the United States' motion for a new trial and its motion to vacate the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The administrator, who was the resident heir of the deceased, sought a final accounting of the deceased's estate. There were two additional heirs who were nonresident aliens and citizens of Germany. The superior court ordered the entire estate to be distributed to the administrator as the sole heir of the deceased and held that the nonresident heirs were not entitled to any part of the estate because their rights were forfeited under Cal. Prob. Code § 259. The United States filed a motion for a new trial and to vacate the judgment, contending that it had vested rights to the nonresidents' share of the estate. The superior court denied the motions. The United States appealed and the court reversed, ruling that § 259 was not a forfeiture statute but law of succession, which merely placed limitations on the rights of aliens to inherit. Pursuant to the 1925 treaty with Germany, 44 Stat. 2132, which was not incompatible with the Trading with the Enemy Act, 40 Stat. 411, 50 U.S.C.S. app. § 1 et seq., the nonresident heirs were entitled to their share of the estate and the United States, as the alien property custodian, was entitled to claim the shares by reason of the vesting order.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's judgment and held that the administrator was not entitled to the decedent's entire estate, that the nonresident alien heirs were entitled to their share of the proceeds from the estate, and that the United States was entitled to claim the nonresident aliens' share pursuant to the vesting order. The court dismissed the appeal from the judgment denying a new trial and the motion to vacate judgment as moot.

 

534 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Bevilacqua


S. F. No. 17058


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 2d 580; 191 P.2d 752; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 340



March 31, 1948 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco granting and refusing petitions for letters of administration. T. I. Fitzpatrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a first cousin of decedent, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) granting letters of administration to respondent public administrator and denying the petition of the cousin.


OVERVIEW: The cousin testified that he was a resident of California, that the decedent was also a California resident, that no will was found, and that decedent died intestate leaving a wife and four adult children. The wife and children were aliens residing in Italy. The cousin testified that he was the only relative of the decedent residing in the United States. He alleged that he was entitled to letters in preference of the public administrator because the wife and children were nonresident heirs and were not entitled to succeed to the estate pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code §§ 259. 259.1 and 259.2. The trial court ruled that the code sections were unconstitutional and that the Italian heirs were entitled to the estate. The court found that the trial court erred in adjudging that the code sections were unconstitutional as an invasion of fields reserved to the federal government. The cousin established a prima facie case showing a right to the letters. Under § 259, the burden of proving reciprocal inheritance rights was on the nonresident aliens. There was some proof that the cousin was entitled to inherit the estate, and it was error to deny him appointment as administrator.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment entered in favor of the public administrator.

536 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Sandstrom v. California Horse Racing Board


L. A. No. 20332


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 2d 401; 189 P.2d 17; 1948 Cal. LEXIS 320; 3 A.L.R.2d 90



February 3, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 1, 1948. Carter, J., Voted for a Rehearing.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Henry M. Willis, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel racing board to cancel order suspending petitioner's license to train horses.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for petitioner reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant racing board sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered a judgment in mandamus requiring the racing board to cancel an order that suspended appellee trainer's license.


OVERVIEW: The racing board issued a suspension of the trainer's license to train horses after a stimulant was found in the urine of one of his racehorses. The trainer sought a writ of mandamus that was issued once the racing board failed to answer the trainer's complaint after the racing board's demurrer was overruled. On appeal, the racing board again attacked the sufficiency of the trainer's petition and the trainer asserted that the Board's Rule 313, codified at tit. 4, Cal. Adm. Code, § 1930 (Rule 313), was unconstitutional because it made a trainer the absolute insurer of a horse's condition. The court held that Rule 313 was not unconstitutional but was an appropriate exercise of police power by the state over racing and the wagering on it. The strict liability the rule imposed was for the condition of the horse. The application of strict liability did not create any presumption that the trainer was the person administering drugs to the horse because fault was not at issue in Rule 313. The trainer did not dispute his position of trainer or that a stimulant was found in the urine of his horse. He was strictly liable under Rule 313 and a suspension was required.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of mandamus requiring the racing board to reinstate the trainer's license.

 

537 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Taylor v. Madigan


Civ. No. 36073


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


53 Cal. App. 3d 943; 126 Cal. Rptr. 376; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1626



December 22, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 21, 1976, and Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 3, 1976. Richardson, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 443647, Spurgeon Avakian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment entered by the trial court is hereby reversed and the injunction ordered dissolved. Appellant is awarded his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sheriff sought review from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted summary judgment for respondent homeowners, ruled that the homestead statutes protected respondents, and enjoined appellant from satisfying judgments by property sales. The issue on appeal involved whether the homestead statutes, in conjunction with the execution statutes, were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent homeowners had judgments against them and appellant sheriff sought to execute the judgments by the sale of respondents' homes. The trial court ruled that the homestead statutes protected respondents and enjoined appellant from executing the judgments. On appeal the issues concerned legal arguments as to whether the homestead statutes in conjunction with the execution statutes violated the California Constitution or the equal protection clauses of the state or federal Constitutions. The court examined the constitutional and statutory language that purported to control the situation. The court held that the homestead exemption was of purely statutory origin and that the legislature properly carried out the mandate of Cal. Const. art. XVII, § 1. The court indicated that respondents were not automatically protected under the constitution but rather had the responsibility to take affirmative steps under statutes that allowed protection. The court concluded that the homestead statutes in conjunction with the execution statutes did not violate the equal protection clauses of the state and federal Constitutions. The order of the trial court was reversed.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court that prevented appellant sheriff from executing judgments against respondent homeowners by the forced sale of their homes was reversed because the homestead exemption was of purely statutory origin and there were no automatic constitutional protections. The court found that respondents were not protected where they failed to take the required steps in order to qualify under the homestead statutes.

 

538 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Erven v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 15086


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


53 Cal. App. 3d 1004; 126 Cal. Rptr. 285; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1631



December 22, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 108825, Leo A. Deegan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which dismissed plaintiffs' petition against defendant seeking a declaration that actions taken by defendant in furtherance of resolutions which sought to implement and finance road improvement and maintenance in a specified service area were invalid.


OVERVIEW: Defendant adopted a resolution declaring its intention to provide road improvements and maintenance service in an area and levy a tax upon taxable property in the area to provide for the service. Defendant then adopted a resolution calling for a special election in order to submit to voters in the service area the question whether the maximum tax rate in the area should be increased to pay for the service. Plaintiffs sued defendant, asserting that defendant's actions were invalid because, among other things, defendant's resolution authorizing road improvement and maintenance was in excess of its jurisdiction. The court dismissed plaintiffs' complaint, and plaintiffs appealed. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 25210.4 authorized the establishment of a county service area for the purpose of providing within the area any other governmental services, referred to as miscellaneous extended services, which the county was authorized by law to perform. Therefore, because road improvement and maintenance were traditional governmental functions rendered by counties, those functions constituted miscellaneous extended services within the purview of § 25210.4. Judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Judgment dismissing plaintiffs' petition against defendant was affirmed where defendant was properly within its jurisdiction when it adopted resolutions for the improvement and maintenance of roads within the county.

 

539 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Socialist Workers etc. Committee v. Brown


Civ. Nos. 45943, 46314, 46258


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


53 Cal. App. 3d 879; 125 Cal. Rptr. 915; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1619



December 19, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On January 9, 1976, the Judgment was Modified to Read as Printed Above. The Petitions of All the Plaintiffs and Appellants and of the Defendants, Cross-complainants and Respondents for a Hearing by the Supreme Court were Denied March 3, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C101761 and C115029, Campbell M. Lucas, Jerry Pacht and Norman R. Dowds, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Each of the orders denying a preliminary injunction is affirmed. The order which vacated the order granting leave to intervene, and struck the answer in intervention, is affirmed. The order granting a preliminary injunction is reversed. Each party is to bear its own costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs and defendants appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied plaintiffs' applications for preliminary injunction to enjoin defendants from enforcing certain provisions of the Waxman-Dymally Campaign Disclosure Act, Cal. Elec. Code § 11500 et seq. (the Act), and which granted defendants' applications for preliminary injunctions ordering plaintiffs to comply with the Act.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sued defendants seeking a declaration that certain provisions of the Waxman-Dymally Campaign Disclosure Act, Cal. Elec. Code § 11500 et seq. (the Act), which required the disclosure of individuals who made contributions to campaign committees and prohibited anonymous contributions, were unconstitutional. Plaintiffs also sought preliminary injunctions enjoining defendants from enforcing the Act. Defendants filed a cross-complaint against plaintiffs seeking injunctions ordering plaintiffs to comply with the Act. The trial court denied plaintiffs' application and granted defendants', and all parties appealed. The court held that on appeal from the denial of a preliminary injunction, the scope of inquiry was limited to determining whether the trial court abused its discretion, and the burden was upon the appellant to make a clear showing of that abuse. Here, plaintiffs made no such showing, but argued only the merits of the controversy. Denial of plaintiffs' application was, therefore, affirmed. However, because the granting of defendants' application had the effect of enforcing the order denying a preliminary injunction, it was, therefore, void and was reversed.


OUTCOME: Orders denying plaintiffs' preliminary injunctions was affirmed where plaintiffs failed to show that the trial court abused its discretion in denying the same. Order granting defendants' application for a preliminary injunction which ordered plaintiffs to comply with the challenged act revered where because that order had the effect of enforcing the order denying a preliminary injunction, it was therefore void.

 

540 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cameron


Crim. No. 1985


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


53 Cal. App. 3d 786; 126 Cal. Rptr. 44; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1610



December 16, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 15, 1976. Ginsburg, J., * was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 11, 1976. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Assigned by Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 16572, John D. Jelletich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court, Kern County (California) for infliction by a husband of corporal injury upon his wife in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 273d.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of infliction by a husband of corporal injury upon his wife in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 273d. On appeal, appellant argued, in part, that the statute was unconstitutional because it denied a defendant equal protection of the law in that it applied only to married men who assaulted their wives and not to unmarried men who assaulted their paramours nor to wives who assaulted their husbands. The court of appeals affirmed appellant's conviction. The state had a greater interest in deterring crimes which disrupted the marriage relationship than in other classes of crime; thus, the classification did not violate the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. V. The legislature was not bound, in order to adopt a constitutionally valid statute, to extend it to all cases which could possibly have been reached, but was free to recognize degrees of harm and to confine its regulation to those classes of cases in which the need was deemed to be the most evident.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction for infliction of corporal injury upon his wife was affirmed because the penal statute prohibiting such conduct did not violate the Equal Protection Clause.

 

541 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Roth v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 36634


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


53 Cal. App. 3d 679; 126 Cal. Rptr. 163; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1598



December 12, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 9, 1976, and Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 18, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 4577, Ernest J. Zack, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, except for that part of the judgment awarding respondents $ 23.10, plus interest. The parties will bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that was in favor of respondents in their suit for the recovery of an assessment paid under protest.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ordered respondents to clean up the weeds on property owned by their foundation. When respondents failed to clean up the property, appellant declared that the property was a public nuisance, cleaned up the property, and assessed the costs on the property. Respondents protested and then paid the assessment. They then filed suit against appellant seeking a refund. The trial court entered judgment in favor of respondents and additionally held that the applicable code provisions constituted a denial of due process. Appellant sought review, and the court reversed. The court first found that respondents failed to exhaust their administrative remedies; therefore, they were precluded from filing suit for recovery of the assessments paid. If an administrative remedy was provided by statute, it had to be invoked and exhausted before judicial review of the administrative action was available. The court noted that an exception to exhaustion was where the available remedy did not meet the requirements of due process. The trial court erred in determining that there was such a denial here. The available administrative remedies satisfied the requirements of due process.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of respondents in their suit for recovery of assessments paid under protest was reversed. Respondents failed to exhaust their available administrative remedies before proceeding to court. Respondents were incorrect in their assertion that the available remedies constituted a denial of procedural due process.

 

542 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mel Mack Co.


Civ. No. 15275


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


53 Cal. App. 3d 621; 126 Cal. Rptr. 505; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1593



December 10, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 220118, H. Walter Steiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that issued a preliminary injunction prohibiting defendants from charging mobile home park brokers a fee on the ground the fee was in violation of Cal. Civ. Code §§ 789.7 and 789.8.


OVERVIEW: Defendants operated several mobile home parks. Whenever a park tenant sold a mobile home through a mobile home broker, defendants charged the broker a flat fee before allowing the buyer to move into the park. The state filed suit against defendants, alleging that these fees were being charged in violation of Cal. Civ. Code §§ 789.7 and 789.8. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction prohibiting defendants from charging the fees, and defendants sought review. The court affirmed. Defendants alleged that the language of the statutes did not apply to fees charged to brokers but only prohibited the charging of additional fees to tenants. The court disagreed. Looking at the intent of the legislature in passing the statutes and at the plain and ordinary meaning of the statutes' language, the court concluded that no fees were to be charged in connection with the transfer or sale of a mobile home where no services were performed by park management in the sale. Defendants did not show that they had performed any special services in connection with the sales; therefore, the fees were improper.


OUTCOME: Judgment issuing a preliminary injunction against defendants prohibiting them from charging a fee to mobile home brokers was affirmed. The statutes prohibiting the charging of fees in connection with any transfer or sale of a mobile home within a mobile home park was applicable here, especially in light of the fact that defendants performed no special services in the course of the sale.

 

543 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Pesola v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 45831


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


54 Cal. App. 3d 479; 126 Cal. Rptr. 580; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1838



December 10, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 84299, Arnold Levin, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that dismissed her complaint alleging that her payment of a municipal equine license tax was unconstitutionally duplicative of, and thereby preempted by, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 5701.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff owned a race horse on which she had to pay a municipal license tax. After paying the taxes, she filed a claim for refund on the ground the taxes were preempted by the property tax assessed pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 5701. Defendant denied her claim for refund, and plaintiff filed suit. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrers and dismissed the complaint. Plaintiff appealed, and the court affirmed. The character of a tax had to be determined by its incidents and from the natural and legal effect of the language employed. Looking at the municipal ordinance, the court determined that what was imposed was truly a license or excise tax or fee and was not a property tax like that imposed by § 5701. The court further held that there was no unconstitutional double taxation because before invalid double taxation could be said to exist, both taxes had to have been imposed in the same year, for the same purpose, upon property owned by the same person, and by the same taxing authority. These conditions did appear here.


OUTCOME: Judgment dismissing plaintiff's complaint alleging unconstitutional double taxation was affirmed. The municipal ordinance imposing a license fee for plaintiff's ownership of a race horse was not unconstitutionally duplicative of a similar property tax imposed by the state. The fact that different taxing bodies were involved precluded a finding of double taxation.

 

544 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Justus v. Atchison


Civ. No. 45917Civ. No. 45918


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Three


53 Cal. App. 3d 556; 126 Cal. Rptr. 150; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1589



December 9, 1975 


NOTICE:

 [**1] Pages 556-575 have been omitted; HEARING GRANTED.  NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, parents of stillborn children appealed from judgments of a California trial court in favor of defendant doctors and hospital, dismissing with prejudice two causes of action of their medical malpractice complaint, for wrongful death of the children and for plaintiff fathers' emotional trauma in watching the delivery.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed claims of plaintiff parents of stillborn children, in two cases alleging wrongful death of the children and plaintiff fathers' emotional trauma in watching the delivery, against defendant doctors and hospital. The court affirmed, finding that it could review the issues despite the final judgment rule where plaintiff mothers had remaining claims, because both parties sought review. The court concluded that the words of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 377 were to be strictly construed and, therefore, plaintiffs could not claim wrongful death for an unborn child. Further, § 377 did not violate equal protection. The court found that plaintiff fathers sufficiently alleged physical injury from the emotional trauma from witnessing the delivery, but the shock they suffered was not from their view of the deliveries themselves but from the information given them immediately thereafter that their sons had died during the deliveries. The court found that plaintiff fathers did not have a cause of action because they were voluntary witnesses of the births that occasioned their emotional distress.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, finding that the wrongful death cause of action was properly dismissed because the statute that provided for wrongful death causes of action required that the child be born alive and did not violate equal protection. The court held that plaintiff fathers' cause of action for emotional trauma was properly dismissed because they voluntarily witnessed the births.

People v. Vargas

Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a conviction from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), for furnishing a dangerous drug in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11912.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed a conviction for furnishing a dangerous drug in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11912. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in proceeding with the trial in his absence, that defendant was denied the effective assistance of counsel, and that the trial court erred in giving instructions on flight and with respect to the quantity of a restricted drug that was usable. Defendant further asserted that the trial court abused its discretion in failing to grant him probation, and that the provisions of § 11912 requiring imprisonment for a period of not less than three years before parole eligibility were unconstitutional. The court affirmed the conviction, finding no errors in the trial. However, the court found that the minimum term before parole was out of proportion to the crime to the extent that it was unconstitutional and directed that the minimum eligible date for parole be set under Cal. Penal Code § 3049 instead of Cal. Heath & Safety Code § 11912.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction, finding no error in the trial, and directed that the sentence be modified to reduce the minimum time before eligibility for parole.

People v. Cobbs


Crim. No. 13793


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


53 Cal. App. 3d 937; 126 Cal. Rptr. 494; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1625



November 24, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 88514, Joseph Karesh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order setting aside the information and discharging the defendants is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) setting aside an information charging respondent health care providers with felonies based upon false reimbursement claims.


OVERVIEW: Respondent health care providers, a licensed physician and a licensed clinical social worker, prepared and submitted service reports seeking reimbursement from the state for services provided to indigent patients. The service reports were found to be fraudulent because the patients were never seen or treated by either respondent. Most of the patients were not even acquainted with respondents. Respondents were charged with certain felonies under Cal. Penal Code § 72 and Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14107. The trial court set aside the information, and the state challenged the order. The court noted that respondents' arguments that there were no fraudulent statements made on the claim forms and that the claim forms themselves were ambiguous were without merit. The court concluded that the service reports contained sufficient false statements to fulfill the requisite fraudulent intent of the crimes charged. In addition, § 72 and § 14107 were not unconstitutionally vague because each statute proscribed with certainty the presentation of a fraudulent claim, with intent to defraud, to a public officer or agency. The order setting aside the information was reversed.


OUTCOME: The order setting aside the information charging respondent health care providers with certain felonies was reversed because the service reports prepared and submitted by respondents contained sufficient evidence of fraudulent conduct with the intent to defraud a public agency.

 

547 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Perez


Crim. No. 14664


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Three


53 Cal. App. 3d 135; 125 Cal. Rptr. 814; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1546



November 21, 1975 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion deleted on direction of Supreme Court by order dated January 15, 1976


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner criminal sought a writ of habeas corpus, claiming his prior conviction should be stricken because the pronouncement of judgment by a California trial court did not contain a recitation of the prior conviction. Petitioner further contended that the sentence of five to 20 years with no eligibility for parole for five years under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(b) constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner criminal sought a writ of habeas corpus to overturn his sentence of five to 20 years for a narcotics offense. Petitioner claimed that his prior conviction should be stricken because the oral pronouncement of judgment did not contain a recitation of the prior conviction and that the sentence under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(b), with no eligibility for parole for five years, constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The state contended that with a negotiated plea bargain, it was unnecessary for the lower court to recite findings. The court found that the lower court's intent to sentence petitioner based upon admitted prior offenses was evident because the record was replete with references to the prior conviction and enhancing effect; therefore, the intent was clear, and pronouncement at judgment was not required. The court held unconstitutional a mandatory minimum term before parole under § 11350(b); however, because petitioner did not contend his maximum term was in violation of the constitution, and because the mandatory minimum term was severable from the remainder of the statute, the sentence remained valid. The court denied the writ.


OUTCOME: Petitioner criminal's writ of habeas corpus was denied because the sentencing judge was not required to recite that a prior conviction had been admitted where the record was replete with references to the prior conviction and the intent of the judge in sentencing was clear. The court held the mandatory minimum term before parole was unconstitutional, but the sentence was otherwise valid.

 

548 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Reynolds v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. No. 37781


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


53 Cal. App. 3d 99; 125 Cal. Rptr. 673; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1541



November 19, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 12, 1975, and Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 14, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. S.C.A. 12579, Jay A. Pfotenhauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) certified an appeal of a small claims court order that required appellant city to refund to respondent judge a filing fee he paid to declare his candidacy for judge.


OVERVIEW: Respondent judge desired to continue in office as judge and paid a statutory filing fee to appellant city upon presenting his candidacy papers to the registrar of voters. Thereafter, the statute requiring the filing fee was declared unconstitutional. Respondent's request for a refund was denied, and respondent sued in the small claims court. Respondent prevailed, and the order requiring a refund was appealed to the trial court, which certified the appeal to the appellate court. The appellate court reversed the judgment, holding that because respondent did not protest the fee when he paid it, he was not permitted to recover even though the statute was later declared unconstitutional. The appellate court found no merit in respondent's argument that he paid the fee under coercion; coercion was not present where only the severity of the consequences to the individual was available for consideration.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the small claims court order requiring appellant city to refund respondent judge his filing fee. Because respondent did not protest the fee when he paid it and presented no proof that he paid the fee under coercion, he was not permitted to recover even though the statute under which it was paid was later declared unconstitutional.
