Board of Osteopathic Examiners v. Board of Medical Examiners


Civ. No. 14902


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


53 Cal. App. 3d 78; 125 Cal. Rptr. 619; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1539



November 18, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 4, 1975, and Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 14, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 242159, Lloyd Allan Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Board of Medical Examiners sought review of the summary judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which held that 1973 Cal. Stat. 1132, was null and void because the 1922 Osteopathic Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 3600- 1 et seq., precluded the legislature from authorizing appellant with jurisdiction over osteopathic school graduates.


OVERVIEW: In 1973, the legislature passed a statute, 1973 Cal. Stat. 1132, providing that appellant Board of Medical Examiners could issue a physician's and surgeon's certificate to anyone who obtained an out-of-state osteopathic school and obtained a doctor of osteopathic degree from it. Respondent Board of Osteopathic Examiners filed suit, claiming that the statute was null and void. The trial court held that the statute was null and void. The court reversed the trial court's holding. The court held that the legislature had the power to enact it. A similar law, enacted in 1962, was declared unconstitutional on equal protection grounds by the California Supreme Court, but the court held that only part of that law was held unconstitutional. The 1973 act amended earlier acts, providing for the licensing of new osteopaths under specified conditions. The court held that the title of the 1973 statute did not render it invalid under Cal. Const. art. IV, § 9. The court also held that the statute's title was not misleading.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's holding that the 1973 statute was null and void. The court held that a 1962 statute which transferred power to license osteopaths to appellant Board of Medical Examiners was only held unconstitutional in part by the supreme court. Therefore, the legislature had the power to enact the 1973 law.

 

550 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Williams


Crim. No. 8255


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


53 Cal. App. 3d 10; 125 Cal. Rptr. 457; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1532



November 14, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On December 12, 1975, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Petitioner's Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 8, 1976.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged. The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate, who had been convicted of sodomy and sentenced to a term of not less than one year, filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, alleging that his term had never been fixed by respondent California Adult Authority. Respondent did not challenge the allegation but averred that it was diligently setting terms of all prisoners in its custody.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was convicted in 1967 for sodomy under Cal. Penal Code § 286. He was sentenced to a term of not less than one year. He was released on parole in 1973 but was arrested within days of release and returned to prison for a parole violation. Petitioner filed a request for writ of habeas corpus, asking the court to order respondent California Adult Authority to fix his sentence forthwith or show cause why it had not done so. Respondent set out for the court a detailed procedure by which it was fixing the term of indeterminate sentences. The court held that petitioner was not entitled to a writ of habeas corpus. While petitioner was entitled at some point to have his sentence fixed, petitioner's claim that if it were done now, it would be fixed at a lesser time than he had already served was not supported by the record because the details of his offense were not known. The court also held that respondent's need for circumspection in fixing sentences mandated that respondent proceed carefully. An inmate's behavior or circumstances could result in a lesser sentence than the one respondent fixed on him, but never a greater one -- hence the need for caution.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner inmate's request for a writ of habeas corpus. While noting that at some point petitioner had a right to have his indeterminate sentence fixed, the court found that respondent California Adult Authority was not acting with less than entire good faith and that any other method of setting terms for prisoners with indeterminate sentences was not apparent.

 

551 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Arredondo


Crim. No. 2181


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


52 Cal. App. 3d 973; 125 Cal. Rptr. 419; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1526



November 13, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 16579, Leonard M. Ginsburg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the action is remanded for consideration of appellant's suitability for probation and re-sentencing as provided by law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which sentenced him to prison, as was recommended by the jury, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 264, for having sexual intercourse with a girl 15 years of age in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261.5.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of having sexual intercourse with a girl 15 years of age in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261.5. The trial court sentenced him to prison as was recommended by the jury, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 264. The appellate court held that since every person charged with the offense has the same chance for leniency as well as the same possibility of receiving the maximum sentence there is nothing discriminatory in the statute. Appellant made no factual showing of intentional discriminatory application of Cal. Penal Code § 264. The court found that appellant waived the right to appeal alleged error by failing to make an appropriate objection at trial, however the court continued that it could review any instruction given even though no objection was made in the trial court because substantial rights of appellant were affected. The record did not indicate that the trial court considered the report of the probation officer before passing on probation or sentence as required by Cal. Penal Code § 1203. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded for consideration of appellant's suitability for probation and resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court sentencing appellant to prison and remanded for consideration of appellant's suitability for probation and re-sentencing. The court held that that record did not reflect that the trial court considered the report of the probation officer before passing on probation and sentencing because the trial court apparently believed it was bound by the jury's recommendation of imprisonment.

Redevelopment Agency v. Goodman


Civ. Nos. 36155, 36433


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


53 Cal. App. 3d 424; 125 Cal. Rptr. 818; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1575



November 13, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 605192, Clayton W. Horn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's order in 1 Civil No. 36155 is affirmed, respondent therein to recover costs on appeal. The trial court's order in 1 Civil No. 36433 is reversed with directions to enter an order requiring payment to appellants of the interest actually earned on their share of the condemnation award and on their award for costs. Appellants to recover their costs on this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a combined appeal, appellant property owners sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that denied appellants' motion for an order determining interest on a condemnation judgment in favor of respondent Redevelopment Agency of the City and County of San Francisco and that denied appellants interest on funds paid on the judgment.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Redevelopment Agency obtained a judgment and order for condemnation against appellant property owners, in which a sum of money was deposited into an interest bearing account for appellants, pursuant to the judgment, which included no provision for interest. Pursuant to the trial court's order of disbursement, appellants withdrew the award. The trial court granted respondent an order for possession, but denied both appellants' motion for an order determining interest on the judgment through the appellate process and appellants' motion to recover the interest already earned. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellants' motion for an order determining interest, holding that appellants denied the existence of their right to take the interest by withdrawing the award and pursuing an appeal. The court reversed the trial court's order denying the motion to recover the interest earned, holding that as the funds were appellants' and could have been withdrawn at any time, appellants were entitled to the interest actually earned on their share of the condemnation award and on their award for costs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and reversed the decisions of the trial court that denied appellant property owners' motion for an order determining interest on a condemnation judgment in favor of respondent Redevelopment Agency and denied appellants interest on funds paid on the judgment. The court held that appellants were not entitled to the interest they might have received, but that they were entitled to interest that the judgment had already earned.

 

553 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Fasken


Civ. No. 34905


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


52 Cal. App. 3d 859; 125 Cal. Rptr. 296; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1516



November 7, 1975


NOTICE:

Opinion omitted. HEARING GRANTED.  NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW



DISPOSITION: The petition for rehearing is denied December 3, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state controller challenged the order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which calculated the excess federal estate credit on the basis of Texas's normal tax and found additional tax owing to the State of California.


OVERVIEW: The decedent, a resident of California, died and left her entire estate to respondent. Approximately 54 percent of the estate was located in Texas, with the balance in California. The maximum federal estate tax credit allowed to the states under I.R.C. § 2011 was $ 3,407,198. Texas has a pickup statute, which takes precedence over its normal inheritance tax, in which the percentage of the estate in Texas was multiplied by the total credit available to all the states. The Inheritance Tax Referee calculated the excess federal credit on the basis of Texas' normal tax and found additional tax owing to the State of California. Respondent filed an objection to the report and the trial court sustained the objection. Petitioner filed a timely appeal. On appeal, the court affirmed because it found that the pickup tax was constitutional as California was entitled to that proportion of the federal tax credit as property within the jurisdiction of California bore to the total estate property.


OUTCOME: The additional tax owing to the State of California when calculating the excess federal estate credit on the basis of Texas' normal tax was affirmed because the pickup tax was constitutional as California was entitled to that percent of the federal estate tax credit as the property within California bore to the total estate property.

In re Heredia


Crim. No. 7666


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist


52 Cal. App. 3d 785; 125 Cal. Rptr. 182; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1510



November 5, 1975 


NOTICE:

Opinion deleted on direction of Supreme Court by order dated January 15, 1976  NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought habeas corpus relief while confined in prison upon a judgment in which he was convicted of possession of heroin for sale in violation of former Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500.5. His sentence, as provided by statute, was 10 years to life imprisonment with a mandatory minimum of six years without possibility of parole, because he was charged with and admitted conviction of one prior felony narcotic offense.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was confined in prison after being convicted of possession of heroin for sale in violation of former Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500.5. His sentence, as provided by statute, was 10 years to life imprisonment with a mandatory minimum of six years without possibility of parole, because he was charged with and admitted conviction of one prior felony narcotic offense. Petitioner sought habeas corpus relief on the basis that § 11500.5 was unconstitutional insofar as it imposed a mandatory minimum of six years before petitioner was eligible for parole; such mandatory minimum was claimed to be cruel and unusual punishment. The court denied petitioner's request for habeas corpus relief, finding that the sentence imposed under § 11500.5 was not cruel and unusual punishment. The court's basic inquiry was whether the six-year minimum was so disproportionate to the crime for which it was inflicted that it shocked the conscience and offended fundamental notions of human dignity. The court looked at relevant factors such as the nature of the offense, as well as isolation of the offender from society, and deterrence.


OUTCOME: The petition for habeas corpus relief was denied because the court found that a minimum sentence of six years of confinement without parole for a second offense of possession of heroin for sale was not cruel and unusual punishment.

In re Carter


Crim. No. 7655


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist


52 Cal. App. 3d 955; 125 Cal. Rptr. 177; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1524



November 5, 1975 


NOTICE:

Opinion deleted on direction of Supreme Court by order dated January 15, 1976  NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought habeas corpus relief while confined in prison upon a judgment in which he was convicted of possession of heroin in violation of former Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500. His sentence as provided by that section is five to 20 years imprisonment, without possibility of parole for five years, because he was charged with and admitted a prior felony narcotic conviction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was confined in prison after being convicted of possession of heroin in violation of former Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500. His sentence was five to 20 years of imprisonment, without possibility of parole for five years, as he had a prior felony narcotic conviction. He sought habeas corpus relief on the basis that § 11500 was unconstitutional insofar as it imposed a mandatory minimum of five years before eligibility for parole; that was claimed to be cruel and unusual punishment. The court denied petitioner's request for habeas corpus relief. However, the court concluded that the provision of § 11500 precluding a second offender from parole consideration for a period of five years, without statutory regard to the existence of such mitigating circumstances as possible addict status of the offender and the quantity of narcotics involved in the offense for which he was tried as well as in the prior offense, was cruel and unusual punishment in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 6. The state adult authority was directed to grant parole consideration to petitioner at such time as was otherwise appropriate.


OUTCOME: The writ of habeas corpus was denied. However, the court found the applicable parole restriction invalid. The state adult authority was directed to grant parole consideration to petitioner at such time as is otherwise appropriate under the laws of the state. The court concluded that the minimum five-year period of incarceration for simple possession of heroin was cruel and unusual punishment.

 

556 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Howard v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 46967


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


52 Cal. App. 3d 722; 125 Cal. Rptr. 255; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1503



November 3, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On December 2, 1975, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petition of the real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 28, 1976. Tobriner, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. Let a peremptory writ issue directing the superior court to vacate its order granting the preliminary injunction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner district attorney sought to vacate a preliminary injunction by the Superior Court of Los Angles County (California), in favor of real party in interest do-it-yourself divorce business. The injunction prohibited enforcement of Cal. Penal Code § 159a, which made it a misdemeanor to advertise offers to procure, or obtain dissolution or annulment of marriage because the statute was declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The State of California made it a misdemeanor to advertise offers to procure or obtain, or to aid in procuring or obtaining, any dissolution or annulment of marriage under Cal. Penal Code § 159a. Real party in interest do-it-yourself divorce businesses challenged the law and sought declaratory relief that the law was unconstitutional. The trial court, without an evidentiary hearing, entered a preliminary injunction against enforcement of § 159a as unconstitutional. The court vacated the order holding that § 159a was constitutional on its face based on the record before it. The court held that § 159a was a proper use of the police power by promoting the stability of marital relationships. The court found that § 159a fell within the state's concern that involved the delivery of legal advice. The court found that § 159a continued the presence of a policy against the excitation of vexatious litigation, and that prohibitions against the use of advertising to promote litigation harmonized with this policy. In addition, the court held that the relief granted was inappropriate because the order was made on mere allegations without an evidentiary hearing.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's decision holding that the statute prohibiting advertising for divorce services was constitutional and that the scope of its regulation fell within the police power of the state.

 

557 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Gill v. De Sanz


Civ. Nos. 44223, 44787


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


52 Cal. App. 3d 457; 125 Cal. Rptr. 384; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1476



October 24, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of appellant Gill for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 23, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NWC 35153, Raymond R. Roberts and Alexander R. Early III, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The orders appealed from in 2d Civ. No. 44223 and 2d Civ. No. 44787 are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff receiver for a California corporation appealed from the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which released and discharged an ex parte interim attachment of goods belonging to defendants, a state resident and Mexican companies, after defendants' general appearance.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a receiver for a California corporation, brought an action against defendants, a state resident and three Mexican companies, claiming that defendants owed the corporation money for overpayments made to defendants for labor and services. Plaintiff brought a motion seeking a reversal of an order entered by the trial court that released an ex parte attachment of defendant corporations' goods upon defendants' general appearance, which the trial court denied. On appeal of the trial court's order releasing the attachment, plaintiff asserted that he was entitled to the attachment because he was suing on a contract for "money loaned" or "services rendered," two of the four types of contracts covered by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537.1, subd. (a) for issuance of attachments. The court held that because what plaintiff was really suing for was a series of overcharges for work that was never performed and money obtained on a fraudulent representation, his position was untenable, because the clear intent of the legislature was to confine the availability of attachment against resident defendants to four specified types of transactions, none of which was alleged here.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the release of an ex parte attachment of defendant Mexican company's assets by the trial court and affirmed the denial of plaintiff receiver's action to prevent the release of the attachment, where the basis for plaintiff's action had no legal basis in the applicable statute, which authorized release upon defendants' general appearance.

Yuen v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 36350


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


52 Cal. App. 3d 351; 125 Cal. Rptr. 87; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1463



October 21, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of the plaintiff and respondent for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 30, 1975. Mosk, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 89056, Morton R. Colvin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded with directions that the writ of prohibition be denied and that the criminal proceedings in the municipal court be ordered reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant people of the city and county of San Francisco sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted respondent's writ of prohibition that permanently restrained the municipal court from taking any further action against him regarding a charge for violation of San Francisco, CA., Police Code § 1291, prohibiting loitering while carrying concealed weapons.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was charged with a violating San Francisco, CA., Police Code § 1291, which prohibited loitering while carrying a concealed weapon. Respondent was granted his petition for writ of prohibition that permanently restrained the municipal court from taking any further action against respondent because § 1291 had been preempted by state law. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the lower court and remanded the cause with directions that the criminal proceedings against respondent be reinstated. Section 1291 was not preempted by California state law that prohibited loitering and therefore, did not violate Cal. Const. art. XI, § 7. Section 1291 supplemented the state legislation against loitering, which the legislature did not intend to preempt. Cal. Penal Code §§§ 653(k), 3024, 12020, did not preempt Police Code § 1291 because the state law had not completely covered the proscriptions against knives making it exclusively a state matter. Section 1291 was not vague or overbroad because it gave a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that in addition to loitering an individual must also be carrying a concealed weapon in order to be in violation of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment granting respondent's writ of prohibition and remanded the cause with directions that respondent's petition for the writ of prohibition be denied and that the criminal proceedings against him be reinstated. The court found that California state law prohibiting loitering did not preempt the San Francisco ordinance that prohibited loitering with a concealed weapon that respondent was accused of violating.

 

559 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Waters


Crim. No. 2198


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


52 Cal. App. 3d 323; 125 Cal. Rptr. 46; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1460



October 20, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 30, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 126926, Jeremy C. Cook, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), denying motions to withdraw a plea of guilty and to set aside the indictment pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 995. Appellant had pleaded guilty to an indictment charging conspiracy to sell a controlled substance in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182 and sale of a controlled substance in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pleaded guilty to conspiracy to sell a controlled substance, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182 and sale of a controlled substance, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379. The trial court denied appellant's motions to withdraw his plea and to set aside the indictment under Cal. Penal Code § 995. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not abuse its discretion under Cal. Penal Code § 1018 in denying appellant's motion to withdraw his guilty plea. Considering all factors, the court found no clear and convincing evidence that appellant's plea of guilty was not the result of his free and clear judgment. The court held that the motion to set aside the indictment was properly denied because once appellant entered a guilty plea, his right to contend that the indictment was based on insufficient evidence terminated. Furthermore, a guilty plea was an admission of each element of the offense and, thus, an admission of probable cause. The court held that the entry of the guilty plea was a bar to a § 995 motion. The court found that the sentence was not cruel and unusual in view of the gravity of the offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant's motion to withdraw his guilty plea because appellant was properly advised of his appeal rights and elected to enter a plea and pursue appellate remedies. The court affirmed the denial of the motion to set aside the indictment because it was filed after appellant had entered his guilty plea and therefore, his right to challenge the sufficiency of the indictment terminated.

Silva v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 46121


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


52 Cal. App. 3d 269; 125 Cal. Rptr. 78; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1453



October 17, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 27, 1975, and petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 11, 1975.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged, the peremptory writ is denied, and the case is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, consul of the Republic of Mexico, filed suit to obtain an alternative writ of prohibition seeking to restrain respondent, Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), from conducting further proceedings against him and to have the charges against him dropped. Petitioner was charged by indictment with conspiring to commit the crime of soliciting business for an attorney in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, consul of the Republic of Mexico, was charged with conspiring to commit the crime of soliciting business for an attorney in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182. He brought suit seeking to restrain respondent superior court from proceeding against him. He contended that the courts of California had no jurisdiction due to the federal courts having exclusive jurisdiction of all actions and proceedings against consuls, and claimed that he was immune from prosecution since the acts complained of were within the scope of his official duties. The court ruled that the federal courts were without jurisdiction to try alleged violations of state statutes. The court also determined that although article 5 of the Vienna Convention on Consular Relations, Apr. 24, 1963, art. 5, 21 U.S.T. 82-85, provided that petitioner may have had the right to assist his nationals in acquiring legal representation, the manner in which such an authorized function was carried out would determine whether the assistance was within the scope of his consular duties. In refusing to issue the writ, the court held that the state trial court was the appropriate forum for such a factual determination.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ and denied the request of petitioner, consul of the Republic of Mexico, for a writ of prohibition on respondent superior court continuing criminal proceedings against him. The court concluded that Congress never conferred jurisdiction over state criminal proceedings against consuls on the federal courts or deprived state courts of such jurisdiction.

Allen v. Jordanos' Inc.


Civ. No. 43395


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


52 Cal. App. 3d 160; 125 Cal. Rptr. 31; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1442



October 14, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 10, 1975, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 11, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 98679, John T. Rickard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed as to the action for breach of contract. The judgment of dismissal is reversed insofar as it relates to the defamation action, and the case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision. Each party to bear his or its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of judgment of Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which dismissed plaintiff's action pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581(3) after granting defendant employer's demurrer to the defamation claim in plaintiff's first amended complaint and the breach of contract claim in his fourth amended complaint on the ground of failure to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee filed defamation and breach of contract claims against defendant employer for alleged breach of its oral agreement to lay off plaintiff, which allowed him to obtain unemployment benefits, and to not communicate to third persons that plaintiff was discharged or resigned for dishonesty, theft, and bad employment attitude and that defendant would not rehire him. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrers to the defamation and breach of contract claims, and dismissed without leave to amend. The court held that, with regard to the defamation claim, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 830 violated due process and equal protection rights under U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7 and 15, because its bond requirement in effect deprived plaintiff of property prior to hearing. With regard to the breach of contract claim, the court found that the bargained-for consideration of defendant's silence as to the truth of plaintiff's unemployment was illegal and therefore voided the contract. The court affirmed dismissal of the contract claim and reversed and remanded on the defamation claim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal as to plaintiff employee's breach of contract claim because consideration was based on an illegal agreement to withhold facts from third parties. Dismissal on defamation was reversed and remanded because the bond requirement was an unconstitutional deprivation of property before a hearing.

Thayer v. Madigan


Civ. No. 35678


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


52 Cal. App. 3d 16; 125 Cal. Rptr. 28; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1430



October 9, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 4, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 441958, Spurgeon Avakian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, seven taxpayers, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), that granted summary judgment for respondents, sheriff and others, in an action that challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690.6(c).


OVERVIEW: Appellants, seven taxpayers, brought an action against respondents, sheriff and others, that sought a declaration that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690.6(c) unconstitutionally deprived impoverished persons of equal protection of the laws. Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690.6(c)(1) exempted from garnishment earnings of a debtor for personal services rendered within 30 days of levy except garnishment for debts incurred for the common necessaries of life. The lower court granted respondents' motion for summary judgment. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment because appellants did not cite and the court could not find any provision of either the state or federal constitution that expressly or impliedly guaranteed the right they sought, which was not to be deprived by the state of the very income one needed to furnish his family with adequate food, clothing and housing. The court held that the right that they sought was not a fundamental right and § 690.6(c)(1) was constitutional because the class of persons that appellants argued was harmed by the statute could not be identified or defined in customary equal protection terms.


OUTCOME: The order of the lower court that granted summary judgment for respondents, sheriff and others, was affirmed because the court, using the rational relationship test, determined that the statute at issue was not unconstitutional.

Tillie Lewis Foods, Inc. v. Pittsburg


Civ. No. 30324


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


52 Cal. App. 3d 983; 124 Cal. Rptr. 698; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1527



October 7, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied November 6, 1975, and the petitions of all the appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied December 17, 1975.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the city, its agencies, officers, and intervenors inhabitants of areas to be annexed by the city, sought review of a judgment entered by the Contra Costa County Superior Court (California), which invalidated and terminated certain annexation proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs corporations obtained a temporary restraining order to stay an annexation proceeding initiated by defendants, the city, its agencies, officers, and intervenors inhabitants of land, concerning unincorporated territory which defendant city sought to annex. Defendants sought mandamus relief from the temporary restraining order. Gov't Code § 54797; therefore, the LAFCO order was void and properly enjoined.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment in favor of plaintiff corporations and denied an application for writ of mandamus filed by defendants, the city, its agencies, officers, and intervenors inhabitants of land, because the trial court's findings were supported by substantial evidence and the local agency formation commission did not have the power to declare uninhabited land to be "inhabited" when reviewing an annexation proposal.

 

564 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Day v. City of Glendale


Civ. No. 45720


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


51 Cal. App. 3d 817; 124 Cal. Rptr. 569; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1414



September 30, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 28, 1975, and the petition of the real parties in interest and respondents Kirst-MacDonald-Hensler for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 25, 1975. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NC C5824G, David N. Eagleson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the cause is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellants' petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent city to amend its environmental guidelines and require an environmental impact report as a condition for the issuance of a grading permit to real parties in interest joint venture.


OVERVIEW: Appellant residents filed a petition for a writ of mandate seeking to compel respondent city to reconsider its environmental guidelines and its decision in not requiring real parties in interest joint venture to file an environmental impact report as a condition of the issuance of a grading permit. The lower court denied appellants' petition for a writ of mandate. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the lower court's order. The court held that the proposed project would have involved some discretionary acts on the part of respondent, which made the project discretionary within the meaning of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 6, and such project required the filing of an environmental impact report pursuant to the California Environmental Quality Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et. seq. The court further held that although the proposed project did appear to have characteristics of a discretionary project, as well as a ministerial project within the meaning of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 6, policy required that the project be construed as discretionary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the order of the lower court, which denied appellant residents' petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent city to amend its environmental guidelines and require an environmental impact report as a condition for the issuance of a grading permit. State law required the making of an environmental impact report because the proposed action required some discretionary decisions by respondent.

 

565 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Eckl v. Davis


Civ. No. 44984


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


51 Cal. App. 3d 831; 124 Cal. Rptr. 685; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1415



September 30, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 14, 1975, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 17, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 95509, David N. Eagleson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying a preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nudists sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellants a preliminary injunction in an action brought against respondents, city and its police chief. The action sought a declaration that an ordinance banning nudity in public places, which was passed by respondent city, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city, in response to increasing nudity in public places, passed an ordinance banning nudity in parks and on beaches. Appellant nudists brought an action seeking a declaration that the ordinance was unconstitutional because it was preempted by state law in the form of Cal. Penal Code § 314, that it violated the U.S. Const. amend. I right to free speech, and that there was no urgency to pass the ordinance. Appellants also sought a preliminary injunction preventing enforcement of the ordinance, which was denied. Appellants sought review. The court affirmed, holding that state law did not preempt the ordinance. The court stated that Cal. Penal Code § 314 proscribed conduct that was sexually motivated. However, the court determined that ordinance did not proscribe sexual conduct but protected the health, welfare, and morals of the community. Since the legislature had not enacted legislation on a statewide basis banning public nudity, there was no legislative intent to prohibit local legislation regarding public nudity, and therefore the ordinance was not preempted. The court further held that the ordinance proscribed conduct and did not infringe on free speech.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court denying appellant nudists a preliminary injunction in their declaratory judgment action that sought a declaration that an ordinance banning nudity in public places, which was passed by respondent city, was unconstitutional. The court held that the ordinance was not preempted by the penal code and was thus a valid exercise of respondent city's power to protect the health and welfare of its citizens.

 

566 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Coast-United Advertising, Inc. v. City of Long Beach


Civ. No. 45857


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


51 Cal. App. 3d 766; 124 Cal. Rptr. 487; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1408



September 26, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 6, 1975, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 11, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 92639, Roy J. Brown, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant advertising company sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which was granted to respondent city in appellant's action for declaratory relief and for preliminary and permanent injunctions.


OVERVIEW: Summary judgment was granted to respondent city in appellant advertising company's challenge to an ordinance passed by respondent that prohibited advertising on benches. The court affirmed the summary judgment because appellant's affidavits did not show that there was evidence requiring the weighing procedures of a trial. The court concluded that there had been no showing of unequal protection of the law because appellant's affidavits did not raise the kind of factual issues that would overcome the presumption of constitutionality enjoyed by the statutory classification. The affidavits merely pointed to discriminatory behavior that, even if proved, was allowable under the police power. The court determined that the trial court was correct in not ordering a continuance for discovery because the actual intent of the city council in enacting the ordinance was irrelevant and not a fact essential to justify opposition to the motion for summary judgment under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code 437c.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment granted to respondent city was affirmed because appellant advertising company's affidavits did not raise the kind of factual issues that would overcome the presumption of constitutionality enjoyed by the statutory classification in respondent's ordinance.

Simi Valley Recreation & Park Dist. v. Local Agency Formation Com.


Civ. No. 44715


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


51 Cal. App. 3d 648; 124 Cal. Rptr. 635; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1403



September 25, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 4, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. SP 47284, Jerome H. Berenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant development district entity appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which dismissed fourteen causes of action regarding land use rulings included in a petition for a writ of mandate.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. Appellant development district entity sought to have some 10,000 acres of undeveloped land within appellant's territory detached for purposes of commercial, industrial and residential development. Respondent commission rejected the detachment after hearing, and appellant alleged fourteen causes of action to allow for detachment. On appeal, the court held that the California Quality Environmental Act (CEQA), Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21050, was inapplicable to the detachment proceeding under the circumstances alleged and that the statute of limitations applicable to respondent determination precluded review. The court held that respondent's determination was consistent with the state and federal constitutions as there was no unconstitutional delegation of legislative power and the right to vote was not infringed. The court further held that, pursuant to common law, there was no right for independent review of each determination by respondent. The court, therefore, held that he trial court did not abuse its discretion..


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court on the grounds that respondent board's determination was not violative of state law or state and federal constitutions, and there was no right to independent review of each determination.

 

568 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


California School Employees Asso. v. Foothill Community College Dist.


Civ. No. 35541


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


52 Cal. App. 3d 150; 124 Cal. Rptr. 830; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1441



September 25, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 24, 1975, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 20, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 299492, John D. Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant employer sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted the petition of plaintiffs, employee and employee association, for a writ of mandate to reinstate plaintiff employee.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, employee and employee association, were granted a writ of mandate by the trial court after employer dismissed plaintiff employee for conduct unbecoming an employee in public service, under a rule set forth in defendant's staff handbook. The court affirmed the judgment because the rule at issue was vague on its face and defendant made no attempt to show any impairment or disruption of its functions a result of plaintiff employee's actions. The court determined that defendant's rule did not create a standard of conduct that was capable of objective interpretation by the district employees who must abide by it, the district officials who must enforce it, and by any judicial or administrative tribunal that might be called upon to review any disciplinary proceeding. The court concluded that there was no authoritative interpretation of the rule, and nothing in the language of the rule purported to limit unbecoming conduct to exclude constitutionally protected expressive conduct, whatever the intent of the framers of the rule.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting the writ of mandate to plaintiffs, employee and employee association, was affirmed. Defendant employer's rule did not create a standard of conduct that was capable of objective interpretation and defendant made no attempt to show any impairment or disruption of its functions a result of plaintiff employee's actions.

 

569 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


City of Los Angeles v. Crawshaw Mortgage & Inv. Co.


Civ. No. 46381


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


51 Cal. App. 3d 696; 124 Cal. Rptr. 363; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1405



September 25, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 20, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 26728, Thomas L. Griffith, Jr., Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered in favor of respondent city in an action to collect delinquent business taxes imposed by Los Angeles, Cal., Code § 21.108.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer challenged the constitutionality of a respondent city's business tax, Los Angeles, Cal., Code § 21.108, that imposed a tax on persons engaged in the business of lending money, but did not impose a tax on the business of lending money where the obligation was secured by a lien on real estate. Appellant contended that the distinction between money lenders who made loans secured by real property and those who made loans not secured by real property was an unjust discrimination that violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend XIV. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of respondent. The court held that in view of the essential differences in the methods of conducting a mortgage banking business and a business of making loans not secured by real property, the distinction between the two types of businesses for the purpose of taxation embodied in the municipal ordinance was not arbitrary or unreasonable. The court held that a municipality has broad power to classify for the purposes of taxation and that difference in the method of conducting a business is generally a sound basis for classification.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court entered in favor of respondent city because the statute imposing the taxes was not unconstitutional as applied to appellant taxpayer and any error by the trial court in excluding documentary evidence was harmless.

 

570 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Younger v. F. E. Crites, Inc.


Civ. No. 35934


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


51 Cal. App. 3d 961; 124 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1423



September 22, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, Nos. 141786, 141790, Robert J. Cooney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting preliminary injunction is reversed insofar as it permits the defendant F. E. Crites, Inc. to "hydraulically dredge up to 80,000 cubic yards of [or any] sand"; in all other respects it is affirmed. The People will recover their costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State appealed an order from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which granted state's preliminary injunction against defendant corporation, but allowed defendant to continue dredging and waste discharge operations, at legally imposed levels, pending further order from the trial court on the matter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant corporation began waste and dredging operations after it complied with permit and reporting requirements, which limited the amount defendant could dredge per year and required the work to be performed at the same location. Thereafter, without seeking a permit or filing a report, defendant significantly increased its dredging activity on the preexisting location and soon moved to a new location to dredge the same significant amount, all without complying with permit and reporting requirements. State filed a suit against defendant and sought a preliminary injunction to enjoin defendant from further dredging activity, which the trial court granted, but permitted defendant to continue dredging limited to the original permit, pending further order from the trial court on the matter. On state's appeal, the court held that the trial court did not have the power by injunction to restrain the permit and reporting agencies from enjoining defendant's actions because by policing dredging and waste activities, the agencies were performing official acts, which they were required by valid law to perform.


OUTCOME: The order granting state's preliminary injunction was reversed insofar as it permitted defendant corporation to continue its dredging activities pursuant to the original limitations, and affirmed in all other respects on the grounds that the lower court did not have the power to prevent the permit and reporting agencies from exercising their official duties of monitoring dredging activity as required by law.

 

571 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bartalo v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 46156


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


51 Cal. App. 3d 526; 124 Cal. Rptr. 370; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1393



September 19, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The order overruling the demurrer of Bartalo is reversed and the trial court is directed to sustain such demurrer without leave to amend. The alternative writ of mandate heretofore issued is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner tortfeasor sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to sustain petitioner's demurrer to an amended complaint that sought recovery for loss of consortium in a personal injury action brought by the real parties in interest, husband and wife.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner tortfeasor was a defendant in a personal injury case filed by real party in interest wife that arose out of an automobile accident that occurred on June 15, 1973. On August 21, 1974, California recognized that the spouse of an injured person in a tort action had a right of recovery for loss of consortium. Thereafter, respondent superior court allowed an amended complaint to be filed that added real party in interest husband as an additional plaintiff. After respondent denied petitioner's demurrer to real party in interest husband's claim, petitioner sought a writ of mandate. The court reversed respondent's order and directed the trial court to sustain the demurrer. The court held that real party in interest husband's loss of consortium claim was barred by the one-year statute of limitations for personal injury actions. Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340(3). The court held that real party in interest husband's claim did not relate back to the filing of his wife's claim because his claim was for a wholly different legal liability or obligation. It was not for her personal injuries but was for the separate and independent loss that he sustained.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of respondent superior court that overruled petitioner tortfeasor's demurer and directed the trial court to sustain the demurrer without leave to amend because real party in interest husband's claim for loss of consortium was barred by the statute of limitations and did not relate back to his wife's claim for personal injuries.

 

572 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Jefferson v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 36895


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


51 Cal. App. 3d 721; 124 Cal. Rptr. 507; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1407



September 15, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining respondent court from further proceedings under the information filed against petitioner, other than to dismiss the charges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court issued an alternative writ of prohibition to review an order of respondent the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied petitioner's motion to dismiss an information charging him with receiving stolen property in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 496. Petitioner contended that there was no probable cause for his arrest, and that the evidence from that arrest should have been suppressed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was arrested for violating Cal. Penal Code § 415 when a police officer heard him use profanity while petitioner was accompanying a woman at the police station. Upon his arrest, he was instructed to remove the contents of his pants, which revealed a stolen traveler's check. Petitioner was charged with receiving stolen property under Cal. Penal Code § 496. Respondent the Superior Court of Alameda County denied petitioner's motion to dismiss the information charging him with a violation of § 496. On review, the court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining respondent from further proceedings under the information filed against petitioner, other than to dismiss the charges. The court found that petitioner's arrest was invalid and that respondent and the magistrate erred in failing to suppress the evidence secured as a result of that arrest. There was no evidence that the epithet used by petitioner was directed at the arresting officer, or at anyone else in the hallway, or in the office, and considering the context in which the words were used, there was no clear and present danger that violence would have erupted.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining respondent the Superior Court of Alameda County from further proceedings under the information filed against petitioner charging him with receiving stolen property, other than to dismiss the charges. The initial arrest for disturbing the peace was not supported by probable cause and the trial court erred in failing to suppress the evidence secured as a result of that arrest.

People v. Pruett


Crim. No. 14657


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


51 Cal. App. 3d 329; 124 Cal. Rptr. 273; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1375



September 12, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On December 11, 1975, the judgment was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Justice Court for the Pleasanton Judicial District of Alameda County, No. LL 206T, William H. Gale, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Justice Court for the Pleasanton Judicial District denying petition to seal records is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This case was before the court upon certification by the Superior Court (California), and a transfer of the appeal by this court, for a determination of whether Cal. Penal Code § 851.7 was unconstitutional as a denial of equal protection. The superior court reversed an order from the justice court denying defendant's petition to seal his records pursuant to § 851.7, and ordered defendant's arrest record sealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, while a minor, was arrested and charged in the justice court with two misdemeanor violations under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530, possession of marijuana, and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11556, being in a place where marijuana was being used. Two days later, the charges were withdrawn and the case dismissed. Defendant petitioned for an order sealing the records in the case, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 851.7, and the petition was denied. The superior court reversed, and upon certification by the superior court, this court transferred the appeal to determine the constitutionality of § 851.7. The court held that to the extent that § 851.7 prohibits the sealing of arrest records of narcotics offenses charged as misdemeanors, it was unconstitutional as a denial of equal protection of the laws. The court found no justification for sealing the record of a minor who has been convicted and has received relief under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.4, and excluding from the sealing privilege the record of a minor who has suffered an arrest but who had the charges against him dismissed. The court reversed the order of the justice court denying the petition to seal defendant's records.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the justice court denying the petition to seal defendant's records, finding that the statute that prohibited the sealing of arrest records of narcotics offenses charged as misdemeanors, was unconstitutional as a denial of equal protection of the laws.

In re Mahood


Crim. No. 14356


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


51 Cal. App. 3d 248; 124 Cal. Rptr. 404; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1369



September 11, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 6, 1975.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged; the petition for habeas corpus is denied insofar as it seeks petitioner's release, and the director of corrections is directed to recompute petitioner's minimum sentence and minimum eligible parole date in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was a return of an order to show cause issued by the supreme court (California), which was issued in response to petitioner's request for habeas corpus claiming that the Department of Corrections erred in fixing his minimum parole date in a corrected notice of legal status.


OVERVIEW: The attorney general filed an order to show cause on behalf of the director of the Department of Corrections (Department) in response to petitioner's habeas corpus petition. Petitioner claimed that the Department erred in fixing his minimum parole date in a corrected Notice of Legal Status. Petitioner sought relief on the grounds that the corrected notice of legal status, which increased his minimum eligible parole date, was an ex post facto attempt to increase the punishment for his crimes. On a return to an order to show cause, the court determined that the Department erred in computing the minimum term and eligible parole date in the amended notice. The court held that there was no question that the Department's corrected notice of status that increased petitioner's sentencing was an ex post facto punishment. The court held that Cal. Penal Code §§ 3024(d), 3049, which relates to minimum sentences and minimum eligible parole dates, were to be applied generally to all sentences prescribed by law. The court discharged the order to show cause, and directed the Department to recompute petitioner's minimum sentence and eligible parole date.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause, and found that the Department of Corrections (Department) erred in fixing petitioner's minimum parole date in the corrected notice of legal status. The court directed the Department to recompute petitioner's sentencing.

 

575 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Rosato v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 2623


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


51 Cal. App. 3d 190; 124 Cal. Rptr. 427; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1368; 1 Media L. Rep. 2560



September 8, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 20, 1975. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of contempt are annulled as to the questions appearing on Appendix "A" as numbers 8, 9, 11, 12, 13, 14, 15 and 23 asked of Rosato, numbers 3, 8, 11, 14, 20, 21, 22 and 24 asked of Patterson, and as numbers 9 and 10 asked of Bort. The judgments are affirmed as to the balance of the questions asked of each petitioner; in view of the understandable uncertainty as to the scope of the privilege, and believing the petitioners refused to answer the latter questions in good faith, fairness requires that the cause be and it hereby is remanded to the superior court for the purpose of affording each of the petitioners an opportunity to purge his contempts before his sentence is executed and for such further proceedings as may be appropriate under the circumstances.

Each party shall bear his or its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, newspaper reporters, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which held them in contempt for refusing to answer questions as to where they obtained a grand jury transcript, that were published in newspaper articles concerning criminal defendants, who were subject of the grand jury transcripts. Petitioners argued they were not required to answer the question under Cal. Evid. Code § 1070(a).


OVERVIEW: Petitioners published newspaper articles concerning sealed grand jury material involved three criminal defendants. Respondent trial court ordered petitioners to answer where they obtained the grand jury material. Petitioners refused to answer, arguing that they had a journalist's privilege under Cal. Evid. Code § 1070(a), U.S. Const. amend. I, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2. Respondent held petitioners in contempt, and petitioners filed an appeal. The court held that neither the U.S. Constitution nor the state constitution's free speech clause protected petitioners, because the court had a fundamental right to protect its orders. The court also determined that journalists did not have any more right than the average citizen to disobey court orders. The court further stated that § 1070(a) did protect petitioners from revealing their source, but that it was unconstitutional as applied to court officers, because it violated the separation of powers. Finally the court ordered that petitioners could be ordered to answer any question concerning the leak of the grand jury materials as it applied to court personnel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgment of the lower court, and held that petitioner newspaper reporters could be held in contempt for refusing to answer questions as to where they obtained sealed grand jury materials. However, petitioners could not be compelled to answer any question concerning whether they obtained the material from non-court officials under the shield law. The court remanded for further proceedings.

In re Marriage of Pilcher


Civ. No. 15157


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


51 Cal. App. 3d 142; 123 Cal. Rptr. 868; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1363



September 2, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. FL 5701, Willis T. Lyman, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order denying appellant's motion is reversed. Each party to bear his own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which denied appellant's motion to terminate child support payments for his son who had reached 18 years old.


OVERVIEW: A trial court entered an interlocutory judgment that awarded custody of a child to respondent mother and ordered appellant father to pay child support until the child reached the age of 21. Appellant filed a petition for modification of the interlocutory judgment that sought to terminate the child support provisions when the child reached the age of 18. The trial court denied appellant's petition because appellant and respondent had agreed in open court that appellant would support the child until he reached the age of 21. On appeal, the court held that the trial court was without jurisdiction to extend child support payments beyond the age of 18. The trial court's order was made after the effective date of Cal. Civ. Code § 25 which lowered the age of majority from the age of 21 to 18. The court held that absent evidence of an agreement that has been integrated into the divorce decree, appellant's liability for child support terminated when the child reached the age of 18. The court reversed the trial court's judgment, and reversed the order denying appellant's motion for termination of child support payments when the child reached the age of 18.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that denied appellant father's motion to terminate child support payments because the trial court was without jurisdiction to extend the child support payments beyond the age of 18.

Citizens for Parental Rights v. San Mateo County Bd. of Education


Civ. No. 33547


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


51 Cal. App. 3d 1; 124 Cal. Rptr. 68; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1350; 82 A.L.R.3d 544



August 28, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 26, 1975, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 23, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 139710, Robert D. Carter, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: We conclude, therefore, that the trial court properly concluded that, accepting the various allegations of the complaint to be true, the parents have failed to raise any substantial constitutional issues 33 or any factual issues that would entitle them to the declaratory or injunctive relief sought. Accordingly, the county's motion to dismiss the entire complaint was properly granted and the judgment appealed from is affirmed.



33 (Cf. Hobolth v. Greenway (1974) 52 Mich.App. 682 [218 N.W.2d 98].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an unincorporated association of parents and as individual parents, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which dismissed their seventh amended complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief, to prevent the implementation of family life and sex education programs by respondent school board.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, an unincorporated association of parents and as individual parents, sought review of the superior court's judgment which dismissed their complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent the implementation of family life and sex education programs by respondent school board. The court affirmed and held that Cal. Educ. Code §§ 8506, 8701 were constitutional, and that the state's excusal system under Cal. Educ. Code §§ 8506, 8701, 8506 permitted parents to remove children from any programs they found offensive; and as such, the programs were in no manner compulsory. The court held that programs which applied to all students equally, without discrimination, created no denial of equal protection. Objectors did not have the constitutional right to preclude other students' involvement in these programs merely because the objectors believed their own personal rights were impaired. The court further provided a free exercise clause balancing test as well as a three-pronged analysis for determining when a restriction or regulation of conduct, based on religious belief, would be upheld without further inquiry.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint of appellants, an unincorporated association of parents and as individual parents, because there was no violation of constitutional rights under any theory of appellants and appellants failed to raise any substantial constitutional question.

City of San Jose v. Donohue


Civ. No. 35330


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


51 Cal. App. 3d 40; 123 Cal. Rptr. 804; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1351



August 28, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 26, 1975, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 23, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 277500, James Duvaras, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city brought an action in small claims court to recover an unpaid utility tax charged to defendant city resident. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the ordinance, and the case was brought to the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which upheld the validity of the ordinance. Defendant appealed that the ordinance violated the equal protection and due process clauses of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city resident challenged the constitutionality of an ordinance which imposed a tax upon utility users. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which upheld the validity of the ordinance and required defendant to pay the tax to plaintiff city. The tax was for utilities, and was collected by the utility companies. The court found that the ordinance, as written and applied, did not violate the equal protection and due process clauses of the United States Constitution because, although certain entities were excluded from the tax, there was a rational basis and the facts supported the finding of the rational basis. The court also found that the penalty provision of the ordinance did not violate the due process clause of the United States Constitution. The court found that there was no violation because the purpose of the penalty was to induce prompt payment and that it was sufficiently substantial in amount to make sure that result was achieved. The court also found that the evidence established that the cost of collection was over the amount of the penalty and, therefore, the penalty was neither arbitrary nor excessive in amount.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of plaintiff city and found that the ordinance which required city residents to pay utility taxes through their utility companies was not unconstitutional. The court found that there was a rational basis for the tax, that the penalties were proper, and that because there was rational basis, there was no violation of the due process or equal protection clauses of the United States Constitution.

 

579 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Dabis v. San Francisco Redevelopment Agency


Civ. No. 34731


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


50 Cal. App. 3d 704; 122 Cal. Rptr. 800; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1338



August 19, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 621714, Robert J. Drewes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the new trial is reversed; the judgment of the trial court is reinstated and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted respondent San Francisco Redevelopment Agency's motion for a new trial in appellants' action for property damages, on grounds that the trial court erred in giving a jury instruction that Urban Renewal Handbook § 7211.1 created a legal duty requiring respondent to provide security to the property.


OVERVIEW: In an action for property damage resulting from repeated burglaries, appellant property owners, which property was adjacent to respondent San Francisco Redevelopment Agency's vacant, unsecured building, sought review of the trial court's order granting respondent's motion for a new trial based on the trial court's perceived error in instructing the jury that respondent had a legal duty to provide security to area property under Urban Renewal Handbook § RHA 7211.1. The court reversed the trial court's order granting a new trial, holding that because § RHA 7211.1 was promulgated pursuant to the express statutory authority of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1455(c)(1), was incorporated into the permanent federal Department of Housing and Urban Development handbook, and was phrased in mandatory language, the regulation was binding upon respondent. The court further found that § RHA 72211.1 was not vague, overbroad, or contrary to other instructions. Thus, because respondent did not provide the protection to appellants' adjacent property as required by § RHA 7211.1, respondent was negligent per se, and the trial court's original judgment was affirmed and reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order granting respondent San Francisco Redevelopment Agency's motion for a new trial in appellant property owners action for property damages, because the Urban Renewal Handbook created a legal duty on the part of respondent to provide security to appellant's property, and thus the trial court did not err in giving the instruction. The court affirmed and reinstated the trial court's original judgment.

People v. Wilson


Crim. No. 13620


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


50 Cal. App. 3d 811; 123 Cal. Rptr. 663; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1343



August 12, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied October 9, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 88492, Claude D. Perasso, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is reversed and the trial court is directed to dismiss count I, Penal Code, section 664 (attempted second degree robbery). Judgment is affirmed as to count II, Penal Code, section 220 (assault with intent to commit robbery).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted him, after trial by jury, of attempt to commit second degree robbery, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 664, and assault with intent to commit robbery, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 220.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of attempt to commit second degree robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 664, and assault with intent to commit robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 220. On appeal, the court reversed as to the § 664 violation because it was necessarily included within assault with intent to commit robbery, and defendant's conviction for both was impermissible. The court held that the lower court did not commit reversible error in failing to instruct the jury that corpus delicti must be proved independent of admission and that it properly instructed the jury that insulting words were no justification. The court found no cruel and unusual punishment because the maximum sentence did not shock the conscience and was not grossly out of proportion to sentences for similar crimes in other jurisdictions. The court held that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in admitting a prior felony conviction of burglary for impeachment purposes because it had bearing on defendant's veracity. The lower court did not err in allowing evidence of a prior uncharged offense of robbery that was relevant to defendant's intent or in allowing a spontaneous statement as admissible hearsay.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for attempt to commit second degree robbery because it was necessarily included within the offense of assault with intent to commit robbery, and defendant could not be convicted for both. The court affirmed the assault conviction because there was no reversible error in allowing evidence of prior wrongs and hearsay or in instructing the jury on intent, and because there was no excessive punishment.

Aubry v. Gadbois


Civ. No. 45029


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


50 Cal. App. 3d 470; 123 Cal. Rptr. 365; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1311
