DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendant juvenile court judge's motion for judgment on the pleadings in plaintiff's action that sought to enjoin the illegal expenditure of public funds and to obtain declaratory relief with respect to prehearing detention of juveniles.


OVERVIEW: The lower court granted defendant juvenile court judge's motion for judgment on the pleadings in plaintiff taxpayer's action that sought to enjoin the illegal expenditure of public funds and to obtain declaratory relief with respect to prehearing detention of juveniles. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the denial of bail and detention for up to 15 judicial days for juveniles under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 635, 636 was not unconstitutional. The court emphasized that although adult defendants were entitled to bail, the distinction did not deny juveniles due process or equal protection because the purpose of the detention was to protect and benefit the minors, not the public.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order that granted defendant juvenile court judge's motion for judgment on the pleadings in plaintiff taxpayer's action and held that the denial of bail for juveniles was not unconstitutional because the purpose of the detention was to protect and benefit the minors themselves.

 

582 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Reed v. California Coastal Zone Conservation Com.


Civ. No. 36603


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


55 Cal. App. 3d 889; 127 Cal. Rptr. 786; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1844



August 5, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 34395, Timothy W. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant conservation commission sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which granted a writ of mandate in favor of respondent property owners and made a determination that the permit procedure required by the California Coastal Zone Conservation Act was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent property owners were issued a building permit for their proposed motel, which was within the permit zone of the California Coastal Zone Conservation Act (act). On appeal and after a public hearing, appellant conservation commission denied respondents' application for the permit. The trial court found that the permit procedure required by the act imposed an unreasonable limitation on respondents' rights as owners of land and constituted a taking of private property without due process of law. The court reversed and held that the trial court had violated principles of statutory construction and erroneously read Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 27001 to mean that the permit procedure required respondents to concede that their property was held in trust for all the people, and therefore was unconstitutional. The court held that the act was not unconstitutional and that the permit procedure was a proper regulation for protecting natural resources in the coastal area until the completion of a conservation and development plan. The court found that the allocation of burden of proof to respondents and the time limitation for presentation at the hearing were reasonable and necessary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of respondent property owners and held that the California Coastal Zone Conservation Act (act) itself and the underlying permit procedure were constitutional and had not effectuated an unlawful taking of private land for public use. The court found that the act was an appropriate regulation for protecting natural resources in the coastal area until the completion of a conservation plan.

 

583 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rodriquez


Crim. No. 7235


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


50 Cal. App. 3d 389; 123 Cal. Rptr. 185; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1305



August 1, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 20, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-11855, Francis M. Estudillo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted him of violating Cal. Penal Code § 4574, possession of a deadly weapon in county jail.


OVERVIEW: A razor blade was found in the possession of defendant while in county jail. Defendant was charged and convicted of violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4574, possession of a deadly weapon in county jail. Defendant appealed the conviction. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that, contrary to defendant's contention, specific intent was not an element of the prima facie case for a violation of § 4574, and although the jury was improperly instructed in that regard, such instruction was not reversible error, because it was vastly in favor of defendant. In addition, the court held that proof of defendant's intended violent use of the razor blade was not necessary. The court rejected defendant's contention that the term "deadly weapon" rendered § 4574 unconstitutionally vague because "deadly weapon" was not ambiguous or vague, and § 4574 gave defendant adequate notice that possession of a razor blade in county jail was proscribed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant inmate's conviction and held that specific intent was not an element of the prima facie case of the charged offense, the improper jury instruction was not reversible error, proof of defendant's intended violent use of the razor was not necessary, and the language of the charged offense gave defendant adequate notice that possession of a razor blade in county jail was proscribed.

Espinosa v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 15089


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


50 Cal. App. 3d 347; 123 Cal. Rptr. 448; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1302



July 31, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The instant factual situation is therefore not within the exceptions to section 1418 contained in section 1419. Since section 1418 imposes a mandatory duty upon the court in the existent circumstances, a peremptory writ of mandate will issue directing the trial court to vacate its orders confiscating petitioner's property and denying petitioner's motions for return of his property and to order the same restored to him.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner gun owner appealed an order of respondent Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which denied his motions to vacate an order confiscating his personal property and to return evidence to him pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1418.

Upon his arrest at his residence, petitioner had two handguns, a rifle, and ammunition in his immediate possession, which were seized by the police. Ultimately, petitioner was acquitted of the criminal charge. Instead of returning the weapons and ammunition to petitioner, respondent superior court offered petitioner his choice between the two handguns and ordered confiscation of the remaining handgun, the rifle and the ammunition. On appeal, the court issued a writ of mandate directing respondent to vacate its orders confiscating petitioner's property and denying petitioner's motions for return of his property, and ordered the same restored to him, holding that neither Cal. Penal Code § 12028 nor Cal. Penal Code § 1419 authorized the confiscation, sale, retention, or destruction of petitioner's weapons, and the factual situation was not within the exceptions to § 1418 contained in Cal. Penal Code § 1419.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate that directed respondent superior court to vacate its orders confiscating petitioner gun owner's property and denying his motions for return of his property, and ordered the same restored to him, because there was no statutory authorization for the confiscation, sale, retention, or destruction of petitioner's weapons.

Alonso v. State of California

Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One

The court held that the denial of benefits to appellant was consistent with federal legislation pertaining to immigration.

People v. Getty


Crim. No. 26393


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


50 Cal. App. 3d 101; 123 Cal. Rptr. 704; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1282



July 23, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A584640, Robert P. Schifferman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The superior court order of September 27, 1974, is reversed. The March 1, 1974 (entered Mar. 4, 1974) order committing defendant to the Youth Authority is modified as follows: The sentence reading "Court orders placement in Ventura School for Girls." is deleted, and in its place is substituted a sentence reading "Court recommends placement in Ventura School for Girls."


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus, discharged defendant from the custody of the California Youth Authority, and released defendant on probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to second degree burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 459 and was sentenced to six months in county jail. Execution of sentence was suspended and she was granted three years' probation. Defendant was found to be in violation of probation, her probation was revoked, and she was committed to the California Youth Authority. Though defendant filed a notice of appeal and a petition for writ of habeas corpus with the court, defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus was heard in the trial court, which released defendant from custody, recalled her commitment to the Youth Authority, and restored her probation. On appeal, the court reversed, concluding that the trial court had no jurisdiction to modify its order of commitment because defendant had begun serving her sentence. At the time of the hearing, defendant's appeal was pending. The trial court failed to meet the procedural requirements of a proper writ of habeas corpus to initiate any proceedings that could lead to defendant's release because the state was deprived of its right to be heard by way of a return to any writ granted.


OUTCOME: On appeal, the court reversed the grant of defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus, because the trial court was without jurisdiction to modify its commitment order. Defendant had begun serving her sentence, her appeal was pending, and the procedurally deficient writ of habeas corpus could not release defendant from custody where the state was deprived of its right to be heard.

Lampley v. Alvares


Civ. No. 14601


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


50 Cal. App. 3d 124; 123 Cal. Rptr. 181; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1284



July 23, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Shasta County, No. 48420, Richard B. Eaton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons previously stated, the judgment is reversed with directions to the superior court to issue the peremptory writ as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner debtor appealed an order of the Superior Court of Shasta County (California), which denied his application for writ of mandate after respondent constable refused to accept petitioner's claim of exemption from real party in interest judgment creditor's writ of execution without payment of a fee by petitioner.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest judgment creditor of petitioner debtor directed respondent constable to levy a writ of execution on petitioner's bank account. Petitioner attempted to file a claim of exemption pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690.18 with respondent. Respondent refused to accept the claim of exemption without payment by petitioner of a fee, asserting as authority for such payment Cal. Gov't. Code § 26721. Petitioner's application for writ of mandate was denied. On appeal, the court reversed with directions to the trial court to issue the peremptory writ, holding that respondent acted beyond his authority in the absence of any provision or requirement for petitioner's payment of a filing fee because respondent was required to secure his fee in the manner prescribed by Cal. Gov't. Code § 26722. Petitioner's due process and equal protection claims were not reached because the court's conclusion on petitioner's first contention was dispositive of the appeal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of petitioner debtor's writ of mandate with directions for the trial court to issue the peremptory writ, holding that respondent constable acted beyond his authority because respondent was required to secure his fee in the manner prescribed by statute, which he failed to do here.

 

588 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kirk


Crim. No. 13446


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


49 Cal. App. 3d 765; 122 Cal. Rptr. 653; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1250



July 8, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 87302, Donald B. Constine, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant accused sought review of the judgment, following a jury verdict, by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), committing appellant to a state hospital as a mentally disordered sex offender, pursuant to Cal. Wel. & Inst. § 6300.


OVERVIEW: Appellant accused was found in violation of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6300 by jury trial and committed to a state hospital as a mentally disordered sex offender by judgment of the trial court. On appeal, appellant contended that the preponderance of evidence standard of proof in mentally disordered sex offender proceedings and the lack of a requirement for a unanimous verdict constituted an unconstitutional denial of appellant's Sixth and Fourteenth Amendment rights, U.S. Const. amend. VI and amend. XIV as well as his rights under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, holding that, because commitment under § 6300 infringed on the liberty rights guarded by the constitutions, such commitment on a preponderance of the evidence and without unanimity of the jury did, indeed, infringe appellant's constitutional rights. On an ancillary note, the court observed that the use of the word "dangerous" in § 6300 was not so vague or ambiguous to violate due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which committed appellant accused to a state hospital as a mentally disturbed sex offender, holding that the limitation of constitutional rights in such a judgment was severe enough to warrant a jury verdict based on greater proof than merely preponderance of the evidence. The court also held that the verdict must be unanimous.

* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment by the Superior Court of Lake County (California), which convicted him of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11357 involving possession of marijuana.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for transportation of heroin under 21 U.S.C.S. § 174 because the court held that the presumption of guilt arising from possession of the drug was not unconstitutional. The jury could infer from defendant's unexplained possession that the heroin was unlawfully obtained. The court also affirmed defendant's conviction under 18 U.S.C.S. § 545 for importation of marijuana because the court held this charge met the requirements of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531. The court held that an absolute prohibition on defendant receiving parole consideration for at least five years under Cal. Health & Safety § 11357 violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 6. The court held that defendant could be considered for parole before serving five years if the California Adult Authority deemed it appropriate under defendant's sentence of five years to life imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed but the California Adult Authority was directed to grant parole consideration to defendant at such time as was otherwise appropriate under the laws of the state and that a five year mandantory active sentence was not required.

Kramer v. Municipal Court

Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


49 Cal. App. 3d 418; 122 Cal. Rptr. 672; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1223



June 26, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On July 16, 1975, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an alleged criminal defendant, challenged the denial of her sought-after peremptory writ of mandate from respondent Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California). Petitioner contended that it was trial court error to deny the writ because of the wrongful termination of her drug diversion program.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, an alleged criminal defendant, was charged with felony possession of heroin. After arraignment, the matter was referred to the probation department for investigation and recommendation on whether petitioner's case could be diverted into a drug treatment program. Diversion was recommended and the case was diverted on the condition that petitioner participate in the program and cooperate with the probation department. The court reversed the lower court and issued a peremptory writ of mandate ordering respondent court to set aside its order terminating petitioner's diversion. It held that respondent had jurisdiction to terminate, but that petitioner was entitled to a hearing.

Townsend v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 44591


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


49 Cal. App. 3d 263; 122 Cal. Rptr. 500; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1204; 12 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1517; 10 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P10,425



June 20, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SEC 11323, Vincent S. Dalsimer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of the summary judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) granted in favor of defendant employer. Plaintiff contended that Cal. Gov't Code § 31671.01 was unconstitutional in that it deprived him of his right to earn an economic livelihood, that any mandatory retirement age was unconstitutional, and that defendant's 65-year mandatory retirement plan was a denial of equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee, a public defender, was forced to retire from the service of defendant employer at the age of 65. Defendant had adopted a pension plan that required county employees to retire at age 65. The trial court granted summary judgment for defendant. Defendant challenged the lower court's judgment on constitutional grounds. The court affirmed. The court held that the mandatory retirement provisions in question achieved a permissible and logical end. In addition, the means chosen are reasonable and rational. The stated purpose of Cal. Gov't Code § 31671.01, the provision upon which defendant's program was based, was to guarantee to all citizens a fair and equal opportunity for employment in the various county offices governed by the provision. In addition, it was designed to establish conditions of employment and to promote economy and efficiency in such offices. The court found nothing arbitrary, discriminatory, or unreasonable in establishing maximum retirement ages. Consequently, the court found no denial of equal protection, or any other constitutional defect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that plaintiff employee had not been denied equal protection and had not been denied of his right to earn an economic livelihood. The action taken was not arbitrary or discriminatory. In addition, his fundamental right to pursue a lawful occupation did not extend to the right to work for a particular employer.

Cooper v. Board of Medical Examiners


Civ. No. 35011


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


49 Cal. App. 3d 931; 123 Cal. Rptr. 563; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1266



June 18, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. M 6038, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying relief in mandamus is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant psychologist sought review of a judgment entered by the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) which denied his petition for mandamus relief to set aside respondent, Board of Medical Examiners' action revoking his license.


OVERVIEW: After respondent, Board of Medical Examiners revoked his license to practice, appellant psychologist contended that respondent's statutory and rule-making authority was vague, and also claimed numerous violations of the Administrative Procedures Act (APA), Cal. Gov't Code § 11500 et seq. The court affirmed the revocation. The court held that respondent and its committee's statutory rule-making authority was sufficiently clear to allow regulation in accord with the standards of the profession. The court held that it was not required to independently review the evidence; rather, revocation would be sustained if based upon any credible, competent evidence. The court found that the pleadings were sufficiently clear to apprise appellant of the bases for the charges, and that the procedural notice he received was issued early enough to allow appellant sufficient time to prepare his defense. Finally, the court held that respondent's executive sessions, conducted during the hearing, did not violate the APA because the sessions were used for the purpose of deciding procedural, not substantive issues, and that all such matters were placed on the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied appellant psychologist's petition for mandamus relief to set aside respondent, Board of Medical Examiners' decision to revoke appellant's license. The court held that respondent and its committee's statutory rule-making authority was sufficiently clear to allow regulation in accord with the standards of the profession, and that the evidence was sufficient to warrant the revocation.

Environmental Law Fund, Inc. v. Town of Corte Madera


Civ. No. 33766


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


49 Cal. App. 3d 105; 122 Cal. Rptr. 282; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1189



June 13, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Marin County, No. 63461, Joseph G. Wilson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town and appellant real estate developers, the real parties in interest, sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Marin County (California), which invalidated appellant town's grant of a conditional use permit to appellant real estate developers because it violated the California Environmental Quality Act of 1970, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant real estate developers, the real parties in interest, applied to appellant town to pre-zone their property for multiple residential. After at least two public hearings on the matter, a tentative subdivision map was approved. Following the approval of a conditional use permit for the development, appellant developers spent less than $ 15,000 on improvements to the property. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that the conditional use permit granted by appellant town to appellant real estate developers, the real parties in interest, was invalid because it conflicted with state law.

 

595 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hyde


Crim. No. 24351


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


49 Cal. App. 3d 97; 122 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1188



June 12, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A104198, A107588, William H. Rosenthal, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order (denying credit for presentence time in custody) is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's motion for credit for presentence jail time after appellant was convicted of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211.


OVERVIEW: While a fugitive from justice, appellant inmate was held in an out-of-state jail for 26 days and by the Los Angeles Police Department for 1 day. After appellant's conviction of robbery, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, the Adult Authority refused to give appellant credit for the 27 days at issue. Lower court denied appellant's motion for presentence credit for these 27 days. On appeal, the court reversed lower court's denial of presentence credit. The court determined that appellant was merely requesting that the lower court supplement its judgment and advise the Adult Authority of the simple fact that he had served the 27 days. The court found that lower court held authority under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 187 and 128 to transmit the appropriate facts to the Adult Authority. In the court's view, the ultimate obligation to allow presentence credit rested with the Adult Authority under Cal. Penal Code § 2940. On remand, lower court was directed to ascertain the amount of presentence confinement served by appellant and issue an appropriate order to the Adult Authority to credit appellant accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court reversed lower court's denial of presentence credit after appellant was convicted of robbery. The court held that lower court had jurisdiction to entertain the matter because it had jurisdiction to enforce its commands. On remand, the lower court was directed to ascertain the amount of presentence confinement served by appellant and to issue an appropriate order to give credit to appellant accordingly.

 

596 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Cooper v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 44153


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


49 Cal. App. 3d 34; 122 Cal. Rptr. 464; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1183



June 11, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 11241, Richard Leslie Wells, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter is remanded to the trial court to make new and more specific findings in light of our opinion and to enter judgment based on those findings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner, a real party in interest, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found that defendant Los Angeles County's zoning ordinance was void under Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21170 after plaintiff landowner filed an amended complaint alleging noncompliance with the California Environmental Quality Act of 1970, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowner, a real party in interest, owned property zoned for residential use. Upon appellant's request, defendant County of Los Angeles rezoned the property to recreational and resort use by ordinance. After plaintiff landowner alleged noncompliance with the California Environmental Quality Act of 1970, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq., the trial court found the rezoning ordinance void, holding that the saving provisions of Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21170 did not protect appellant. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that the trial court incorrectly interpreted § 21170, which, as the court interpreted the statute, saved or validated appellant if he completed a large part of the project or incurred a large percentage of the total liability of the project. In the court's view, the trial court misinterpreted § 21170's use of the word "substantial" so as to impose too many conditions on the qualification of appellant's project to satisfy § 21170 for application for relief under Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 21169. On remand, the trial court was directed to make new and more specific findings as to the magnitude of appellant's liability incurred in the project.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's order voiding defendant County of Los Angeles's ordinance rezoning appellant landowner's property, holding that appellant was protected under the California Environmental Quality Act of 1970 if he completed a large part of the project or incurred a large percentage of the total liability of the project. On remand, the trial court was directed to make new findings as to appellant's liability.

 

597 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Gonzalez v. Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board


Civ. No. 14287


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


49 Cal. App. 3d 280; 122 Cal. Rptr. 515; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1206; 40 Cal. Comp. Cases 371



June 10, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 25, 1975, and Petitioners' Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 6, 1975.



DISPOSITION: The award is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurance company sought annulment of the compensation award granted by the California Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board to respondent widow on the basis that she must have lived in the United States to be presumed wholly dependent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent widow was awarded compensation from the California Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board as a wholly dependant surviving widow after her husband died in an industrial accident. Petitioner insurance company wanted the award annulled due to respondent's immigration status. The court affirmed the award and found that respondent was a resident alien for the purpose of Cal. Lab. Code § 3500 which meant that she was entitled to the presumption of dependence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of workmen's compensation benefits to respondent widow. The court found that respondent's status as a resident alien governed, regardless of how long she actually resided in the United States, and the presumption that she had been wholly dependant on her husband was applicable.

 

598 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Coffee-Rich, Inc. v. Fielder


Civ. No. 44921


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


48 Cal. App. 3d 990; 122 Cal. Rptr. 302; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1173



June 9, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 3, 1975, and the Petition of the Plaintiffs and Appellants for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 6, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 943548, Samuel L. Kurland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporations sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which on remand from the court after reversal of a judgment reached at trial, entered a new finding of fact and made declarations pertaining to the application of certain provision of Cal. Food & Agric. Code §§ 38901 through 38987. Defendants were the Department of Agriculture and its representatives.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporations brought suit against defendants, Department of Agriculture and its representatives, seeking a declaration that the enactment of Cal. Food & Agric. Code §§ 38901 through 38987 dealing with products resembling milk products were either inapplicable to their product or were unconstitutional as applied to their product. The trial court entered judgment. Both parties appealed the judgment. The court reversed and remanded the matter to the trial court. The parties both appealed again. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the trial court lacked authority to change its finding of facts, but that there was no prejudice because its consequence was not carried into the judgment. The court further held that the conclusion by the trial court on remand that the facts, as determined by it, established that plaintiffs' powdered product did not meet the statutory definition of a product resembling a milk product was proper. The court found that it was not included in the statutory definition because the application of the definition was excluded by an approved finding of fact.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court after remand, holding that the court lacked authority to change its finding of facts but no prejudice resulted. The court further held that plaintiff corporation's powdered product did not meet the statutory definition of a product resembling a milk product because the application of the definition was excluded by an approved finding of fact.

 

599 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Oliver & Williams Elevator Corp. v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 42844


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


48 Cal. App. 3d 890; 122 Cal. Rptr. 249; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1166



June 5, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 2, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C968852, Samuel Greenfield, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to the superior court to enter judgment on the complaint in favor of plaintiff Oliver & Williams Elevator Corp., a corporation, in the sum of $ 5,172.82 with interest at 6 percent per annum from October 17, 1968 until paid and on the supplemental complaint in favor of said plaintiff in the sum of $ 9,931.69 with interest at 6 percent per annum from April 23, 1971 until paid together with costs of suit.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Board of Equalization, challenged a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which favored appellee elevator corporation in its action seeking a refund of sales and use taxes assessed by appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant board of equalization challenged a decision by the superior court favoring appellee elevator corporation in its action seeking a refund of sales and use taxes assessed by appellant against appellee. Appellee challenged the taxes arguing that its elevators and elevator components were not fixtures within the purview as Cal. Code Regs., tit. 18, § 1521, also known as rule number 11, that the component parts of an elevator were materials, not fixtures, and that rule number 11 was unconstitutional in violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. After a nonjury trial, the superior court held appellant's decision was an incorrect, arbitrary and unreasonable classification. Appellant challenged the decision. The court stated that the issue presented was a question of law reviewable and determinable by the appellate court. The court reversed, holding that under the circumstances, it was not arbitrary, unreasonable or capricious or without reasonable or rational basis in fact for appellant to classify an elevator cab and its components as a fixture of the building. The court held that the trial court had no right to substitute its own policy judgment for that of appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the superior court which favored appellee elevator corporation in its action challenging taxes imposed by appellant board of equalization. The court held that the trial court was not entitled to substitute its own judgment for that of appellant and that based on the stipulated facts, appellant's classification of appellee was neither arbitrary, unreasonable or capricious, or without rational basis in fact.

 

600 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Currier v. Pasadena


Civ. No. 45313


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


48 Cal. App. 3d 810; 121 Cal. Rptr. 913; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1158



June 4, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 24, 1975, and Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 30, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA 000066, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment appealed from is reversed; the appeal from the order is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that held that Pasadena, Cal., Ordinance 5121, which provided for warrantless searches of homes as a condition to issuance of a certificate of occupancy, was unconstitutional as violative of U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city sought review of the ruling of the trial court that held that the Pasadena, Cal., Municipal Ordinance 5121 was unconstitutional as a violation of U.S. Const. amend. IV's prohibition against warrantless searches of private homes. The ordinance provided for an inspection of single and multi-family residences as a condition to issuance of a certificate of occupancy. On review, the court reversed the order and held that when read in connection with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1822.50 and 1822.57, the ordinance could trigger the issuance of a warrant if an occupant had not consented to an inspection. The court found that the statute by its terms applied to routine inspections to protect a valid public purpose in the enforcement of appellant's zoning, health, and housing ordinances that involved a minimal invasion of privacy. Therefore, the ordinance did not violate the Fourth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, holding that appellant city's ordinance did not violate the Fourth Amendment's prohibition against warrantless searches. The ordinance provided for routine home inspections based on reasonable standards such as changes in ownership, occupancy, or a change in tenants. It provided an on-going check on observance of appellant's zoning, health and housing ordinances in a manner involving a minimal invasion of privacy.
